The Harm of Mixing Logic with Religious Sciences
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes mixing Greek logic with prophetic sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of using Greek logical definitions in Islamic sciences and identify his grounds for rejecting them.

Core Idea
Greek definitions corrupt the mind; prophetic knowledge alone suffices.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a sharp critique against the practice of mixing Greek logical definitions with the prophetic sciences. He argues that such definitions are not only useless but actively harmful, leading to wasted time, mental fatigue, and even hypocrisy. In this lesson, we will dissect his argument and understand why he considers the logicians' definitions inferior to the simple, revealed knowledge brought by the prophets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Locate where Ibn Taymiyya explicitly attacks the logicians' definitions (al-hudud). He accuses them of being verbose, pretentious, and ultimately uninformative.
The passage begins with 'خلط مقالات أهل المنطق بالعلوم النبوية'.
2
Extract the epistemological claim
Ibn Taymiyya cites al-Razi's view that conceptualizations (tasawwurat) cannot be acquired through definitions. This undermines the very purpose of logical definitions.
The relevant phrase: 'أن التصورات لا تكون مكتسبة'.
3
Analyse the consequences of mixing
Ibn Taymiyya lists harms: waste of time, fatigue of minds, false claims of verification, and corruption of religion. Note the strong language: 'كثرة الهذيان' (excessive nonsense) and 'أصل النفاق' (root of hypocrisy).
He also connects this to the early prohibitions of kalam.
4
Contrast with the prophetic method
Ibn Taymiyya implies that the simple, clear knowledge brought by the prophets is sufficient and superior. The logicians' definitions only produce more words, not real knowledge.
He states: 'لا تفيد الإنسان علماً لم يكن عنده، وإنما تفيده كثرة كلام'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - بعض فوائد المنطق اليوناني

خلط مقالات أهل المنطق بالعلوم النبوية وإفساد ذلك للعقول والأديان

وقد تفطّن أبو عبد الله الرازي بن الخطيب لما عليه أئمة الكلام، وقرّر في محصله وغيره أن التصورات لا تكون مكتسبة، وهذا هو حقيقة قول القائلين: «إن الحد لا يفيد تصوير المحدود». لكنه لم يهتد هو وأمثاله إلى ما سبق إليه أئمة الكلام في هذا المقام. وهذا مقام شريف ينبغي أن يعرف فإنه بسبب إهماله دخل الفساد في العقول والأديان على كثير من الناس، إذ خلطوا ما ذكره أهل المنطق في «الحدود» بالعلوم النبوية التي جاءت بها الرسل - التي عند المسلمين واليهود والنصارى - بل وسائر العلوم، كالطب، والنحو، وغير ذلك. وصاروا يعظمون أمر «الحدود»، ويدعون أنهم هم المحققون لذلك، وأن ما يذكره غيرهم من الحدود إنما هي لفظية لا تفيد تعريف الماهية والحقيقة بخلاف حدودهم، ويسلكون الطرق الصعبة الطويلة والعبارات المتكلفة الهائلة. وليس لذلك فائدة إلا تضييع الزمان، وإتعاب الأذهان وكثرة الهذيان ودعوى التحقيق بالكذب والبهتان، وشغل النفوس بما لا ينفعها - بل قد يضلّها - عما لا بد لها منه، وإثبات الجهل الذي هو أصل النفاق في القلوب، وإن ادعوا أنه أصل المعرفة والتحقيق. وهذا من توابع الكلام الذي كان السلف ينهون عنه، وإن كان الذي نهوا عنه خيراً من هذا إذ هو كلام في أدلة وأحكام ولم يكن قدماء المتكلمين يرضون أن يخوضوا في «الحدود» على طريقة المنطقيين، كما دخل في ذلك متأخروهم الذين ظنوا ذلك من التحقيق، وإنما هو زيغ عن سواء الطريق. ولهذا لما كانت هذه «الحدود» ونحوها لا تفيد الإنسان علماً لم يكن عنده، وإنما تفيده كثرة كلام، سموهم «أهل الكلام». وهذا - لعمري - في الحدود التي ليس فيها باطل. فأما حدود المنطقيين التي
The Second Station: Some Benefits of Greek Logic

Mixing the Statements of the Logicians with the Prophetic Sciences and the Corruption of Minds and Religions

And Abu Abdullah al-Razi ibn al-Khatib became aware of what the leading theologians were upon, and he established in his al-Muhassal and elsewhere that conceptualizations cannot be acquired, and this is the reality of the saying of those who say: 'The definition does not provide a depiction of the defined.' But he and his likes did not attain what the leading theologians had preceded to in this station. And this is a noble station that should be known, for due to its neglect, corruption entered the minds and religions of many people, as they mixed what the logicians mentioned about 'definitions' with the prophetic sciences that the messengers brought - which are with the Muslims, Jews, and Christians - indeed with all sciences, such as medicine, grammar, and others. And they began to venerate the matter of 'definitions', and claimed that they themselves are the verifiers of that, and that what others mention of definitions are merely verbal and do not provide knowledge of the essence and reality, unlike their definitions, and they adopt difficult and lengthy paths and pretentious ponderous expressions. And there is no benefit in that except wasting time, fatiguing minds, excessive nonsense, claiming verification by lying and slander, occupying souls with what does not benefit them - rather, it may mislead them - from what is necessary for them, and establishing ignorance which is the root of hypocrisy in hearts, even if they claim it is the root of knowledge and verification. And this is from the offshoots of the speech that the early predecessors used to forbid, although what they forbade was better than this, for it was speech about proofs and rulings, and the early theologians were not satisfied to delve into 'definitions' in the manner of the logicians, as the later ones did, who thought that was verification, but it is a deviation from the straight path. And for this reason, since these 'definitions' and the like do not give a person knowledge that he did not have, but rather give him excessive speech, they called them 'Ahl al-Kalam'. And this - by my life - applies to definitions that contain no falsehood. As for the definitions of the logicians which
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a critical and epistemological analysis of the impact of Greek logic on Islamic sciences. Ibn Taymiyya begins by acknowledging al-Razi's insight that conceptualizations (tasawwurat) are not acquired, which aligns with the rejection of definition's ability to depict the defined. However, al-Razi and others did not fully grasp the earlier theologians' position. Ibn Taymiyya then condemns the mixing of logicians' definitions with prophetic sciences—Judaism, Christianity, Islam—and other fields like medicine and grammar. He accuses logicians of arrogantly claiming that only their definitions capture essence, while others are merely verbal. Their methods are laborious and ultimately wasteful, producing no real knowledge but rather confusion and hypocrisy. He traces this to the speech that the salaf forbade, noting that even the early kalam was better as it dealt with proofs and rulings. The later theologians who adopted logical definitions deviated from the straight path. The passage ends with a remark that even sound definitions (without falsehood) are unproductive, let alone the logicians' definitions. This guards against the error of thinking that complex logical definitions are superior to the simple, revealed knowledge from the prophets.

Key Terms
التصورات
الحدود
أهل الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the main result of using the logicians' definitions in religious sciences?
A) They lead to greater precision in theological arguments.
B) They waste time, fatigue minds, and produce ignorance.
C) They help harmonize reason and revelation.
D) They are necessary for verifying prophetic knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique warns that adopting Greek logical methods in Islamic sciences does not yield true knowledge but rather leads to confusion, waste, and deviation from the simple prophetic path.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a passage from a classical usul al-fiqh text (e.g., al-Mustasfa by al-Ghazali) that uses a logical definition. Evaluate whether Ibn Taymiyya's critique would apply to that definition.
?
Discuss in a short paragraph: How does Ibn Taymiyya's critique affect the way we approach definitions in tafsir? Provide an example of a definition of a Qur'anic term (e.g., 'salah') that is simple versus one that is logically complex.

Why Isolated Words Cannot Convey Faith
Ibn Taymiyya attacks those who think uttering the isolated name is better than the full testimony of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the isolated name practice and explain why a complete proposition is necessary for faith and guidance.

Core Idea
Isolated words cannot convey faith; only complete propositions produce knowledge and guidance.

Ibn Taymiyya confronts a practice that elevates reciting the name 'Allah' alone above the full testimony of faith. He argues that such an isolated utterance fails to produce genuine faith or guidance. In this lesson, we examine the logical and linguistic basis of his critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target practice
Locate where Ibn Taymiyya describes the group who prefer reciting the isolated name (al-ism al-mufrad) or the pronoun 'Huwa' over the complete shahada.
This is in the first few lines of the passage.
2
Extract the grounds of critique
Ibn Taymiyya argues that such utterances 'do not benefit faith nor guidance.' Note that he calls them empty expressions that Satan enticed.
3
Analyze the Quranic misinterpretation
He references the verse 'Qul Allah' and explains that it is an answer to a question, not a command to recite the name alone. Identify the full context from Surah al-An'am.
4
Recognize the logical flaw
Understand that an isolated name without a predicate cannot form a meaningful proposition; it cannot assert or deny anything about reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلي مما طلعت عليه الشمس (۱). وقال: من كان آخر كلامه «لا إله إلا الله» دخل الجنة» (٢) . وأمثال ذلك . فظن طائفةٌ من الناس أن ذكر الاسم المفرد مشروع ، بل ظنه بعضُهم أفضل في حق من قول «لا إله إلا الله ونحوها. وظن بعضهم أن ذكر «الاسم المضمر» ـ وهو «هو» ـ هو أفضل من ذكر الاسم المظهر». الخاصة وأخرجهم الشيطان إلى أن يقولوا لفظاً لا يفيد إيماناً ولا هدى، بل دخلوا بذلك في مذهب أهل الزندقة والإلحاد - أهل وَحْدَة الوجود» ـ الذين يجعلون وجودَ المخلوقات وجود الخالق. ويقول أحدهم : ليس إلا (الله)» و«(الله) فقط»، ونحو ذلك . وربما احتج بعضهم عليه بقوله تعالى: ﴿ قُلِ اللهُ ثُمَّ ذَرْهُمْ فِي خَوْضِهِمْ يَلْعَبُونَ ) وظَنُّوا أنه مأمور بأن يقول الاسم مفرداً. وإنما هو جواب الاستفهام، حيث قال : ﴿ وَمَا قَدَرُوا اللَّهَ حَقَّ قَدْرِهِ: إِذْ قَالُوا مَا أَنزَلَ اللَّهُ عَلَى بَشَرٍ مِّن شَيْرٍ ؛ قال الله تعالى: ﴿قُلْ مَنْ أَنزَلَ الْكِتَبَ الَّذِي جَاءَ بِهِ. مُوسَى نُورًا وَهُدًى لِلنَّاسِ تَجْعَلُونَهُ قَرَاطِيسَ تُبْدُونَهَا وَتُخْفُونَ كَثِيرًا ... قل الله ﴾ [سورة الأنعام: ٩١] أي ، الله أنزَلَ الكتاب الذي جاء به موسى عود إلى أصل الموضوع : . (۳)
From which the sun has risen. And he said: 'Whoever's last words are "La ilaha illa Allah" will enter Paradise.' And similar things. A group of people thought that mentioning the isolated name is legislated; rather, some thought it is better in the right than saying 'La ilaha illa Allah' and the like. And some thought that mentioning the implicit pronoun — which is 'Huwa' — is better than mentioning the explicit name. Satan brought them out to utter an expression that does not benefit faith nor guidance; rather, by that they entered into the doctrine of the people of zandaqa and ilhad — the people of 'Wahdat al-Wujud' — who make the existence of creatures the existence of the Creator. And one of them says: 'There is nothing but Allah' and 'Allah only', and the like. And perhaps some of them argue by His saying: 'Say: Allah, then leave them to their idle talk.' They thought that it is commanded to say the name singly. But it is only an answer to the question, when He said: 'And they did not measure Allah with His true measure when they said: Allah has not sent down anything to any human. Say: Who sent down the Book that Moses brought as light and guidance for the people? You make it into sheets... Say: Allah.' i.e., Allah sent down the Book that Moses brought. Return to the original subject.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a critical digression by Ibn Taymiyya against a group of Muslims who elevated the practice of uttering the isolated name (ism mufrad) — such as simply saying 'Allah' or 'Huwa' — above the complete testimony of faith. He traces this practice to a misunderstanding of hadiths and Quranic verses, showing that the Quranic command 'Qul Allah' in Surah al-An'am is a response to a question, not a command to recite the name alone. He warns that such distortion leads to the heretical doctrine of Wahdat al-Wujud (unity of existence), which blurs the distinction between Creator and creation. This illustrates his broader argument that isolated words, without a complete proposition, cannot convey the intended meaning or produce genuine knowledge or guidance.

Key Terms
الاسم المفرد
وحدة الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the correct interpretation of the Quranic command 'Qul Allah' in Surah al-An'am?
A) It commands the isolated utterance of the name 'Allah' as a form of dhikr.
B) It is a response to a question about who sent down the Book to Moses.
C) It permits the use of 'Allah' alone as a shortcut to faith.
D) It is abrogated by later verses.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya insists that isolated words without a complete proposition cannot convey the intended meaning of faith; the Quranic command 'Qul Allah' is an answer, not a command to recite the name alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the practice of some Sufi circles of repeating 'Allah' or 'Huwa' in isolation. Using Ibn Taymiyya's criteria from this passage, explain why this is insufficient for faith compared to saying 'La ilaha illa Allah'.
?
Find a Quranic verse that is sometimes quoted out of context to support a practice. Using Ibn Taymiyya's method, demonstrate how reading the full context corrects the misinterpretation.

From Isolated Utterance to Wahdat al-Wujud
Ibn Taymiyya attacks those who think uttering the isolated name is better than the full testimony of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the connection Ibn Taymiyya draws between the isolated name practice and the heretical doctrine of Wahdat al-Wujud.

Core Idea
Exalting the isolated name above the shahada leads to blurring the distinction between Creator and creation.

Ibn Taymiyya warns that the practice of uttering the isolated name does not merely depart from sunna; it opens the door to the heresy of Wahdat al-Wujud. This lesson explores how a seemingly harmless innovation can lead to a fundamental corruption of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim that isolated name is better
Find where Ibn Taymiyya says some people thought the isolated name or pronoun is 'better' than the complete shahada.
2
See Ibn Taymiyya's counter
He says such utterances 'do not benefit faith nor guidance' and that Satan enticed them.
3
Trace the connection to Wahdat al-Wujud
Note that Ibn Taymiyya states this practice leads to entering the doctrine of Wahdat al-Wujud, which equates the existence of creatures with the Creator.
4
Reflect on the corrective
Ibn Taymiyya's alternative is the full shahada and other complete propositions that preserve the distinction between Allah and creation.

Ibn Abbas's Four Aspects of Tafsir and the Role of Ijtihad
Ibn Taymiyya presents Ibn Abbas's classification of tafsir and argues that legal definitions require ijtihad beyond language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any instance of tafsir into one of Ibn Abbas's four categories and explain why legal terms require ijtihad beyond language.

Core Idea
Tafsir is not just language; scholars must reason to define shar'i terms.

Ibn Taymiyya presents Ibn Abbas's famous division of tafsir to show that legal definitions cannot be derived from language alone. This lesson unpacks the four categories and highlights the third category—tafsir known by scholars—which requires ijtihad for shar'i names.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
List the four types of tafsir as given by Ibn Abbas: known from Arab speech, inexcusable ignorance, known by scholars, known only by Allah.
2
Focus on the third category
This category includes shar'i names (e.g., salat, zakat, hajj) whose exact scope requires ijtihad. Ibn Taymiyya gives examples like including sand in 'sa'id' for tayammum.
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that this type of ijtihad is agreed upon among Muslims.
3
Distinguish from language alone
Language gives general meaning, but ijtihad determines application to new cases. The scholars' tafsir involves determining the entities and types that fall under a shar'i name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أوجه التفسير الأربعة
الخيل، ورمي التُشَاب في ذلك، ودخول «الرَّمْل ونحوه في الصعيد/ المذكور في قوله تعالى : ( فَتَيَمَّمُوا صَعِيدًا طَيِّبًا [سورة المائدة : ٦] ، ودخولِ مَنْ خرج منه مني ( يشبه كأنه : فابتل في قوله: ﴿ وَإِن كُنتُمْ جُنُبًا فَأَطَهَرُوا ) ، ودخول «الساعد» في قوله: فَامْسَحُوا بِوُجُوهِكُمْ وَأَيْدِيكُم مِّنه ، ودخول البياض الذي بين العذار والأذن في قوله: ﴿ فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ ، ودخول الماء المتغير بالطاهرات» أو «ما وقعت به نجاسة ولم تغيّره في قوله: ﴿ فَلَمْ تَجِدُوا مَا ﴾ [سورة المائدة : ٦] ودخول المائع الذي لم يغيره ما مات فيه في الطيبات أو الخبائث، ودخول سارق الأموال الرطبة في قوله : وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ ﴾ [سورة المائدة : ۳۸] ودخولِ «النبَّاش» في ذلك، ودخول «الحلفة بما يلزم الله في في مسمى الأيمان، ودخولِ بنات البنات والجدات في قوله: ﴿ حُرِّمَتْ عَلَيْكُمْ أُمَّهَتُكُمْ وَبَنَاتُكُمْ ﴾ [سورة النساء : ٢٣] .
ومثل هذا الاجتهاد)) متفق عليه بين المسلمين - ممن يثبت القياس» ومَنْ ينفيه . فإن بعض الجهال يظن أن مَنْ نَفَى «القياس» يكفيه في معرفة مراد الشارع مجرد العلم بـ«اللغة». وهذا غلط عظيم جداً.
ولهذا قال ابن عباس : التفسير» على أربعة أوجه : تفسير تعرفه العرب من كلامها ، وتفسير لا يعذر أحد،بجهالته وتفسير يعلمه العلماء، وتفسير لا يعلمه إلا الله». والتفسير الذي يعلمه العلماء يتضمَّن الأنواع التي لا تعلم بمجرد «اللغة»، كـ «الأسماء الشرعية»، ويتضمن أعيان المسميات وأنواعها التي يفتقر دخولها في المسمى إلى
«اجتهاد العلماء .
The four aspects of tafsir: [examples of ijtihad in legal definitions such as] horses and arrow-shooting in that, and the inclusion of sand and similar in the 'clean earth' mentioned in His statement: 'so seek clean earth' [5:6], and the inclusion of one from whom maniy exits... and the like. This type of ijtihad is agreed upon among Muslims, both those who affirm qiyas and those who negate it. For some ignorant people think that one who negates qiyas is sufficient in knowing the Lawgiver's intent by mere knowledge of language. This is a very great error. Hence Ibn Abbas said: 'Tafsir is of four types: tafsir known by the Arabs from their speech, tafsir for which ignorance is inexcusable, tafsir known by the scholars, and tafsir known only by Allah.' The tafsir known by the scholars includes the types that cannot be known by language alone, such as the 'shar'i names', and includes the entities of the named things and their types, the inclusion of which in the name requires ijtihad of the scholars.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage begins by listing numerous examples from fiqh where the scope of a legal term is determined by ijtihad, such as the inclusion of horses in the prohibition of gambling, the inclusion of sand in 'sa'id' for tayammum, the inclusion of the forearm in the verse on wudu, etc. The author then states that this type of ijtihad is agreed upon among Muslims, whether they accept qiyas or not. He refutes the ignorant who think that negating qiyas means knowing the Lawgiver's intent by language alone. Then he quotes Ibn Abbas's famous division of tafsir into four categories: that known from Arabic speech, that which is inexcusable to be ignorant of, that known only by scholars, and that known only by Allah. The scholars' tafsir includes the types that cannot be known by language alone, such as the 'shar'i names' (e.g., salat, zakat, hajj) and the specific entities and categories that require ijtihad to determine whether they fall under the name.

Key Terms
التفسير
الأسماء الشرعية
الاجتهاد

Checkpoint

A) Tafsir known by the Arabs from their speech
B) Tafsir for which ignorance is inexcusable
C) Tafsir known by the scholars
D) Tafsir known only by Allah

Key Takeaway
Tafsir includes a category that requires scholarly ijtihad, especially for shar'i names whose scope is not determined by language alone.

The Error of Negating Qiyas and Sufficing with Language
Ibn Taymiyya refutes those who think negating qiyas makes language sufficient for understanding legal intent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of equating the negation of qiyas with the sufficiency of language alone, and explain why ijtihad remains necessary for legal definitions.

Core Idea
Negating qiyas does not make language sufficient; ijtihad is still needed for shar'i names.

Ibn Taymiyya refutes a common misconception: that those who reject qiyas (analogical reasoning) can know the Lawgiver's intent purely from language. He labels this a 'very great error.' This lesson analyzes the epistemological mistake behind this view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the erroneous claim
Some ignorant people think that if one negates qiyas, mere knowledge of language is enough to understand legal texts.
Ibn Taymiyya states this explicitly: 'some ignorant people think that one who negates qiyas is sufficient in knowing the Lawgiver's intent by mere knowledge of language.'
2
Why it is an error
Shar'i names (like salat) have meanings defined by the Lawgiver that go beyond linguistic usage. Determining their scope requires ijtihad to classify new instances.
Example: Is 'saa'id' in tayammum limited to earth or includes sand?
3
Agreement among Muslims
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that this type of ijtihad is agreed upon by both proponents and opponents of qiyas.
Thus the error is not just about qiyas but about the nature of legal terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why Definitions Cannot Teach the Unknown
Critique of definitions and proposal of tamthil/tashbih as cognitive method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya’s critique of logical definitions (hadd) and explain why tamthil/tashbih is proposed as the alternative method for acquiring new knowledge.

Core Idea
Definitions presuppose what they claim to teach; only analogy from the known opens the unknown.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a cornerstone of Aristotelian logic: that definitions (hadd) composed of genus and differentia can convey the essence of an unknown thing. He argues that any distinguishing property (fasl, khassa) of an object cannot be understood without prior knowledge of that object itself, leading to circularity. Instead, he contends that the intellect naturally learns through analogy (tamthil) and comparison (tashbih), identifying a common factor (qadr mushtarak) between the known and unknown. This critique has profound implications for how we acquire knowledge in Islamic sciences, from understanding the attributes of Allah to the realities of the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim about definitions
Find in the passage where Ibn Taymiyya describes the standard view that definition (hadd) via genus and differentia can make someone know the essence of a thing they did not previously conceive.
The logicians claim that answering 'What is it?' with a proper definition imparts new knowledge.
2
Understand the two cognitive states: knower vs. ignorant
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between someone who already has a conception of the thing (al-mahdud) and someone who does not. For the knower, a definition is merely a reminder like a name. For the ignorant, a definition is impossible.
Focus on the sentence: 'As for someone who does not have a conception of it, it is impossible to mention for him a single attribute specific to the definite thing.'
3
Analyze the circularity (dawr) argument
Ibn Taymiyya uses the example of defining 'horse' as 'neighing animal'. If someone does not know the horse, they cannot understand 'neighing'. Trying to define 'neighing' as 'sound of a horse' requires knowing the horse first, creating a circle.
This shows that definitions presuppose the very thing they are supposed to explain.
4
Examine the alternative: tamthil and tashbih
Ibn Taymiyya proposes that the intellect conceives the unknown through analogy (tamthil) and comparison (tashbih) by isolating a common factor (qadr mushtarak) between the unknown and something known.
He gives the example of 'intoxicating drink' – the common factor of intoxication links an unknown drink to known wine.
5
Apply to Islamic sciences: knowledge of the unseen
Ibn Taymiyya connects this to how people understand reports of the unseen (e.g., angels, Paradise, Hell) through analogies from worldly experience. He also links it to the Quranic distinction between muhkam (clear) and mutashabih (ambiguous) verses.
The mutashabih (promises, threats) is understood by analogy; the muhkam (commands, prohibitions) is acted upon directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني - الوجه الرابع : العقل يتصور بـ «التمثيل» و«التشبيه»
والمقصود أنه مَنْ تصوّر المحدود بنفسه فلا بد أن يتصور ما يختصه ويميّزه عن غيره. وهذا لا يحتاج في تصوره إلى حد ولكن يترجم له الاسم الدال عليه ويميّز له المسمى عن غيره لكن الحد يكون منبهاً له على الحقيقة كما ينبهه الاسم إذا كان عارفاً بمسماه .
وأما من لم يكن متصوّراً له فلا يمكن أن يُذكر له صفة واحدة تختص بالمحدود، فلا يمكن تعريفه إياه لا بـ «فصل» ولا بـ «خاصة» سواء ذكر «الجنس» و «العرض العام» أو لم يذكر ، لأن الصفة التي تختص المحدود لا تتصور بدون تصور المحدود، إذ لا وجود لها بدونه. والعقل إنما يجرد «الكليات» إذا تصوَّرَ بعض «جزئياتها». فمن لم يتصور الشيء الموجود كيف يتصور جنسه ونوعه؟
ولكن يتصور بـ «التمثيل» و «التشبيه». ويتصور القدر المشترك بين تلك الصفة الخاصة وبين نظيرها من الصفات و القدر المشترك ليس هو «فصلا» ولا «خاصة» ولكن قد ينتظم من قدر مشترك و قدر مشترك ما يخص المحدود، كما في قولك شراب مسکر.
وعلى هذا، فإذا قيل في «الفَرَس»: إنه حيوان صاهل»، وفي «الحمار»: إنه حيوان ناهق»، وفي «البعير»: إنه حيوان راغ»، وفي «الثور»: إنه حيوان خائر»، وفي «الشاة»: إنها حيوان ثاغ»، وفي «الظبي»: إنه حيوان باغم وأمثال ذلك، فهذه بالخواص الأصوات مختصة بهذه الأنواع، لكن لا تفيد تعريف هذه الحيوانات لمن لم يكن عارفاً بها. فمن لم يعرف الفرس لا يعرف الصهيل»، ومن لم يعرف «الحمار» لا يعرف «النهيق»، أعني لا يعرف معناه وإن سمع اللفظ، فإذا أريد تعريف «النهيق» قيل له : هو صوت الحمار»، فيلزم الدَّوْرَ ، إذ يكون قد عرف الحمار» بـ «النهيق»، و «النهيق» بـ «الحمار».
وهكذا سائر الخواص المميّزة للمحدود - لا يعرف بها المحدود لمن لم يكن عرفه . فتبين أن تعريف الشيء إنما هو بتعريف عينه، أو بذكر ما يشبهه. فمن عرف عين الشيء لا يفتقر في معرفته إلى حد ومن لم يعرفه فإنما يعرف به إذا عرف ما يشبهه، ولو من بعض الوجوه . فيؤلفُ له من الصفات المشتبهة المشتركة بينه وبين غيره ما يخص المعرّف .
-
ومن تدبر هذا وجد حقيقته؛ وعلم معرفة الخلق بما أُخبروا به من الغيب ـ من الملائكة، واليوم الآخر، وما في الجنة والنار من أنواع النعيم والعذاب؛ بل عَرَف أيضاً ما يدخل من ذلك في معرفتهم بالله تعالى وصفاته ؛ ولم قال كثير من السلف : «المتشابه» هو الوعد والوعيد، و «المحكم» هو الأمر والنهي ؛ ولِمَ قيل : القرآن يُعمل بـ «محكمه» ويؤمَنُ بـ «متشابهه ؛ وعَلم أن تأويل «المتشابه» ـ الذي هو العلم بكيفية ما أخبرنا به ـ لا يعلمه إلا الله وإن كان العلم بتأويله - الذي هو تفسيره ومعناه المراد به ـ يعلمه الراسخون في العلم ، كما بسطناه في غير هذا الموضع .
هذا كله إذا كان السائل القائل ما هو ؟ » غير عالم بـ «المسمى».
وأما إن كان عالماً بـ «المسمى ودلالة الاسم عليه فلا يحتاج إلى التمييز بينه وبين غيره، ولا إلى تعريفه دلالة الاسم عليه، فيكون مطلوبه قدراً زائداً على التمييز بينه وبين غيره وهذا هو الذي يجعلونه مطلوباً للسائل عن المحدود، وجوابه هو عندهم المقولُ في جواب ما هو ؟ » .
·
ومعلوم أن مطلوب هذا قد يكون ذِكْرَ خصائص له باطنة لم يطلع عليها، وقد يكون مطلوبه بيان علته الفاعلية أو الغائية، وقد يكون مطلوبه معرفة ما يتركب منه شيء آخر، وقد يكون مطلوبه معرفة حقيقته التي لا يعلمها المسئول، أو علمها ولا عبارة تدل السائل عليها كالسائل عن حقيقة النفس»، وأمثال ذلك .
و جواب مثل هذا لا يجب أن يحصل بذكر صفة مشتركة مع الصفات المختصة
The second station - the fourth aspect: the intellect conceives through 'tamthil' (analogy) and 'tashbih' (comparison). The intended meaning is that whoever conceives the definite thing by itself must conceive what is specific to it and distinguishes it from others. This does not need a definition in its conception, but the name indicating it translates it and distinguishes the named for him from others. However, the definition serves as a reminder of the reality just as the name reminds him if he is knowledgeable of its meaning. As for someone who does not have a conception of it, it is impossible to mention for him a single attribute specific to the definite thing; thus it is impossible to define it for him by either a 'differentia' (fasl) or a 'property' (khassa), whether one mentions the 'genus' (jins) and the 'common accident' (arad 'amm) or not, because the attribute specific to the definite thing cannot be conceived without the conception of the definite thing, since it has no existence without it. The intellect only abstracts 'universals' when it has conceived some of their 'particulars'. So whoever does not conceive the existing thing, how can he conceive its genus and species? Rather, he conceives through 'tamthil' and 'tashbih'. He conceives the common factor (qadr mushtarak) between that specific attribute and its counterpart among attributes. The common factor is not a 'differentia' nor a 'property', but from a common factor and another common factor may be composed what is specific to the definite thing, as in your saying 'an intoxicating drink'. And accordingly, if it is said of 'the horse': it is a neighing animal; of 'the donkey': it is a braying animal; of 'the camel': it is a grunting animal; of 'the ox': it is a lowing animal; of 'the sheep': it is a bleating animal; of 'the gazelle': it is a belling animal; and the like, these are by the properties of sounds specific to these species, but they do not benefit the definition of these animals for someone who is not familiar with them. For whoever does not know the horse does not know 'neighing'; whoever does not know the donkey does not know 'braying'—I mean, he does not know its meaning even if he hears the word. So if one wants to define 'neighing', it is said to him: it is the sound of the horse, then circularity (dawr) follows, because one would have defined the horse by neighing and neighing by the horse. And likewise all the other distinguishing properties of the definite thing—the definite thing cannot be known by them for someone who does not already know it. Thus it becomes clear that the definition of a thing is only by defining its essence ('ayn), or by mentioning what resembles it. So whoever knows the essence of a thing does not need a definition in his knowledge; and whoever does not know it can only know it if he knows what resembles it, even if in some respects. Thus for him is composed from the resembling attributes shared between it and other things what is specific to the defined. And whoever ponders this will find its reality; and he will know the creation's knowledge of what they were informed of from the unseen—of angels, the Last Day, and what is in Paradise and the Fire of types of bliss and punishment; rather, he also knows what enters into that from their knowledge of Allah Most High and His attributes; and why many of the Salaf said: 'The ambiguous (mutashabih) is the promise and threat, and the clear (muhkam) is the command and prohibition'; and why it was said: 'The Quran is acted upon by its clear and believed in its ambiguous'; and he knows that the interpretation of the ambiguous—which is the knowledge of the modality of what we were informed of—is not known except by Allah, even if the knowledge of its interpretation—which is its explanation and intended meaning—is known by those firmly rooted in knowledge, as we have elaborated elsewhere. All this is when the questioner who says 'What is it?' is not knowledgeable of the 'named' (musamma). But if he is knowledgeable of the 'named' and the indication of the name to it, then he does not need the distinction between it and others, nor the definition of the name's indication to it. Thus his request is an additional element beyond the distinction between it and others, and this is what they make the request of the questioner about the definite thing, and its answer is according to them what is said in answer to 'What is it?'. And it is known that the request of this person may be a mention of inner properties he has not been informed of; or his request may be an explanation of its efficient or final cause; or his request may be knowledge of what something else is composed of; or his request may be knowledge of its reality which the respondent does not know, or knows it but there is no expression that indicates it to the questioner, like the questioner about the reality of the soul, and the like. And the answer to such should not be obtained by mentioning a common attribute with the specific attributes.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the standard logical theory of definition (hadd). He begins by distinguishing two types of people: one who already has a conception of the thing being defined (al-mahdud) and one who does not. For the first, a definition is merely a reminder, like a name. For the second, a definition is impossible because any distinguishing property (like fasl or khassa) presupposes knowledge of the thing itself. For example, if you define 'horse' as 'neighing animal', the term 'neighing' is meaningless without knowing the horse. Attempting to define 'neighing' as 'sound of horse' leads to circularity (dawr). Ibn Taymiyya then proposes an alternative: the intellect conceives the unknown through analogy (tamthil) and comparison (tashbih), by identifying a common factor (qadr mushtarak) between the unknown and something known. He illustrates with animal definitions based on characteristic sounds and shows they fail for someone unfamiliar with the animal. He extends this principle to knowledge of the unseen (ghayb), explaining that people come to understand eschatological realities through analogies from worldly experience. This also explains why the Quran contains clear (muhkam) and ambiguous (mutashabih) verses: the ambiguous (like promises and threats) are understood by analogy to known things, while the clear (commands and prohibitions) are directly acted upon. Finally, he considers a different scenario: when the questioner already knows the referent of the name but seeks further information (e.g., hidden properties, causes, composition, or the very essence). In such cases, the answer should not be restricted to a definition by genus and differentia; rather, it should address the specific additional knowledge sought.

تشبيه

حد
قدر مشترك

D) Because the term 'horse' is more specific than 'neighing animal'.

Key Takeaway
Logical definitions cannot produce first-time knowledge; the intellect must rely on analogy (tamthil) and comparison (tashbih) from the known to the unknown.

The Conventional Terminology of Quiddity and Existence and Their Identity in the External World
Ibn Taymiyya explains the conventional terms 'mahiya' and 'wujud' and argues for the identity of external existence with external quiddity, as agreed by Ahl al-Sunna.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conventional distinction between mahiya (quiddity) and wujud (existence) as per Ibn Taymiyya and state why external existence is identical to external quiddity according to Ahl al-Sunna.

Core Idea
The distinction between quiddity and existence is merely conventional; in external reality, they are one and the same.

Ibn Taymiyya addresses a common confusion: the terms 'mahiya' and 'wujud' are often used to distinguish mental conception from external reality. But this verbal convention does not mean that existence is an extra attribute added to essence in the external world. In this passage, he clarifies the etymology of 'mahiya' and establishes the Ahl al-Sunna position that external existence is identical to external quiddity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the etymology of 'mahiya'
The term 'mahiya' is derived from the interrogative phrase 'ma huwa?' (what is it?), similar to terms like 'kayfiyya' (quality) and 'ayniyya' (whereness). The answer to 'what is it?' provides a mental depiction of the thing, which is mental subsistence (al-thubut al-dhihni).
2
Distinguish the conventional usage
Conventionally, 'mahiya' is predominantly used for what exists in the mind, while 'wujud' is used for what exists in the external world. Ibn Taymiyya stresses that this is merely a verbal convention, not a real metaphysical distinction.
The convention arose because the question 'what is it?' seeks a mental picture.
3
Recognize the qualified usage
When qualified, 'mental existence' (al-wujud al-dhihni) is exactly the same as the quiddity in the mind. Similarly, 'external quiddity' (mahiyat al-shay' fi al-kharij) is identical to its external existence. This shows that the terms can be used interchangeably when context is specified.
4
Identify the Ahl al-Sunna position
The leading thinkers of Ahl al-Sunna, such as Ibn Kullab, al-Ash'ari, and Ibn Karram, agree that the external existence of a thing is identical to its external quiddity. They also agree that the non-existent (ma'dum) has no essence in the external world prior to its existence.
This position opposes the view that existence is an accident added to essence.
5
Avoid the mistaken implication
If one restricts 'quiddity' to the mental concept and 'existence' to the external, they are distinct. However, this does not entail that in the external world existence is an additional attribute. The conventional distinction does not imply a real separation in external reality.
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the philosophers' doubt arises from conflating conventional terminology with metaphysical reality.

المقام الثاني - الوجه السادس : اصطلاحات «الماهية» و«الوجود»

وإنما نشأت الشبهة من جهة أنه غلب على أن ما يوجد في الذهن يسمى مأخذ لفظ «ماهية»، وما يوجد في الخارج يسمى «وجوداً». لأن «الماهية» مأخوذة من قولهم «ما هو؟» كسائر الأسماء المأخوذة من الجُمَل الاستفهامية، كما يقولون «الكيفية» و «الأينية». ويقال «ماهية» و «مايية»، وهي أسماء مولدة. وهي المَقُول في جواب «ما هو؟» بما يُصوِّر الشيء في نفس السائل. فلما كانت الماهية منسوبة إلى الاستفهام بـ «ما هو؟»، والمستفهم إنما يطلب تصوير الشيء في نفسه، كان الجواب عنها هو المقول في جواب «ما هو؟» بما يصور الشيء في نفس السائل، وهو الثبوتُ الذهني، سواء كان ذلك المقول موجوداً في الخارج أو لم يكن. فصار بحكم الاصطلاح أكثر ما يُطلَق «الماهية» على ما في الذهن، ويُطلق «الوجود» على ما في الخارج. فهذا أمر لفظي اصطلاحي.

وإذا قُيِّدَ، وقيل: «الوجود الذهني» كان هو الماهية التي في الذهن، وإذا قيل: «ماهية الشيء في الخارج» كان هو عين وجوده الذي في الخارج.

فوجود الشيء في الخارج عين ماهيته في الخارج، كما اتفق على ذلك أئمة النظار المنتسبين إلى أهل السنة والجماعة وسائر أهل الإثبات من المتكلمة الصفاتية وغيرهم كأبي محمد بن كلاب وأبي الحسن الأشعري وأبي عبد الله بن كرام وأتباعهم - دع أئمة أهل السنة والجماعة من السلف والأئمة الكبار.

واتفقوا على أن «المعدوم» ليس له في الخارج ذات قبل وجوده. وأما في الذهن، فنفس ماهيته التي في الذهن هي أيضاً وجوده الذي في الذهن. وإذا أُرِيدَ بـ «الماهية» ما في الذهن وبـ «الوجود» ما في الخارج كانت هذه الماهية غير الوجود. لكن ذلك لا يقتضي أن يكون وجود الماهيات التي في الخارج زائداً عليها في الخارج، وأن يكون للماهيات ثبوت في الخارج غير وجودها في الخارج.
The second station - the sixth aspect: Terminologies of 'quiddity' and 'existence'. The doubt only arose from the fact that it became prevalent that what exists in the mind is called the derivation of the term 'quiddity', and what exists in the external world is called 'existence'. Because 'quiddity' is derived from their saying 'what is it?', like other names derived from interrogative sentences, as they say 'quality' and 'whereness'. And it is said 'mahiya' and 'mayiya', and they are derived names. And it is what is said in answer to 'what is it?' by that which depicts the thing in the soul of the questioner. So when quiddity is attributed to the interrogation by 'what is it?', and the questioner only seeks the depiction of the thing in himself, the answer to it is what is said in answer to 'what is it?' by that which depicts the thing in the soul of the questioner, and it is mental subsistence, whether that which is said exists in the external world or not. Therefore, by convention, most often 'quiddity' is applied to what is in the mind, and 'existence' is applied to what is in the external world. This is a verbal conventional matter. And if it is qualified, and it is said 'mental existence', it is the quiddity that is in the mind, and if it is said 'quiddity of the thing in the external world', it is identical to its existence that is in the external world. So the existence of a thing in the external world is identical to its quiddity in the external world, as agreed upon by the leading thinkers affiliated with Ahl al-Sunna wa'l-Jama'a and all the affirmers among the theologians of the attributes and others, such as Abu Muhammad ibn Kullab, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, Abu Abdullah ibn Karram, and their followers - let alone the imams of Ahl al-Sunna wa'l-Jama'a from the predecessors and the great imams. And they agreed that 'the non-existent' has no essence in the external world before its existence. As for in the mind, the very quiddity that is in the mind is also its existence that is in the mind. And if by 'quiddity' is intended what is in the mind and by 'existence' what is in the external world, then this quiddity is other than existence. But that does not necessitate that the existence of the quiddities that are in the external world is additional to them in the external world, and that the quiddities have a subsistence in the external world other than their existence in the external world.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a terminological and philosophical clarification by Ibn Taymiyya regarding the terms 'quiddity' (mahiya) and 'existence' (wujud). He begins by explaining that the confusion arises from the conventional usage: what exists in the mind is called 'quiddity' and what exists externally is called 'existence'. He traces the etymology of 'mahiya' to the interrogative phrase 'ma huwa?' (what is it?), similar to other derived terms like 'kayfiyya' (quality) and 'ayniyya' (whereness). The answer to 'what is it?' provides a mental depiction of the thing, which is mental subsistence, regardless of external existence. Thus, conventionally, 'quiddity' often refers to the mental concept, while 'existence' refers to external reality. However, this is merely a verbal convention. When qualified, 'mental existence' is identical to the quiddity in the mind, and 'external quiddity' is identical to external existence. Ibn Taymiyya then asserts that, according to the leading scholars of Ahl al-Sunna and other affirmers (like Ibn Kullab, al-Ash'ari, Ibn Karram), the external existence of a thing is exactly its external quiddity. They also agree that the non-existent (ma'dum) has no external essence prior to its existence. In the mind, the quiddity is the same as mental existence. If one restricts 'quiddity' to the mental concept and 'existence' to the external, then they are distinct, but this does not imply that external existence is an extra added to the external quiddity, or that quiddities have a separate subsistence apart from their external existence. The passage thus guards against the misconception that existence is a separate attribute added to essence in the external world.

Key Takeaway

Critiquing Philosophers on Universals, Matter, Time, and Place
Ibn Taymiyya reviews and refutes the philosophers' claims about universals, matter, time, and place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the philosophers' claims about Platonic forms, prime matter, existential time, and void.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya exposes the philosophers' internal contradictions and their departure from rational and Islamic principles.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya systematically critiques the metaphysical doctrines of the philosophers (mutafalsifa), from Pythagoreans to Aristotelian. He shows how they fell into errors by positing external realities for mental constructs. Understanding this critique hones your ability to assess metaphysical claims against tawhid and sound reason.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups of philosophers
Notice that Ibn Taymiyya divides the philosophers into ancients (Pythagoras, Plato) and later ones (Aristotle, Ibn Sina). Each group made distinct errors.
This division helps trace the development of error.
2
Extract the errors of the ancients
List the three errors: (1) numbers and magnitudes as external entities; (2) Platonic forms as separate, eternal realities; (3) positing prime matter, existential time, and void as external substances.
These are all cases of reifying mental abstractions.
3
Note Aristotle's partial correction and new error
Aristotle recognized that forms, numbers, time, and void are mental estimates, but he then claimed that matter exists externally without form, which is contradictory.
Ibn Taymiyya highlights Aristotle's inconsistency.
4
Relate to Islamic theology
Contrast the philosophers' views with the Islamic position: only God is eternal; universals are mental; matter and time are created attributes of bodies.
This shows why the critique matters for tawhid.

أقوال المتفلسفة في الوجود والثبوت والمدة والمكان

ومما يوضح الكلام في الأصل الأول أن المتفلسفة، أتباع أرسطو، كابن سينا ودونه، لا يقولون أن كلَّ معدوم من الأشخاص وغيرها هو ثابت في الخارج. وإنما يقول ذلك مَنْ يقوله من المعتزلة والشيعة - وهم أحسن حالاً من المتفلسفة - فإن المتفلسفة على قولين:

أما قدماؤهم، كفيثاغورس وأتباعه وأفلاطون وأتباعه، فقد كانوا في ذلك على ضلال مبين؛ رَدّه عليهم أرسطو وأتباعه، كما ذكر ذلك ابن سينا في «الشفاء» وغيره. كان أصحاب فيثاغورس يظنون أن الأعداد والمقادير أمور موجودة في الخارج، غير المعدودات المقدرات. ثم أصحاب أفلاطون تفطنوا لفساد هذا، وظنوا أن الحقائق النوعية، كحقيقة الإنسان والفرس وأمثال ذلك ثابتةٌ في الخارج، غير الأعيان الموجودة في الخارج، وأنها أزلية لا تقبل الاستحالة. وهذه التي تسمى «المُثُل الأفلاطونية» و «المثل المعلقة».

ولم يقتصروا على ذلك، بل أثبتوا ذلك أيضاً في «المادة»، و«المدة»، و«المكان». فأثبتوا مادةً مجردة عن الصور ثابتة في الخارج، وهي «الهيولى الأولية» التي غَلَّطهم فيها جمهور العقلاء من إخوانهم وغير إخوانهم. وأثبتوا «مدة» وجودية خارجة عن الأجسام وصفاتها. وأثبتوا «خلاء» وجودياً خارجاً عن الأجسام وصفاتها.

وتفطن أرسطو وذووه أن هذه كلها أمورٌ مقدَّرة في الأذهان، لا ثابتة في الأعيان كالعدد المعدود. ثم زعم أرسطو وذووه أن «المادة» موجودة في الخارج غير الصور مع أنها لا توجد في الخارج إلا بالصور.
Sayings of the philosophers on existence, subsistence, time, and place. And among what clarifies the discussion in the first principle is that the philosophers, the followers of Aristotle, like Ibn Sina and those beneath him, do not say that every non-existent among individuals and others is established in the external world. Rather, that is said by whoever says it among the Mu'tazila and Shi'a - and they are in a better state than the philosophers - for the philosophers are of two opinions: As for their ancients, like Pythagoras and his followers and Plato and his followers, they were in that regard in clear error; Aristotle and his followers refuted them, as Ibn Sina mentioned in 'al-Shifa'' and elsewhere. The followers of Pythagoras thought that numbers and magnitudes are things existing in the external world, separate from the numbered and measured things. Then the followers of Plato realized the corruption of this and thought that the specific realities, like the reality of man and horse and the like, are established in the external world, separate from the individual existents in the external world, and that they are eternal and not subject to change. And these are called 'Platonic forms' and 'suspended forms'. And they did not limit themselves to that, but also affirmed that in 'matter', 'time', and 'place'. So they affirmed a matter abstracted from forms, established in the external world, which is 'prime matter', concerning which the majority of rational people among their brethren and others declared them mistaken. And they affirmed an existential 'time' external to bodies and their attributes. And they affirmed an existential 'void' external to bodies and their attributes. And Aristotle and his followers realized that all these are things estimated in the minds, not established in external entities like numbered numbers. Then Aristotle and his followers claimed that 'matter' exists in the external world separate from forms, even though it does not exist in the external world except with forms.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a critical historical review by Ibn Taymiyya of the views of the philosophers (mutafalsifa) on existence, subsistence, time, and place. He begins by clarifying that the philosophers, such as Aristotle and Ibn Sina, do not claim that every non-existent has a fixed reality in the external world—unlike the Mu'tazila and Shi'a who assert that non-existent things have some kind of external subsistence. The philosophers themselves are divided into two groups: the ancients (Pythagoras and Plato) and the later ones (Aristotle). Ibn Taymiyya then details the errors of the ancients: the Pythagoreans believed numbers and magnitudes exist externally, independent of the things that are numbered and measured. Plato's followers corrected this but fell into another error, believing that universal realities (like the essence of man or horse) exist externally, separate from individual existents, and are eternal and immutable—these are the Platonic forms or 'suspended forms'. Furthermore, these ancient philosophers also posited a separate matter (prime matter) devoid of form and existing externally, a distinct time (al-muddah) and a void (al-khala') both existing externally and independent of bodies. The majority of rational people have refuted these ideas, showing that they are merely mental estimates. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya points out that Aristotle and his followers recognized that these are mental constructs, not real external entities, yet Aristotle himself then claimed that matter exists externally separate from form, despite the fact that matter never exists in the external world except with form. This shows a contradiction in Aristotle's position.

المادة

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya exposes the philosophers' inconsistency in reifying mental constructs as external realities, providing a model for critiquing metaphysical claims from an Islamic perspective.

The Correct Distinction Between Mahiya and Wujud
Ibn Taymiyya states the correct distinction between mahiya and wujud and critiques the logicians' view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's correct distinction between mahiya and wujud and identify the arbitrariness of the logicians' essential/accidental distinction.

Core Idea
Mahiya and wujud are identical when referring to the same domain; the logicians' separate essences are arbitrary inventions.

Ibn Taymiyya tackles a foundational issue: the relationship between essence (mahiya) and existence (wujud). He provides a clear, intuitive distinction that guards against the metaphysical confusion introduced by the logicians. This passage is a powerful critique of the logicians' tendency to reify mental constructs as external realities. By the end, you will see why their essential/accidental distinctions are ultimately conventional and lack epistemological grounding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the correct distinction
Ibn Taymiyya defines mahiya as the mental representation of a thing, and wujud as the thing's external reality. This is the 'correct distinction' (al-farq al-sahih). Notice that he grounds it in the undeniable difference between what is in the mind and what exists outside.
2
Understand the identity when domains match
He explains that if both terms refer to the mental domain, mahiya is wujud; if both refer to the external, they are also the same. Only when one term refers to the mental and the other to the external are they different. This shows that mahiya and wujud are not separate entities but the same thing considered from different perspectives.
3
Recognize the arbitrariness of the logicians' claims
Ibn Taymiyya points out that the logicians' talk implies a mahiya only in the mind, which is arbitrary. For example, one can claim 'human' is 'rational animal' while another says 'laughing animal'. Similarly, they claim 'animality' is essential to human but 'number' is not to even/odd, yet another can reverse this. See how such distinctions are mere conventions, not grounded in external reality.
This is the core of his critique: the logicians' essential/accidental distinction is a human construct, not a discovery of objective reality.
4
Assess the consequence: futility of the enterprise
If the logicians claim their mahiya corresponds to the thing in itself, they are wrong (because it's just mental invention). If they admit it's a convention, it can be countered by another convention. Thus the whole project of defining essences in this way is futile and has no benefit. This undermines the logicians' claim to provide certain knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحقيقة الفَرْق الصحيح: أن «الماهية» هي ما يَرْتَسِمُ في النفس من الشيء»، و «الوجود هو نفس ما يكون في الخارج منه . وهذا فرق صحيح. فإن الفرق بين ما في النفس وما في الخارج ثابت معلوم لا ريب فيه . وأما تقدير حقيقة لا تكون ثابتة في العلم ولا في الوجود فهذا باطل .
ومعلوم أن لفظ «الماهية يُراد به ما في النفس والموجود في الخارج . (۱) ولفظ «الوجود» يُراد به بيان ما في النفس، والموجود في الخارج (۱). فمتى أريد بهما ما في النفس فَـ «الماهية هي الوجود»، وإن أريد بهما ما في الخارج فـ «الماهية» هي «الوجود» أيضاً. وأما إذا أريد بأحدهما ما في النفس وبالآخر ما في الوجود الخارج ف «الماهية) غيرُ الوجود» .
(۲)
وكلامهم إنما يستلزم ثبوت ماهية» في الذهن لا في الوجود الخارجي. وهذا لا الماهيات اختراعات نزاع فيه ولا فائدة فيه، إذ هذا خبر عن مجرد وضع واختراع، إذ يقدر كلُّ إنسان أن يخترع ماهية في نفسه غير ما اخترعها الآخر. وإذا ادعى هذا أن «الماهية» هي «الحيوانُ الناطق أمكن الآخَرَ أن يقول : بل هي «الحيوان الضاحك». وإذا قال هذا : إن «الحيوانية» ذاتية لـ «الإنسان» بخلاف العَدَدية» لـ «الزوج والفرد أمكن الآخر أن يعارضه ويقول : بل «العدد» ذاتي لـ «الزوج والفرد»، و «اللون» ذاتي لـ «السواد»، بخلاف «الحيوان» فليس ذاتياً لـ «الإنسان». إذ مضمون هذا كله أن يأتي شخص إلى صفات متماثلة في الخارج فيدعي أن «الماهية» التي يخترعها في نفسه هي هذه الصفة دون هذه. فإنه إن جعل هذا مطابقاً للأمر في نفسه ـ وهو قولهم - كان مبطلاً . وإن قال : هذا اصطلاح اصطلحته ، قوبل باصطلاح آخر، وكان هذا مما لا فائدة فيه .
And the reality of the correct distinction is that 'mahiya' is what is depicted in the soul of a thing, and 'wujud' is the very thing that exists externally. And this is a correct distinction. For the distinction between what is in the soul and what is in the external is established, known, without doubt. But to posit a reality that is neither established in knowledge nor in existence, this is false.
And it is known that the term 'mahiya' is used to refer to what is in the soul and what exists externally. And the term 'wujud' is used to refer to the description of what is in the soul and what exists externally. So if both are used to refer to what is in the soul, then 'mahiya' is 'wujud'; and if both are used to refer to what is external, then 'mahiya' is also 'wujud'. But if one is used to refer to what is in the soul and the other to what is external, then 'mahiya' is other than 'wujud'.
Their speech only entails the establishment of a 'mahiya' in the mind, not in external existence. And this (the invention of mahiyas in the mind) is not disputed and has no benefit, for it is merely a report of arbitrary positing and invention, as every person can invent a mahiya in his mind different from what another invents. If this one claims that 'mahiya' is 'rational animal', another could say: rather it is 'laughing animal'. And if he says: 'animality' is essential (dhati) for 'human' as opposed to 'numericality' for 'even' and 'odd', another could oppose him and say: rather 'number' is essential for 'even' and 'odd', and 'color' is essential for 'blackness', as opposed to 'animal' which is not essential for 'human'. For the content of all this is that a person comes to similar attributes in the external and claims that the 'mahiya' he invents in his mind is this attribute and not that. So if he makes this correspond to the thing in itself—and this is their statement—he is wrong. And if he says: this is a convention I have adopted, he can be countered by another convention, and this is something that has no benefit.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a critical clarification. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating the correct distinction between mahiya (essence) and wujud (existence). He defines mahiya as the mental image of a thing, and wujud as the external reality itself. This distinction between mental and external is undeniable. However, the logicians' notion of a mahiya that has no mental or external existence is false. He then explains that when both terms refer to the same domain (both mental or both external), they are identical; only when one refers to the mental and the other to the external are they different. The logicians' talk implies a mahiya only in the mind, which is an arbitrary invention without benefit. For example, if one defines 'human' as 'rational animal', another can define it as 'laughing animal'. Similarly, they claim 'animality' is essential to 'human' but 'number' is not essential to 'even' and 'odd'; yet another can reverse this. Thus their essential/accidental distinction is merely a convention, with no grounding in external reality. If they claim correspondence to reality, they are wrong; if they admit it is a convention, it can be countered by another convention, making the whole exercise futile. This passage guards against the misunderstanding that mahiya and wujud are two separate independent realities.

Key Terms

الوجود
الفرق الصحيح
ذاتي

A) They are identical.
B) They are different.
C) Mahiya is a part of wujud.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The correct distinction between mahiya and wujud is the distinction between mental representation and external reality; they are identical when considered within the same domain.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Essential/Accidental Distinction
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim about attributes known with/without middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's two objections to the distinction between attributes known with and without a middle term, and explain why he reduces the essential/accidental distinction to linguistic convention.

Core Idea
Attribute distinctions based on knowledge vs. proof are epistemological, not ontological.

Ibn Taymiyya here targets a specific argument from Ibn Sina and the logicians. They claimed that some attributes, like 'animality' for animal, are known without a middle term, while others, like 'evenness' for a large number, require a proof. Ibn Taymiyya argues this is not a real distinction in reality, but merely a difference in human knowledge. Furthermore, he exposes that the very categories of 'essential' and 'accidental' are based on linguistic convention, not objective ontology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Find the claim that some attributes (e.g., animality) are known without a middle term, while others (e.g., evenness of large numbers) require a middle term. Note that they consider the former 'essential' and the latter 'accidental'.
The example used: animality for animal vs. evenness for 1372.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's first objection
He says this distinction is about human knowledge (epistemological), not about the attributes themselves (ontological). The fact that we know one attribute directly and another through proof does not make the attributes different in reality.
Evenness is an objective property of the number regardless of how we know it.
3
Follow his second objection
He reveals that 'mahiya' (essence) and its parts are defined by the three denotations (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam). These are tied to the speaker's intention and linguistic convention, not objective reality. Thus the essential/accidental division is arbitrary.
The logicians' own theory of denotation shows their distinction is conventional.
4
Evaluate the strength of the critique
Assess whether Ibn Taymiyya successfully undermines the ontological basis of the essential/accidental distinction. Does his linguistic reduction hold? Consider possible counterarguments.
Some later philosophers argued that convention can still track real essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فهم يريدون بذلك ما ذكره ابن سينا وغيره من أن كونَ «العدد زوجاً وفرداً ليس وصفاً بيناً لكل عدد بل تارة يُعلم ثبوتُه لـ «العدد» بلا وسط، كما يعلم أن الاثنين نصف الأربعة»، وتارة لا يعلم ثبوته إلا بوسط به يُعلم أن هذا نصفُ هذا، كما يعلم أن «ألفاً وثلاث مئة واثنين وسبعين لها نصف»، بخلاف الحيوانية» لـ «الحيوان»، فإنها بينة بلا وسط، إذ كلُّ حيوان يعلم أنه حيوان .
قيل : هذا باطل من وجهين :
الوجه الأول أحدهما : إن هذا أمر يرجع إلى علم الإنسان بأن هذه الصفة ثابتة للموصوف بلا وسط، وإلى أن علمه بأن هذه الصفة ثابتةٌ لا بد له من وسط وليس هذا فرقاً يعود إلى الموجودات في نفسها فإن كون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً هو مثلُ كون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً، سواء علم الإنسان بذلك أو لم يعلم. وإذا كان علمه بأحدهما يحتاج إلى دليل دون الآخَرِ لم يُوجِب ذلك الفرق بينهما في نفس الأمر .
ولكن هذا مما يبين حقيقة قولهم الذي بيناه في غير هذا الموضع، وهو أن «الماهية» عبارةٌ عما دل عليه اللفظ بـ «المطابقة) ؛ وجزؤها الداخل فيها ما دل عليه اللفظ بـ «التضمن» ؛ واللازم الخارج عنها ما دل عليه بـ «الالتزام . فترجع «الماهية» و «جزؤها الداخل» و «اللازم «الخارج إلى مدلول «المطابقة» و «التضمن» و «الالتزام» . وهذا أمر يتبع قصد المتكلم وغايته وما دل عليه بلفظه ، لا يتبع الحقائق الموجودة في نفسها ؛ فإن تصور المتكلم قد يكون مطابقاً وقد يكون غير مطابق .
وهم هنا فرقوا بين الصفات المتماثلة فجعلوا بعضها ذاتياً» داخلاً في الحقيقة،
For it is said: they intend by that what Ibn Sina and others mentioned, that being 'even' and 'odd' is not an evident description for every number, but sometimes its establishment for 'number' is known without a middle term, as it is known that two is half of four, and sometimes its establishment is not known except through a middle term by which it is known that this is half of that, as it is known that 'one thousand three hundred and seventy-two' has a half, unlike 'animality' for 'animal', for indeed it is evident without a middle term, since every animal knows that it is an animal.
It is said: this is false from two aspects:
The first aspect: This is a matter that returns to the knowledge of man that this attribute is established for the described without a middle term, and that his knowledge that this attribute is established necessarily has a middle term. And this is not a distinction that goes back to the existing things in themselves. For the fact that this number is even or odd is the same as the fact that this number is even or odd, whether man knows that or not. And if his knowledge of one of them needs a proof without the other, that does not necessitate a difference between them in the thing itself.
But this makes clear the reality of their statement which we have explained elsewhere, namely that 'mahiya' is an expression for what the word indicates by 'mutabaqa'; and its internal part is what the word indicates by 'tadammun'; and the external necessary consequence is what the word indicates by 'iltizam'. So 'mahiya', its internal part, and its external necessary consequence revert to the meanings of 'mutabaqa', 'tadammun', and 'iltizam'. And this is a matter that follows the intention of the speaker and his purpose and what his word indicates, not the realities existing in themselves. For the conception of the speaker may be corresponding to reality or may not be corresponding.
And they here differentiate between similar attributes, making some essential (dhati) and internal to the reality.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific argument from Ibn Sina and others. The objection claims that some attributes (like animality for animal) are known without a middle term, while others (like evenness for a large number) require a middle term. Ibn Taymiyya refutes this with two points. First, the distinction is epistemological, not ontological: it is about human knowledge, not about the attributes themselves. A number's evenness is an objective property regardless of whether we know it directly or need a proof. Second, this argument actually reveals the true nature of the logicians' position: 'mahiya' is simply what a word denotes by mutabaqa (full correspondence), its internal parts by tadammun (inclusion), and its external consequences by iltizam (implication). This is a matter of linguistic convention and speaker intention, not of objective reality. Thus their division of attributes into essential (internal) and accidental (external) is arbitrary, based on how they choose to define a term. This passage guards against the misunderstanding that the essential/accidental distinction is an objective ontological difference.

التضمن
الالتزام
الوسط

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the distinction between essential and accidental attributes is based on human knowledge and linguistic convention, not on objective reality.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Essence-Existence Distinction
Author analyzes the necessary concomitant of essence vs. accidental of existence, concluding no real difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the distinction between essence and existence collapses under analysis, and apply his reasoning to similar metaphysical distinctions in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The essence-existence distinction is only a mental abstraction; in reality they are inseparable.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at a core doctrine of the philosophers and logicians: the real distinction between essence (mahiyya) and existence (wujud). He argues that whether you consider the mental or external realm, the supposed difference evaporates. His critique is not merely philosophical—it has direct consequences for how we understand divine attributes and the creation of the world. Let's walk through his razor-sharp analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts being distinguished
Ibn Taymiyya refers to 'necessary concomitant of essence' (lazim al-mahiyya) and 'accidental necessary for existence' (aradi lazim li-wujudiha). Understand what these mean. The logicians claim one is part of essence, the other a necessary accident of existence.
2
Examine the mental-criterion argument
The logicians say we can mentally separate essence from its concomitant, e.g., imagine a 'horse without a shadow' or 'a black person who is not black'. But Ibn Taymiyya asks: is this mental separation about the essence as mental or as external?
3
Follow the two suppositions
If it's the mental essence, then the mental essence is just its existence in the mind, so no difference. If it's the external essence, then stripping it of concomitants is equivalent to stripping external existence of same; either both are with concomitants or both without—no real distinction.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The only difference between essence and existence is the difference between mental and external existence. Postulating an external essence without its necessary concomitant is as false as postulating external existence without that concomitant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما اللازم للماهية و العرضي اللازم لوجودها فملخصه أنه يمكن أن يُفْرَض الفرق بين في الذهن «ماهية خالية عن هذا اللازم بخلاف الآخر، كما يُفْرَض في الذهن فرس لا ظل لها» و «فرس غير مخلوقة»، بل كما يفرض في الذهن زنجي غير أسود و غراب غير أسود»، وأمثال ذلك .
عنى
فيقال: إذا قدر أن هذا الذي في الذهن هو الحقيقة، فإن عنى به «هو حقيقة ما في الذهن فهذا حق، وهذا هو وجود ما في الذهن ، فلا فرق بين الماهية» و «وجودها». وإن عنى به أن هذا هو الماهية التي في الخارج كان بمنزلة أن يقال : هذا هو «الموجود الذي في الخارج. فإنه إن جعلت الماهية التي في الخارج مجردة عن هذه الصفات اللازمة أمكن أن يجعل الوجود الذي في الخارج مجرداً عن هذه الصفات اللازمة. وإن جعل هذا هو نفس الماهية «بلوازمها كان هذا بمنزلة أن يقال : هذا «الوجود بلوازمه» .
وعلى التقديرين فلا فرق بين الوجود» و «الماهية» إلا فرق بين ما في الذهن وما في الخارج. وتقدير ماهية في الخارج بدون هذا اللازم كتقدير «وجود في الخارج بدون هذا اللازم. وهما باطلان .
As for the necessary concomitant of the essence and the accidental necessary for its existence, its summary is that a difference can be supposed in the mind: 'an essence free from this concomitant' unlike the other, just as one supposes in the mind 'a horse without a shadow' and 'an uncreated horse', rather as one supposes in the mind 'a black person who is not black' and 'a crow that is not black', and the like. It is said: if it is considered that this thing in the mind is the reality, then if by it is meant 'it is the reality of what is in the mind', this is true, and this is the existence of what is in the mind, so there is no difference between 'essence' and 'its existence'. And if by it is meant that this is the essence that is in the external world, it is like saying: this is 'the existent that is in the external world'. For if the essence in the external world is made abstracted from these necessary attributes, it is possible to make the existence in the external world abstracted from these necessary attributes. And if this is made the very essence 'with its necessary concomitants', then it is like saying: this is 'existence with its necessary concomitants'. On both suppositions, there is no difference between 'existence' and 'essence' except the difference between what is in the mind and what is in the external world. And to suppose an essence in the external world without this concomitant is like supposing 'an existence in the external world without this concomitant. Both are false.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the logicians' distinction between 'necessary concomitant of essence' (lazim al-mahiyya) and 'accidental necessary for its existence' (aradi lazim li-wujudiha). Ibn Taymiyya summarizes the distinction: you can mentally separate an essence from its necessary concomitant, e.g., a horse without a shadow, an uncreated horse, a black person who is not black, etc. But he then argues: if you take the mental concept as the reality, then it is simply the existence of the mental concept, so essence and existence are identical. If you take it as the external essence, then you can strip it of concomitants only by also stripping the external existence of those same concomitants. If you include the concomitants, then you are really talking about existence with its concomitants. In either case, the only difference between essence and existence is the difference between mental and external existence. To suppose an external essence without its necessary concomitant is as absurd as an external existence without that concomitant. Both are false. Hence the claimed distinction is invalid.

Key Terms
لازم الماهية
عرضي لازم لوجودها
ماهية
وجود

B) Mental and external existence.
C) Essential and accidental attributes.
D) Subject and predicate.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that the essence-existence distinction, when examined, reduces to the distinction between mental and external existence, not a real metaphysical separation.

The Debate: Is the Universal Part of the Particular?
Ibn Taymiyya presents two opposing views on the universal being part of the particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the two positions on whether the universal (al-mutlaq) is part of the particular (al-mu'ayyan) in external existence.

Core Idea
The universal cannot be a part of the particular because the many cannot be a part of the few.

Ibn Taymiyya presents a debate among scholars about the ontological status of universals. Some argue that the absolute universal is a constituent of particular individuals, while others object based on logical grounds. This lesson unpacks their arguments by examining key terms and the logical puzzle involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim that the absolute is part of the particular
Read the first part of the passage where Ibn Taymiyya states: 'among people there are those who say: the absolute (mutlaq) is a part of the specific (mu'ayyan)'. Notice that this view also says the universal (al-‘amm) is part of the particular (al-khass).
The claim is that whenever a specific human exists, the meaning of 'human' (the absolute) exists as part of it.
2
Find the counterargument
Look for the denial: 'And among people there are those who deny this'. The counterargument is that the universal allows participation by many individuals (kathir), while the particular (mu'ayyan) is a single individual (qalil). The rhetorical question asks: How can the many be part of the few?
This counterargument is based on the logical principle that a universal cannot be a constituent of a particular because the universal is broader.
3
Note the key terms
Identify the terms al-mutlaq (absolute/universal), al-mu'ayyan (specific/particular), al-‘amm (general), and al-khass (specific). Understand that here 'mutlaq' is used synonymously with 'kulli' (universal).
These terms are central to the entire discussion. In later texts, 'juz'i' often replaces 'mu'ayyan'.
