والتحقيق أنه يوجد في الخارج - لكن معيّناً مشخصاً». فلا يوجد في الخارج إلا إنسان معين وفيه حيوانية معينة و ناطقية معينة، ولا يوجد فيه إلا علم معين» و «خبر معين . ثم من الناس من يقول : إن المطلق» جزء من المعين». ويقولون : «العام» جزء من «الخاص». فإنه حيث وُجد هذا الإنسان) و هذا «الحيوان وجد مسمى الإنسان» و مسمى «الحيوان»، وهو «المطلق». ومن الناس من ينكر هذا ويقول : المطلق «الكلي هو الذي لا يمنع تصور معناه من وقوع الشركة فيه، فيجوز أن تدخل فيه أفراد كثيرة و المعين» هو جزئي يمنع تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه . فكيف يكون الكثير» بعض «القليل»؟ وكيف يكون «العام» أو «المطلق» - الذي يتناول أفراداً كثيرة أو يصلح للتناول - جزءاً من «المعين الجزئي الذي لا يتناول إلا ذلك الشخص الجزئي الموجود في الخارج؟ فصل الخطاب : أنه ليس في الخارج إلا جزئي معين، ليس في الخارج ما هو مطلق عام مع كونه مطلقاً عاماً». وإذا وجد المعين الجزئي فَـ «الإنسان» و «الحيوان وجدت فيه إنسانية معينة مختصة مقيَّدة غير عامة ولا مطلقة». فـ «المطلق» إذا قيل هو جزء من المعين) فإنما يكون جزءاً» بشرط أن لا يكون مطلقاً عاماً». والمعنى أن ما يتصوره الذهنُ مطلقاً عاماً يوجد في الخارج، لكن لا يوجد إلا مقيداً خاصاً». وهذا كما إذا قيل : إن ما في نفسي وُجد» أو «فعل» أو «فعلتُ ما في نفسي فمعناه أن الصور الذهنيةَ وُجِدَت في الخارج، أي/ وُجد ما يطابقها . إذا عُرف هذا فكل ما يعلم إنما يعلم بـ علم معين . وهذا «العلم المعين فيه من العلم المطلق العام، ليس فيه علم مطلق عام» مع كونه مطلقاً عاماً». والإنسان إذا تصور هذا الإنسان» و «هذا العلم و هذا الخبر» لم يتصوره مطلقاً عاماً، وإنما تَصَوَّره معيناً خاصاً». وذلك لا يستلزم أن يعلم المحدود الكلي الجامع المانع، الشامل لجميع الأفراد، المانع من دخول غيرها فيه . فـ غير العلم إذا توقف على حصول العلم» فإنما توقف على حصول علم معين جزئي»، لا على تصوره»، كما قيل في الجواب الأول .
And the verification is that it exists in the external world — but as a specific, individuated thing. So nothing exists in the external world except a specific human, and in it there is a specific animality and a specific rationality, and nothing exists in it except a specific knowledge and a specific statement. Then among people there are those who say: the absolute (mutlaq) is a part of the specific (mu'ayyan). And they say: the universal (al-‘amm) is a part of the particular (al-khass). For wherever this human and this animal exist, the meaning of 'human' and 'animal' exists, which is the absolute. And among people there are those who deny this and say: the absolute, the universal, is that whose meaning does not prevent participation in it, so it is permissible for many individuals to enter into it; while the specific is a particular that prevents participation in it. So how can the many be a part of the few? And how can the universal or the absolute — which encompasses many individuals or is suitable for encompassing — be a part of the specific particular which encompasses nothing but that particular individual existing in the external world? The decisive statement: there is nothing in the external world except a specific particular; there is nothing in the external world that is an absolute universal while being absolute and universal. And when the particular individual exists, then 'human' and 'animal' exist in it as a specific, restricted, qualified humanity and animality, not universal and not absolute. So the 'absolute' if it is said to be a part of the specific, then it is only a part on the condition that it is not absolute and universal. And the meaning is that what the mind conceives as absolute and universal exists in the external world, but it does not exist except as restricted and specific. This is like when it is said: 'what is in my mind existed' or 'I did what was in my mind', meaning that the mental images existed in the external world, i.e., what corresponds to them existed. If this is known, then everything that is known is only known by a specific knowledge. And this specific knowledge contains something of the absolute universal knowledge, but there is no absolute universal knowledge within it while being absolute and universal. And when a person conceives this human and this knowledge and this statement, he does not conceive it as absolute and universal, but rather conceives it as specific and particular. And that does not necessitate that he knows the universal, comprehensive, exclusive limited (mahdud) that includes all individuals and prevents others from entering into it. For other than knowledge, if it depends on the acquisition of knowledge, then it depends only on the acquisition of a specific particular knowledge, not on its conceptualization, as was said in the first answer.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' view that the universal (mutlaq) is part of the particular (mu'ayyan). Ibn Taymiyya begins by asserting the verification (tahqiq) that in external existence, only specific, individuated entities exist. For example, there is no 'human' in general but only a specific human with specific animality and rationality. He then presents two opposing views: some say the absolute (mutlaq) is part of the specific, while others deny this, arguing that the universal allows participation by many individuals, whereas the particular excludes participation. Ibn Taymiyya then gives the decisive statement (fasl al-khitab): only the particular exists externally; the universal exists only in the mind. When a particular individual exists, the universal concepts 'human' and 'animal' exist in it only as restricted, not as universal. Thus, if the absolute is considered part of the specific, it is only under the condition of not being absolute and universal. The passage concludes by applying this to knowledge: all knowledge is of specific things, and conceptualization of a specific does not require knowing the universal comprehensive limited (mahdud). Thus, knowledge does not depend on definitions.

Key Terms
المطلق
المعين
العام
الخاص
فصل الخطاب

A) Because the absolute exists only in the mind
B) Because the many cannot be part of the few
C) Because the specific is not real
D) Because the absolute is identical to the specific

Key Takeaway
The debate hinges on whether the universal, which applies to many, can be a constituent of a single particular; the counterargument logically denies this.

Ibn Taymiyya's Decisive Answer: Only the Particular Exists Externally
Ibn Taymiyya gives his decisive answer on the ontological status of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that only particular individuals exist in external reality and that universals exist only in the mind as mental abstractions.

Core Idea
Nothing in external existence is universal; universals are mental constructs that exist only as restricted in particulars.

Ibn Taymiyya resolves the debate by asserting his decisive statement (fasl al-khitab): there is no universal in external existence; only particular individuals exist. The universal exists only in the mind. This has profound implications for epistemology and the nature of knowledge. In this lesson, we unpack his reasoning and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the decisive statement
Find the phrase 'fasl al-khitab' in the passage. Ibn Taymiyya declares: 'ليس في الخارج إلا جزئي معين' (there is nothing in the external world except a specific particular). This is his conclusive position.
This statement is the central thesis of the entire passage.
2
Understand the condition for the absolute being part of the particular
Ibn Taymiyya says: if the absolute is said to be a part of the specific, it must be on the condition that it is not absolute and universal. In other words, when the particular exists, the universal concept is present only as restricted (muqayyad), not as universal.
The universal 'human' in Zayd is Zayd's specific humanity, not the universal 'human' in the abstract sense.
3
Apply to knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya applies this principle to knowledge: all knowledge is of specific things (e.g., 'this human', 'this knowledge'). Knowing a particular does not require knowing the universal definition (al-mahdud al-kulli al-jami' al-mani').
This challenges the logicians' claim that all knowledge depends on definitions (hadd).

The Self-Defeating Nature of the Logicians' Claim
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' claim that knowledge of tasdiqat is only through qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why the logicians' claim that all affirmations require syllogism is self-defeating according to Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
The logicians' universal claim is self-refuting because it fails its own standard of proof.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets a foundational claim of the logicians: that no affirmation (tasdiq) can be known except through the syllogism (qiyas) they have defined. He argues that this very assertion is a negative proposition that cannot be known intuitively (badihi) and is offered without proof. By their own criteria, it would itself require a syllogism to be proven, yet they provide none. Thus, their position is internally inconsistent and lacks justification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The claim is: 'Nothing is known of affirmations except through syllogism' — a universal negative. Recognize it as a proposition that the logicians assert as foundational.
2
Classify the claim according to logicians' own epistemology
The logicians divide knowledge into intuitive (badihi) and discursive (nazari). Their claim is not intuitive, so it must be discursive and require a proof (a syllogism) to be established.
3
Check if proof is provided
Ibn Taymiyya notes that the logicians offer no proof for their universal negative. They merely assert it. Thus, by their own standards, the claim is unjustified.
4
Uncover the self-defeating structure
The claim 'no tasdiq is known except by qiyas' is itself a tasdiq. If it were true, it would require a syllogism to be known, but no such syllogism exists. Hence the claim cannot be known — a self-refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حصر حصولِ العلم على «القياس» قول بغير علم
قولهم : إنه لا يُعلم شيء من التصديقات إلا بالقياس - الذي ذكروا صورته و مادته - قضيةٌ سَلْبية نافية ليست معلومة بالبديهة. ولم يذكروا على هذا السلب دليلاً أصلاً، فصاروا مُدَّعِين ما لم يبينوه، بل قائلين بغير علم؛ إذ العلم بهذا السلب متعذر على أصلهم. فمن أين لهم أنه لا يمكن أحداً من بني آدم أن يعلم شيئاً من التصديقات - التي ليست بديهية عندهم - إلا بواسطة القياس المنطقي الشمولي» - الذي وصفوا مادته وصورته؟
Limiting the attainment of knowledge to syllogism is an unjustified claim. Their statement: 'Nothing is known of affirmations except through syllogism – which they have described in terms of form and matter' is a negative proposition that is not known intuitively. And they have not mentioned any proof for this negation at all. So they are making claims without evidence, indeed speaking without knowledge, since knowledge of this negation is impossible according to their own principle. From where do they get that no human being can know any affirmation – which are not intuitive according to them – except by means of the comprehensive logical syllogism whose matter and form they have described?
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a critique. Ibn Taymiyya points out that the logicians' universal negative claim cannot itself be known either intuitively (badihi) or through demonstration (since it is the foundation). Their claim that all non-intuitive knowledge requires a syllogism is self-defeating. The author exposes a logical inconsistency: the very claim that 'no tasdiq is known except by qiyas' is a tasdiq that, according to their own criteria, would need a syllogism to be proven, yet they provide no proof. Thus, they speak without knowledge. This is a classic reductio ad absurdum of the logicians' position.

Key Terms
القياس
التصديقات
البديهة

Key Takeaway

The Third Station: Some Concomitants Need No Middle Term
Author introduces the third station and explains the nature of the middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's claim that some necessary concomitants are known without a middle term and identify what he means by 'middle term' (al-wasat).

Core Idea
Some necessary truths are known directly through complete conception, not through syllogistic proof.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya introduces the third station (maqam) of his critique: not all necessary concomitants require a middle term (i.e., a proof) to be known. He defines the middle term as the proof itself, and emphasizes that some knowledge is immediate. This challenges the logicians' assumption that all knowledge must be mediated by a syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about lawazim
Find the sentence stating that some necessary concomitants are established without a middle term. Note that this is the key thesis of the third station.
2
Define the middle term
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly says: 'the proof that is the middle term' (al-dalil alladhi huwa al-hadd al-awsat). Understand that 'middle term' here is synonymous with 'proof' or 'syllogistic mediator', not a physical middle.
In standard Aristotelian logic, the middle term is a term in a syllogism; here it is the proof that connects subject and predicate.
3
Recognize the universality claim being rejected
The logicians claim that all necessary concomitants require a middle term to be known. Ibn Taymiyya is rejecting that universality by presenting exceptions.
This is the target of his critique: the epistemological necessity of the middle term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - من «اللوازم» ما ثبت بغير «وسط»
وهو الدليل» الذي هو «الحدّ الأوسط» - سواء كان تصور الطرفين «بديهياً» أو لم يكن . ومعلوم أن الناس يتفاوتون في قُوَى الأذهان أعظم من تفاوتهم في قوى الأبدان، ويتفاوتون في حسن التصور وتمامه وفي سوء التصور ونقصه. فمن الناس من يكون في سرعة التصور وجودته في غاية يباين بها غيره مباينة كبيرة. وحينئذ فيتصور الطرفين تصوراً تاماً بحيث تتبين بذلك التصور التامّ «اللوازم» التي لا تتبين لغيره الذي لم يتصور الطرفين التصور التام .
وذلك أن من الناس من يكون لم يتصور الطرفين إلا ببعض صفاتهما «المميّزة»، فيكون من تصورها ببعض صفاتهما المشتركة مع ذلك - سواء سميت «ذاتية» أو لم تسم - عالماً بثبوت تلك الصفات لهما وثبوتِ كثير مما يكون «لازماً» لهما، بخلاف من لم يتصور إلا الصفات المميّزة)).
The third station: among necessary concomitants, there is what is established without a middle term. And it is the proof that is the middle term—whether the conception of the two extremes is self-evident or not. And it is known that people differ in the powers of their minds more than they differ in the powers of their bodies, and they differ in the quality of conception and its completeness and in the poorness of conception and its deficiency. Some people are in the speed and excellence of conception at an extreme that makes them differ greatly from others. So then they conceive the two extremes with a complete conception such that by that complete conception the necessary concomitants become clear that do not become clear to another who has not conceived the two extremes completely. That is because some people have not conceived the two extremes except by some of their distinguishing attributes, so whoever conceives them by some of their common attributes—whether called 'essential' or not—knows the subsistence of those attributes for them and the subsistence of many things that are necessary for them, unlike one who conceives only the distinguishing attributes.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage introduces the third station (maqam) regarding necessary concomitants (al-lawazim). The author states that some necessary concomitants are established without a middle term (wasat). Here, 'middle term' means the proof (al-dalil) that is the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat). The author then emphasizes that people vary greatly in mental abilities — more than in physical abilities. Some people have a rapid and excellent conception, such that they can conceive the two extremes of a proposition completely, and thus they perceive the necessary concomitants that others, who lack that complete conception, do not perceive. The author illustrates that some people only conceive the two extremes by their distinguishing attributes (al-sifat al-mumayyiza), while others conceive them by their common attributes (al-sifat al-mushtaraka), regardless of whether those are called essential (dhatiyya) or not. The latter gains knowledge of the attributes and many necessary concomitants, whereas the former does not. This sets the stage for a critique of the logicians' claim that some necessary concomitants are known without a middle term.

Variation in Human Conception and Knowledge of Concomitants
Author explains variation in people's understanding of the middle term and its impact on knowing concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between complete and partial conception, and explain how variation in cognitive ability affects the perception of necessary concomitants.

Core Idea
People differ more in mental powers than physical, and complete conception reveals hidden concomitants.

Ibn Taymiyya expands on how people vary in their ability to conceive the two extremes (subject and predicate). Some only grasp distinguishing attributes, while others grasp common attributes, leading to knowledge of many concomitants. This variation explains why some people see necessary relationships that others miss, without any need for a syllogistic middle term.

Guided Path
1
Compare mental and physical powers
Ibn Taymiyya says people differ in mental powers (quwā al-adhhān) more than in physical powers. Note the emphasis on cognitive variation.
This is a foundational observation: the mind's capacity is not uniform across individuals.
2
Distinguish complete vs. incomplete conception
Complete conception (tasawwur tamm) of the two extremes reveals lawazim; incomplete conception hides them. Identify which attributes each type of person grasps.
3
Identify common vs. distinguishing attributes
Some people conceive the extremes by their distinguishing attributes (al-mumayyiza) only; others by common attributes (al-mushtaraka). The latter knows many lawazim, the former does not.
The common attributes are those shared with other concepts; distinguishing attributes are unique.
4
Understand the epistemological implication
Knowledge of lawazim is not dependent on a universal middle term, but on the depth of one's conception. This relativizes the necessity of syllogistic proof.
This is a direct challenge to the Greek logical tradition's universal method.

Source Text

The third station: among necessary concomitants, there is what is established without a middle term. And it is the proof that is the middle term—whether the conception of the two extremes is self-evident or not. And it is known that people differ in the powers of their minds more than they differ in the powers of their bodies, and they differ in the quality of conception and its completeness and in the poorness of conception and its deficiency. So then they conceive the two extremes with a complete conception such that by that complete conception the necessary concomitants become clear that do not become clear to another who has not conceived the two extremes completely. Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Essential/Accidental Distinction by Middle Term
Critical refutation of the middle-term criterion for essential vs accidental necessary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' distinction between essential and accidental necessary based on the need for a middle term, and explain why the distinction fails.

Core Idea
The middle-term criterion cannot separate essence from accident because some accidents are known without a middle term.

The logicians claimed that essential attributes are known without a middle term, while accidental necessary attributes require a middle term. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this distinction collapses because some necessary concomitants (which are accidental) are also established without a middle term, and the middle term itself is understood as proof, which varies by knower. This passage is a masterclass in logical critique that uses the opponent's own definitions against them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' distinction
Find where the source states the logicians' criterion: essential attributes are established without a middle term, while accidental necessary attributes require one. Note this is the claim under attack.
The logicians' distinction is one of three differences between dhati and aradi lazim.
2
Clarify what 'middle term' means here
Ibn Taymiyya explains that the 'middle term' (wasat) intended by Ibn Sina and verifiers is the proof (dalil), i.e., the middle term of a syllogism. It is not an inherent part of the described thing.
This clarification is crucial because a common misunderstanding treats wasat as something intrinsic.
3
Expose the common misunderstanding
The source mentions that some (like al-Razi) mistakenly thought the middle term is in the described thing itself, such that the necessary attribute subsists through it. Ibn Taymiyya rejects this as false.
Ibn Taymiyya calls this interpretation 'false in itself' and notes it has been refuted elsewhere.
4
Find the key counterexample
Ibn Taymiyya points out that some accidental necessary attributes are established without any middle term, just like essential ones. This directly undermines the logicians' criterion.
The source says: 'among necessary concomitants there is what is established without a middle term.'
5
Evaluate the alternative interpretation
Some logicians reinterpret 'middle term' as proof for mental subsistence (not external). Ibn Taymiyya shows this also fails because the need for proof varies among people, and some attributes still require no proof.
He distinguishes between burhan al-illa (demonstration of cause) and burhan al-dalala (demonstration of indication).

The invalidity of the distinction between the essential (al-dhati) and the necessary (al-lazim) because each is established without a middle term. And that is: they determined in logic that among necessary concomitants there is what is necessary without a middle term, so this is known by the very conception of necessity. The intended meaning of the middle term mentioned in these places is, according to Ibn Sina and the verifiers among them, the proof, which is the middle term. And this varies according to people; one person may need to understand the meaning of the proof for knowledge of the necessity, unlike another. And whoever does not understand their intention in this place thinks that they meant by 'middle term' that which is middle in the very described thing such that the subsistence of the necessary attribute for the malzum is through it, and that it is not established by itself, as understood from them, as we have explained their words elsewhere. This is also false in itself. And there is no doubt that among necessary concomitants there is what requires a middle term, and among them what does not require a middle term, according to them. This is one of the three differences (1) by which they distinguished between the essential and the accidental necessary for the essence. And they have invalidated this distinction. Some of them express this difference by 'causality' (al-ta'lil), as Ibn al-Hajib does. So if among necessary concomitants there is what is established in actual fact without a middle term and without a cause, this distinction between the essential and these necessary concomitants does not remain, so the distinction by this is invalidated. However, whoever conceives the essence by these necessary concomitants has a more complete conception than one who does not conceive it by these necessary concomitants. And if the intended meaning of 'middle term' is 'the proof by which mental subsistence, not external subsistence, is caused,' then this varies among people, and there is no doubt that what is used as evidence—whether called 'syllogism' (qiyas) or 'demonstration' (burhan) or otherwise—may be a cause for the subsistence of the judgment in actual fact, and it is called 'syllogism of cause' and 'demonstration of cause' and 'demonstration of 'why''. And it may not be so, and it is the absolute proof, called 'syllogism of indication' and 'demonstration of indication' and 'demonstration of 'that''. And this is what Ibn Sina and others intended by 'middle term'. And this is something in which people's conditions differ. So the distinction between the essential and the accidental necessary is even more remote. For this reason, Ibn Sina invalidated the distinction by this, as we have mentioned his wording [78] in another place. For—on both interpretations—among necessary concomitants there is what is established without a middle term. So if it is said: 'The essential is what is established without a middle term,' and it is known that 'among necessary concomitants there is what is established without a middle term,' it becomes clear that this distinction is invalid in every estimation.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' distinction between essential (dhati) and accidental necessary (aradi lazim) based on the need for a middle term. The author, Ibn Taymiyya, begins by stating the principle: some necessary concomitants are known without a middle term, simply by conceiving the necessity itself. He clarifies that the 'middle term' (wasat) intended by Ibn Sina and the verifiers is the proof (dalil), i.e., the middle term (hadd awsat), and this varies among individuals. Some people need a proof to grasp the necessity, others do not. A common mistake is to think that the middle term is something inherent in the described thing (al-mawsuf) through which the necessary attribute subsists, as some logicians like al-Razi mistakenly held; Ibn Taymiyya rejects this as false. He then explains that this very distinction is one of three differences between essential and accidental necessary. Since some necessary concomitants are established without a middle term or cause, the distinction collapses. Even if one conceives the essence through these necessary concomitants, that does not make them essential. He further considers the alternative interpretation that the middle term is a proof for mental subsistence (not external), which also varies among people. Ibn Taymiyya notes that Ibn Sina himself invalidated this distinction. In conclusion, since it is known that some necessary concomitants are established without a middle term, the definition of essential as 'what is established without a middle term' does not exclude accidental necessary, so the distinction is invalid in every respect.

العرضي اللازم
الوسط
التعليل
برهان العلة

The Two Camps on Causality: Occasionalists vs Affirmers of Natural Powers
Author contrasts the occasionalist position with the majority view that affirms natural powers and causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the occasionalist position from the majority affirmation of natural causes and identify the key differences in their views on causality.

Core Idea
The debate on causality is not between Islam and philosophy but within Islamic theology itself.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya lays out the fundamental divide in Islamic theology over whether objects have inherent causal powers. On one side are the occasionalists (nufat al-ta'lil) like Jahm and some Ash'aris, who deny that any created thing has causal efficacy and attribute all regularities directly to God's will. On the other side are the majority of Muslims—theologians, jurists, hadith scholars, and even Mu'tazila—who affirm that fire has a power to heat, water to cool, and so on. Understanding this split is crucial for grasping later debates in usul al-fiqh and kalam about the basis of analogy and the nature of miracles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two camps
Find the Arabic phrases for 'deniers of causation' (نفاة التعليل) and 'the majority of rational people' (جمهور العقلاء). Who is included in each camp according to the text?
The deniers include Jahm and his followers like Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari).
2
Extract the deniers' view on causality
What explanation do the deniers give for the constant conjunction of events (e.g., fire and heat)? They say it is purely by God's will, without one being a cause or producing the other.
Their position is that there is no natural causality, only divine direct action.
3
Extract the affirmers' view on causality
What do the affirmers say exists in fire, water, etc.? They say there is a power (quwwa) that necessitates the effect—e.g., fire has a power to heat, water to cool.
They also use terms like 'nature' (الطبيعة), 'instinct' (الغريزة), and 'habit' (العادة).
4
Notice the terminology used by affirmers
List the synonyms given for the inherent power: الطبيعة, الغريزة, النَّحِيزة, الخُلق, العادة.
These terms all indicate an inherent disposition.
5
Observe the implicit critique
How does the author frame the deniers? They are presented as a minority within theology, and their view is contrasted with the majority of rational people, including non-Muslims. This implies their position is less rational.
Ibn Taymiyya sides with the majority, so his presentation is evaluative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفاة التعليل هم نفاة لكن من لا يثبت «الأسبابَ» و «العلل» من أهل الكلام كالجَهم وموافقيه في ذلك مثل أبي الحسن وأتباعه يجعلون المعلوم اقترانُ أحد الأمرين بالآخر لمحض مشيئة حكمة الله القادر المريد من غير أن يكون أحدهما سبباً للآخر ولا مولداً له. وأما جمهور العقلاء من المسلمين ، وغير المسلمين وأهل السنة من أهل الكلام والفقه والحديث والتصوف، وغير أهل السنة من المعتزلة وغيرهم، فيثبتون الأسباب»، ويقولون : كما يُعلم اقتران أحدهما بالآخر فيُعلم أن في النار قوة تقتضي التسخين، وفي الماء قوة تقتضي التبريد وكذلك في العين قوة تقتضي الإبصار، وفي اللسان قوة تقتضي الذوق. ويثبتون «الطبيعة» التي تسمى «الغَرِيزة» و «النَّحِيزة» و «الخُلق» و «العادة» ونحو ذلك من الأسماء . ولهذا كان السلف كأحمد بن حنبل والحارث المُحاسِبي وغيرهما يقولون: «العقل غريزة». وأما نُفاة «الطبائع » فليس «العقلُ» عندهم إلا مجرد العلم، كما هو قول أبي الحسن الأشعري (العقل عند الأشعري مجرد العلم، ووافقه آخرون)، والقاضي أبي بكر، والقاضي أبي يعلى، وابن عقيل، وأبي الخطاب، والقاضي أبي بكر بن العربي وغيرهم. وإن كان بعض هؤلاء قد يختلف كلامهم، فيثبتون في موضع آخر «الغرائز» و «الأسباب»، كما هو مذهب الفقهاء والجمهور.
The deniers of causation are those who do not affirm 'causes' and 'effective causes' among the theologians, like Jahm and his followers in that, such as Abu al-Hasan and his followers. They consider that what is known is the conjunction of one of the two things with the other purely by the will of God, the Powerful and Willing, without one being a cause of the other nor producing it. As for the majority of rational people among Muslims and non-Muslims, and the Ahl al-Sunna among the theologians, jurists, hadith scholars, and sufis, and also the non-Ahl al-Sunna like the Mu'tazila and others—they affirm 'causes' and say: just as the conjunction of one with the other is known, so it is known that in fire there is a power that requires heating, in water a power that requires cooling, and likewise in the eye a power that requires sight, and in the tongue a power that requires taste. And they affirm 'nature', which is called 'instinct', 'innate disposition', 'character', 'habit', and similar names. For this reason, the early Muslims like Ahmad ibn Hanbal, al-Harith al-Muhasibi, and others used to say: 'Reason is an instinct.' As for the deniers of 'natures', for them 'reason' is nothing but mere knowledge, as is the saying of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (reason according to al-Ash'ari is mere knowledge, and others agreed with him), and al-Qadi Abu Bakr, al-Qadi Abu Ya'la, Ibn 'Aqil, Abu al-Khattab, al-Qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-'Arabi, and others. However, some of these may have varying statements, so they affirm in another place 'instincts' and 'causes', as is the school of the jurists and the majority.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage lays out a foundational theological and epistemological debate: the question of whether objects possess inherent causal powers (asbab, taba'i') or whether all regularities are direct divine interventions without any intermediary causation. Ibn Taymiyya sides with the majority, which includes Ash'aris and Maturidis on one side, and Hanbalis and Mu'tazila on the other, though he notes internal divisions. He clarifies that occasionalists (like Jahm and some Ash'aris) view all conjunctions as mere voluntary acts of God, while the majority hold that fire has a power to heat, water to cool, etc. The passage then discusses the nature of 'aql (intellect): for the deniers of natures, 'aql is simply acquired knowledge ('ilm), while for the early Hanbali scholars, it is an innate quality (ghariza). Ibn Taymiyya points out that even some of the deniers inconsistently affirm instincts and causes elsewhere, aligning with the jurists' practical reason.

Key Terms
نفاة التعليل
الأسباب
الطبيعة
العقل

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Muslims as described in this passage, what is the status of fire?
A) Fire is merely a place where God creates heat directly
B) Fire has a power that necessitates heating
C) Fire is a cause only by metaphorical attribution
D) Fire's effect is unknown

Key Takeaway
The majority of Muslims affirm that created objects have real causal powers, while occasionalists deny this and attribute all events directly to God.

?
Identify which camp the following statement belongs to: 'When fire touches cotton, God creates heat in the cotton, not from the fire.' Answer: occasionalist.

The Nature of Intellect: Innate Instinct or Acquired Knowledge?
Author contrasts the occasionalist position with the majority view that affirms natural powers and causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the Ash'ari and Hanbali definitions of 'aql and explain how each relates to their stance on causality.

Core Idea
How you define 'aql determines how you understand human knowledge and its connection to causality.

In this passage, after discussing causality, Ibn Taymiyya turns to the nature of the intellect ('aql). The deniers of natural powers (nufat al-taba'i') define 'aql as nothing but acquired knowledge ('ilm). This is consistent with their occasionalism: if no innate powers exist, then reason is simply learning from observation. The early Hanbalis, like Ahmad ibn Hanbal, define 'aql as an innate instinct (ghariza). This fits with their affirmation of natural dispositions: just as fire has a powerful instinct to heat, humans have an instinctual reason to know certain truths. This lesson will help you see how ontology and epistemology are intertwined.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions of 'aql
Find in the Arabic where 'aql is defined. One group says it is 'ilm (knowledge), another says it is ghariza (instinct). Who says what?
The deniers of natures, following al-Ash'ari, say it is 'ilm. The Hanbalis (Ahmad, al-Muhasibi) say it is ghariza.
2
Understand the link to causality
Why would a denier of natures define 'aql as mere knowledge? If objects have no inherent powers, then reason cannot be an innate power either; it must be acquired through experience.
Consistency: occasionalism about causality leads to occasionalism about the mind.
3
Understand the alternative definition
What does 'ghariza' imply? It means an innate disposition or instinct, like the power in fire. Reason is a natural faculty that knows certain things prior to experience.
This aligns with affirming natural powers in objects.
4
Identify the inconsistency
The author notes that some deniers (like al-Ash'ari and others) affirm 'instincts' and 'causes' elsewhere. Why is this a problem? It shows inconsistency: they deny natural causality in one context but affirm it in another.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this as a point of criticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفاة التعليل هم نفاة لكن من لا يثبت «الأسبابَ» و «العلل» من أهل الكلام كالجَهم وموافقيه في ذلك مثل أبي الحسن وأتباعه يجعلون المعلوم اقترانُ أحد الأمرين بالآخر لمحض مشيئة حكمة الله القادر المريد من غير أن يكون أحدهما سبباً للآخر ولا مولداً له. وأما جمهور العقلاء من المسلمين ، وغير المسلمين وأهل السنة من أهل الكلام والفقه والحديث والتصوف، وغير أهل السنة من المعتزلة وغيرهم، فيثبتون الأسباب»، ويقولون : كما يُعلم اقتران أحدهما بالآخر فيُعلم أن في النار قوة تقتضي التسخين، وفي الماء قوة تقتضي التبريد وكذلك في العين قوة تقتضي الإبصار، وفي اللسان قوة تقتضي الذوق. ويثبتون «الطبيعة» التي تسمى «الغَرِيزة» و «النَّحِيزة» و «الخُلق» و «العادة» ونحو ذلك من الأسماء . ولهذا كان السلف كأحمد بن حنبل والحارث المُحاسِبي وغيرهما يقولون: «العقل غريزة». وأما نُفاة «الطبائع » فليس «العقلُ» عندهم إلا مجرد العلم، كما هو قول أبي الحسن الأشعري (العقل عند الأشعري مجرد العلم، ووافقه آخرون)، والقاضي أبي بكر، والقاضي أبي يعلى، وابن عقيل، وأبي الخطاب، والقاضي أبي بكر بن العربي وغيرهم. وإن كان بعض هؤلاء قد يختلف كلامهم، فيثبتون في موضع آخر «الغرائز» و «الأسباب»، كما هو مذهب الفقهاء والجمهور.
The deniers of causation are those who do not affirm 'causes' and 'effective causes' among the theologians, like Jahm and his followers in that, such as Abu al-Hasan and his followers. They consider that what is known is the conjunction of one of the two things with the other purely by the will of God, the Powerful and Willing, without one being a cause of the other nor producing it. As for the majority of rational people among Muslims and non-Muslims, and the Ahl al-Sunna among the theologians, jurists, hadith scholars, and sufis, and also the non-Ahl al-Sunna like the Mu'tazila and others—they affirm 'causes' and say: just as the conjunction of one with the other is known, so it is known that in fire there is a power that requires heating, in water a power that requires cooling, and likewise in the eye a power that requires sight, and in the tongue a power that requires taste. And they affirm 'nature', which is called 'instinct', 'innate disposition', 'character', 'habit', and similar names. For this reason, the early Muslims like Ahmad ibn Hanbal, al-Harith al-Muhasibi, and others used to say: 'Reason is an instinct.' As for the deniers of 'natures', for them 'reason' is nothing but mere knowledge, as is the saying of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (reason according to al-Ash'ari is mere knowledge, and others agreed with him), and al-Qadi Abu Bakr, al-Qadi Abu Ya'la, Ibn 'Aqil, Abu al-Khattab, al-Qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-'Arabi, and others. However, some of these may have varying statements, so they affirm in another place 'instincts' and 'causes', as is the school of the jurists and the majority.

The Quranic Pairing of Senses and Heart
Ibn Taymiyya cites Quranic verses showing Allah pairs hearing, sight, and heart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the recurring Quranic pattern of pairing hearing, sight, and heart, and explain its epistemological significance in Ibn Taymiyya's critique of rationalism.

Core Idea
In the Quran, knowledge requires hearing, sight, and heart working together—not reason alone.

This passage is an epistemological digression by Ibn Taymiyya. He does not employ Greek logical forms; instead, he turns to the Quran to demonstrate that Allah consistently pairs the external senses—hearing and sight—with the internal faculty of the heart (al-fu'ad). By surveying multiple verses, he shows that genuine knowledge arises from the integration of sensory perception and reflection, not from a disembodied intellect. This serves as a fundament for his broader critique of rationalist theologians who claim that reason alone can attain truth independent of revelation and sensory experience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recurring triad
In each verse cited, locate the three terms: hearing (al-sam'), sight (al-basar), and heart (al-fu'ad). Notice that they are always mentioned together, as in 'Indeed the hearing, the sight, and the heart – all of those will be questioned' (al-Isra 17:36).
The heart may appear as 'af'ida' (plural) in some verses.
2
Determine the function assigned to each faculty
In al-Isra 17:36, the faculties are objects of accountability. In al-Nahl 16:78, they are gifts for gratitude. In al-A'raf 7:179, they are faculties that can be neglected. This shows they are not mere biological features but morally significant tools for knowledge.
3
Observe what happens when faculties are blocked
Verses like al-Baqarah 2:7 describe sealing of the heart, hearing, and sight, leading to spiritual blindness. Al-Isra 17:45-46 speaks of coverings over hearts and deafness in ears. This indicates interdependence: if one faculty is impaired, the others become ineffective for guidance.
4
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological claim
By showing this consistent Quranic pairing, Ibn Taymiyya argues that knowledge—especially of the divine—requires both sensory input (hearing and sight) and rational comprehension (heart). He rejects the rationalist notion that intellect operates independently of the senses or revelation.
This is a direct challenge to the 'aql-centered epistemology of some philosophers and theologians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا يقرن الله بينهما وبين الفؤاد في مواضع كقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ السَّمْعَ وَالْبَصَرَ وَالْفُؤَادَ كُلُّ أُوْلَئِكَ كَانَ عَنْهُ مَسْئُولًا﴾ [سورة الإسراء: ٣٦] وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّهُ أَخْرَجَكُم مِّن بُطُونِ أُمَّهَاتِكُمْ لَا تَعْلَمُونَ شَيْئًا وَجَعَلَ لَكُمُ السَّمْعَ وَالْأَبْصَارَ وَالْأَفْئِدَةَ لَعَلَّكُمْ تَشْكُرُونَ﴾ [سورة النحل: ٧٨]. وقال: ﴿وَلَقَدْ ذَرَأْنَا لِجَهَنَّمَ كَثِيرًا مِّنَ الْجِنِّ وَالْإِنسِ هُمْ قُلُوبٌ لَا يَفْقَهُونَ بِهَا وَلَهُمْ أَعْيُنٌ لَا يُبْصِرُونَ بِهَا وَلَهُمْ آذَانٌ لَا يَسْمَعُونَ بِهَا أُولَئِكَ كَالْأَنْعَامِ بَلْ هُمْ أَضَلُّ أُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْغَافِلُونَ﴾ [سورة الأعراف: ١٧٩]. وقال: ﴿وَجَعَلْنَا لَهُمْ سَمْعًا وَأَبْصَارًا وَأَفْئِدَةً فَمَا أَغْنَى عَنْهُمْ سَمْعُهُمْ وَلَا أَبْصَارُهُمْ وَلَا أَفْئِدَتُهُم مِّن شَيْءٍ إِذْ كَانُوا يَجْحَدُونَ بِآيَاتِ اللَّهِ وَحَاقَ بِهِم مَّا كَانُوا بِهِ يَسْتَهْزِئُونَ﴾ [سورة الأحقاف: ٢٦]. وقال تعالى: ﴿وَخَتَمَ اللَّهُ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ وَعَلَى سَمْعِهِمْ وَعَلَى أَبْصَارِهِمْ غِشَاوَةٌ﴾ [سورة البقرة: ٧]. وقال تعالى: ﴿صُمٌّ بُكْمٌ عُمْيٌ فَهُمْ لَا يَرْجِعُونَ﴾ [سورة البقرة: ١٨] في حق المنافقين. وقال في حق الكافرين: ﴿فَهُمْ لَا يَعْقِلُونَ﴾ [سورة البقرة: ١٧١]. وقال تعالى: ﴿وَقَالُوا قُلُوبُنَا فِي أَكِنَّةٍ مِمَّا تَدْعُونَا إِلَيْهِ وَفِي آذَانِنَا وَقْرٌ وَمِنْ بَيْنِنَا وَبَيْنَكَ حِجَابٌ﴾ [سورة فصلت: ٥]. وقال تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا قَرَأْتَ الْقُرْآنَ جَعَلْنَا بَيْنَكَ وَبَيْنَ الَّذِينَ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ بِالْآخِرَةِ حِجَابًا مَسْتُورًا * وَجَعَلْنَا عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ أَكِنَّةً أَنْ يَفْقَهُوهُ وَفِي آذَانِهِمْ وَقْرًا وَإِذَا ذَكَرْتَ رَبَّكَ فِي الْقُرْآنِ وَحْدَهُ وَلَّوْا عَلَى أَدْبَارِهِمْ نُفُورًا﴾ [سورة الإسراء: ٤٥-٤٦]. وَنَظَائِرُ هَذَا مُتَعَدِّدَةٌ.
And for this reason Allah pairs them (hearing and sight) with the heart in places, such as His saying: 'Indeed the hearing, the sight, and the heart – all of those will be questioned.' And His saying: 'And Allah brought you out from the wombs of your mothers not knowing a thing, and He made for you hearing and sight and hearts that you might be grateful.' And He said: 'And We have created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind; they have hearts with which they do not understand, and they have eyes with which they do not see, and they have ears with which they do not hear. Those are like livestock; rather, they are more astray. Those are the heedless.' And He said: 'And We gave them hearing and sight and hearts; but their hearing and sight and hearts availed them nothing when they used to deny the signs of Allah, and they were enveloped by what they used to mock.' And He said: 'Allah has set a seal upon their hearts and upon their hearing, and over their vision is a veil.' And He said: 'They are deaf, dumb, and blind, so they do not return,' concerning the hypocrites. And He said concerning the disbelievers: 'So they do not reason.' And He said: 'And they say: Our hearts are in coverings from that to which you invite us, and in our ears is deafness, and between us and you is a barrier.' And He said: 'And when you recite the Quran, We place between you and those who do not believe in the Hereafter a concealed barrier; and We place over their hearts coverings lest they understand it, and in their ears deafness; and when you mention your Lord alone in the Quran, they turn back in aversion.' And similar instances are numerous.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological digression. Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates through a series of Quranic verses that Allah consistently pairs the senses of hearing and sight with the heart (al-fu'ad). The purpose is to challenge the rationalist view that reason ('aql) operates independently of sensory input. He begins by citing verse 36 of Surah al-Isra', which lists hearing, sight, and heart together as objects of accountability. Then verse 78 of Surah al-Nahl shows that Allah grants these faculties so that we may be grateful, implying their interdependence. The verse from Surah al-A'raf (179) emphasizes that those who do not use these faculties are worse than cattle. Next, Surah al-Ahqaf (26) shows that even when given these faculties, denial of divine signs renders them useless. Surah al-Baqarah (7) speaks of sealing hearts, hearing, and sight, indicating that when the heart is sealed, the senses become useless for guidance. The remaining verses (al-Baqarah 18, 171; Fussilat 5; al-Isra' 45-46) further emphasize the link between sensory perception and understanding. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that such pairings are numerous in the Quran, reinforcing his point that knowledge—especially of the divine—requires both sensory input and rational comprehension working together.

السمع
البصر

Sharing Knowledge: Specific Instance vs. Type
Ibn Taymiyya refines the notion of knowledge sharing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sharing in the specific individual object and sharing in the type, and apply this distinction to evaluate whether a given piece of knowledge can be shared among individuals.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not purely private; it can be shared either in the specific instance or in the type.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' view that experiential and transmitted knowledge are exclusive to the individual. He introduces a crucial distinction that rescues the social epistemology of knowledge: we can share knowledge of the same particular object, or we can share knowledge of the type even if each person experiences a different instance. This distinction is key to understanding how mutawatirat and tajribiyyat can serve as evidence for others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Read the first sentence: the logicians made 'tajribiyyat' and 'mutawatirat' exclusive to those who have them, so they cannot be used as evidence against others. This is the target Ibn Taymiyya refutes.
The claim implies that knowledge is private and non-shareable.
2
Recognize Ibn Taymiyya's response
He says these matters involve both exclusivity and sharing, just like sensations. Then he introduces the key distinction: sharing can be either in the specific individual object (ayn al-malum al-mudrak) or in its type (naw'). Look for the examples: sun and moon for sharing in the specific; hunger, thirst, pleasure, pain for sharing in the type.
The examples illustrate how the same sun is seen by many (ayn), but each person's hunger is distinct yet they share the genus.
3
Apply the distinction to new cases
Given a piece of knowledge, decide: Is it sharable in the specific instance (the exact same object) or only in the type? For example, the taste of a specific fruit only you have eaten is sharable in type (others know the genus 'taste of that fruit' through report) but not in the specific instance (they did not taste that exact fruit).
This step trains you to use the distinction independently.
4
Interpret the significance for mutawatirat
Ibn Taymiyya ends by noting that many objects are known only by some through sight, but others know them through report (khabar), and the report often has more participants. This shows that mutawatirat and tajribiyyat can be shared across individuals, refuting the logicians' exclusivity claim.
Thus, even if a specific event is witnessed by few, the report can transmit knowledge of the type to many.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمتواترات
والمقصود أنهم جعلوا «المجربات» و«المتواترات» مما يختص به من حصل له ذلك، فلا يصلح أن يحتج به على غيره. وهذه قد يحصل فيها اختصاص واشتراك، كما أن «الحسيات» كذلك قد يحصل فيها اختصاص واشتراك.
المعلومات
وأيضاً فالاشتراك قد يكون في عين المعلوم المدرك، وقد يكون في نوعه. فالأول كاشتراك الناس في رؤية الشمس والقمر وغيرهما، والثاني كاشتراكهم في معرفة الجوع والعطش والريّ والشبع واللذة والألم، فإن المعين الذي ذاقه هذا الشخص ليس هو المعين الذي ذاقه هذا، إذ كل إنسان يذوق ما في باطنه، ولكن يشترك الناس في معرفة جنس ذلك.
وما يسمعونه من الرعد وما يرونه من البرق يشترك أهل المكان الواحد في رؤيته وسماعه، ويشترك الناس في النوع، إذ الرعد والبرق الذي يحصل في زمان ومكان يكون غير ما يحصل في زمان آخر ومكان آخر.
ومن المحسوسات المعروفة بالرؤية أنواع كثيرة من الحيوان والنبات وغير ذلك، يوجد ببعض البلاد دون بعض، فتكون مشهورة مرئية لمن رآها دون سائر الناس، فإنهم إنما يعلمون ذلك بـ «الخبر». وذلك «الخبر» قد يكون المشتركون فيه أكثر من المشتركين في «الرؤية».
And the mutawatirat (widely transmitted reports)
What is meant is that they [the logicians] have made 'tajribiyat' (experiential matters) and 'mutawatirat' exclusive to whoever has obtained them, so that they cannot be used as evidence against others. But these [matters] may involve both exclusivity and sharing, just as 'sensations' also may involve exclusivity and sharing.
Information
Also, sharing may be in the specific individual object (madrak) or in its type. The first is like people sharing in seeing the sun and the moon and others. The second is like their sharing in knowledge of hunger, thirst, satiety, pleasure, and pain; for the specific thing that this person tasted is not the specific thing that another tasted, since each person tastes what is inside him, but people share in knowing the genus of that.
And what they hear of thunder and what they see of lightning: the inhabitants of a single place share in seeing and hearing it, and people share in the type, since the thunder and lightning that occur in a certain time and place are different from what occurs in another time and place.
And among the sensible things known by sight are many types of animals, plants, and others that exist in some countries but not in others, so they are well-known and seen by those who have seen them, but not by the rest of people; for they only know that through report (khabar). And that report may have more participants than the participants in the sight.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the logicians' position on mutawatirat and tajribiyyat. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating the logicians' claim: that experiential knowledge (tajribiyyat) and widely transmitted reports (mutawatirat) are exclusive to those who have directly experienced or heard them, and thus cannot be used as evidence against others. He then refutes this by arguing that such knowledge actually involves both exclusivity (ikhtisas) and sharing (ishtirak), just like sensory perceptions. He introduces a crucial distinction: sharing can be either in the specific individual object (ayn al-malum al-mudrak) or in the type (naw') of the object. For example, every person who sees the sun sees the same specific sun (sharing in the particular). But for internal sensations like hunger, each person experiences their own specific hunger, yet they share knowledge of the genus of hunger. Similarly, people in one place share the same thunderclap, while people everywhere share knowledge of the type of phenomenon called thunder. Finally, he notes that many sensory objects (e.g., unique animals or plants) are known only by those who have seen them, but others can know them through report (khabar), and often the number of participants in the report exceeds those in the original sight. This distinction avoids the logicians' charge that such knowledge is purely private.

Key Terms
المتواترات
المجربات
الاشتراك في العين
الاشتراك في النوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does Ibn Taymiyya refute the claim that 'tajribiyyat' and 'mutawatirat' are exclusive to individuals?
A) He argues that all knowledge is inherently shared.
B) He distinguishes between sharing in the specific individual object and sharing in the type.
C) He denies the existence of exclusive knowledge altogether.
D) He claims that logicians never truly understood these concepts.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya rescues the shareability of knowledge by distinguishing between sharing in the specific instance and sharing in the type, refuting the claim that experiential and transmitted knowledge are purely private.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'I know what it feels like to be thirsty because I have been thirsty.' Another person also knows what thirst feels like. Using Ibn Taymiyya's distinction, explain in what sense the two persons share knowledge of thirst (in 'ayn or in naw')?
?
Give an example from Islamic history or fiqh where knowledge of a specific event (e.g., the Battle of Badr) is known to many through both direct sight (for those present) and through reports (for later generations). How does Ibn Taymiyya's distinction apply?

Philosophers' Shirk Worse than Jahiliyyah Shirk
Ibn Taymiyya compares philosophers' shirk to Jahiliyyah and Christian shirk, deeming it worse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key claims of the philosophers regarding angels and intellects, and explain why Ibn Taymiyya considers their shirk worse than that of the Jahiliyyah Arabs and Christians.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya ranks philosophical shirk as the worst because it attributes lordship and creation to a created intellect.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya draws a sharp contrast between the beliefs of the philosophical tradition (especially the ten intellects and active intellect) and the polytheism of the pre-Islamic Arabs and Christians. While the Arabs and Christians acknowledged certain beings as created or divine in a limited sense, the philosophers assert that the active intellect is the lord and creator of the sublunar world — an unprecedented claim that Ibn Taymiyya deems the most severe form of shirk. This lesson will dissect his critique and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups compared
Ibn Taymiyya compares the philosophers to three groups: polytheist Arabs, Jews, and Christians. Note the specific beliefs of each group about angels mentioned in the passage.
The Arabs acknowledged angels as created but some said they were daughters of God; Christians said the Messiah is son of God.
2
Extract the philosophical claims about angels
According to philosophers who accept prophethood, angels are the ten intellects. They are eternal and pre-eternal. The active intellect is considered the lord of everything besides God and the creator of the sublunar world.
This is based on the emanationist cosmology of al-Farabi and Ibn Sina.
3
Find the Quranic evidence cited
Ibn Taymiyya cites three Quranic verses (Saba 22-23, An-Najm 26, Al-Anbiya 28) that negate any share in divinity or independent intercession for beings called upon besides Allah. He uses these to frame the critique.
These verses establish that nothing besides God has any independent power or lordship.
4
Analyze the comparative severity
Ibn Taymiyya states that no Jew, Christian, or Arab polytheist ever claimed that a single angel is the lord of the entire world. The philosophers' claim about the active intellect is thus unprecedented and worse.
The key phrase: 'This is also disbelief that none of the disbelievers of the People of the Book and the polytheist Arabs reached.'
5
Synthesize Ibn Taymiyya's argument
Summarize why philosophical shirk is more heinous: it combines attribution of lordship (rububiyya) and creation (khalq) to a created being, exceeding the shirk of previous groups.

Source Text

المقام الثالث - شرك الفلاسفة أشنع من شرك الجاهلية
وقال تعالى: ﴿ قُلِ ادْعُوا الَّذِينَ زَعَمْتُم مِّن دُونِ اللَّهِ لَا يَمْلِكُونَ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَلَا فِي الْأَرْضِ وَمَا لَهُمْ فِيهِمَا مِن شِرك وَمَا لَهُرُ مِنْهُم مِّن ظَهِيرِ الله وَلَا تَنفَعُ الشَّفَعَةُ عِندَهُ إِلَّا لِمَنْ أَيْن لهم ﴾ [سورة سبأ : ٢٢ - ٢٣] .
وقال تعالى: وَكَم مِّن مَّلَكٍ فِي السَّمَوَاتِ لَا تُغْنِي شَفَعَتُهُمْ شَيْئًا إِلَّا مِنْ بَعْدِ أَن يَأْذَنَ اللَّهُ لِمَن يَشَاءُ وَيَرْضَى (1) [سورة النجم : ٢٦] .
وقال تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا يَشْفَعُونَ إِلَّا لِمَنِ ارْتَضَى وَهُم مِّنْ خَشْيَتِهِ، مُشْفِقُونَ ﴾ [سورة الأنساء : ٢٨].
والعرب كانوا - مع شركهم وكفرهم - يقولون : إن الملائكة مخلوقون. وكان «الملائكة» من يقول منهم إن الملائكة بناتُ الله يقولون أيضاً «إنهم مُحدّثون»، ويقولون: «إنه صاهَرَ إلى الجنّ ، فولدت له الملائكة .
وقولهم من جنس قول النصارى في أن المسيحَ ابنُ الله ، مع أن مريم أنه ولهذا قرن سبحانه بين هؤلاء وهؤلاء (۱) .
وقول هؤلاء الفلاسفة شرّ من قول هؤلاء كلهم .
فإن الملائكة عند من آمن بالنبوات منهم هي العقول العشرة، وتلك عندهم الملائكة قديمة أزلية و العقل ربّ كل ما سوى الرب عندهم. وهذا لم يقل مثله أحد من اليهود والنصارى ومشركي العرب - لم يقل أحد إن ملكاً من الملائكة ربّ العالم كله عند الفلاسفة .
ويقولون : إن العقل الفعّال مُبدع لما تحت فلك القمر. وهذا أيضاً كفر لم يصل إليه أحد من كفّار أهل الكتاب ومشركي العرب .
The Third Station - The shirk of the philosophers is more heinous than the shirk of the Jahiliyyah. And He, exalted, said: 'Say, Call upon those you claim besides Allah. They do not possess an atom's weight in the heavens or the earth, nor have they any share in them, nor is there for Him from among them any assistant. And intercession does not benefit with Him except for whom He permits.' [Saba 22-23]. And He said: 'And how many angels are in the heavens whose intercession avails nothing except after Allah gives permission to whom He wills and approves.' [An-Najm 26]. And He said: 'And they do not intercede except for whom He approves, and they are, from fear of Him, apprehensive.' [Al-Anbiya 28]. The Arabs, despite their shirk and disbelief, used to say that the angels are created. Among those who said the angels are daughters of God, they also said they are originated, and they said that He (God) intermarried with the jinn and the angels were born to Him. Their saying is of the same kind as the Christians' saying that the Messiah is the son of God, even though Mary bore him. That is why He, glorified, linked these with those. And the saying of these philosophers is worse than the saying of all of them. For the angels, according to those among them who believe in prophethood, are the ten intellects, and according to them those angels are eternal and pre-eternal, and the intellect is the lord of everything besides the Lord according to them. No one from among the Jews, Christians, and polytheist Arabs ever said that an angel is the lord of the entire world according to the philosophers. And they say that the active intellect is the creator of what is under the sphere of the moon. This is also disbelief that none of the disbelievers of the People of the Book and the polytheist Arabs reached.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a critical comparison by Ibn Taymiyya, establishing that the philosophical doctrine regarding angels is more severe than the shirk of the pre-Islamic Arabs and Christians. He begins by citing Quranic verses that negate any share in divinity or independent intercession for beings worshipped besides Allah. Then he describes the beliefs of the Arab polytheists: they acknowledged angels as created beings, but some claimed they were daughters of God, and they attributed a kind of divine kinship. He then notes that Christian belief in the divinity of Christ is similar, and points out that the Quran often links these two groups together. He then asserts that the philosophers' view is worse than these. He explains that according to those philosophers who accept prophethood (like Ibn Sina), the angels are actually the ten intellects, which are eternal and pre-eternal. Moreover, they consider the intellect (specifically the active intellect) to be the lord of everything besides God. This is a claim that no Jew, Christian, or Arab polytheist ever made—that a particular angel or intellect is the lord of the entire world. He further states that they consider the active intellect to be the creator of everything under the moon. This is an unprecedented form of disbelief. The passage thus sets up the comparative severity of philosophical shirk.

العقل الفعّال
الشرك

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's ranking of shirk is based on the specificity and severity of the association, with philosophical emanationism being the most extreme because it attributes lordship and creation to a single created intellect.

Philosophers' Intercession Doctrine Refuted
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the philosophers' concept of shafa'a based on their denial of divine will and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the philosophical basis for intercession (shafa'a) and its theological implications for divine agency.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya exposes how philosophers strip divine agency from intercession by denying God's will, power, and knowledge of particulars.

In this passage from the 'Radd al-Mantiqiyyin', Ibn Taymiyya attacks the philosophical reinterpretation of shafa'a (intercession) championed by Ibn Sina and his followers. He argues that this concept is built on a gravely flawed theology: that God does not act with will and power, does not know particulars, and cannot change the world. Instead, the world is a necessary emanation from God, leaving no room for the Islamic understanding of intercession as a petitionary prayer granted by divine permission. Understanding this critique is vital for appreciating the Islamic refutation of deterministic philosophies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target groups
Read the opening: who does Ibn Taymiyya accuse of holding this false view of intercession? Note the mention of Ibn Sina, the author of 'al-Madnun bihi', Qarmatians, Batiniyya, and others. This shows the scope of his critique.
The author of 'al-Madnun bihi' is likely al-Ghazali, but Ibn Taymiyya includes him among the philosophers here.
2
Extract the philosophical premises
Identify the theological claims that the philosophers use to base their concept of intercession. Look for the negative statements: 'the Lord does not act by His will and power', 'He is not knowledgeable of particulars', 'He cannot change the world'. These are the core premises.
3
Understand the emanational model
Find the phrase 'the world is an emanation that emanated from Him without His will, power, and knowledge'. This is the philosophical alternative to creation by will. Realize how this changes the nature of intercession: from a personal divine response to a natural emanation process.
The term 'fayd' (emanation) is key; it implies necessity and impersonality.
4
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's critique
How does he connect these premises to the concept of intercession? He states they 'make intercession based on what they believe'. The critique is that by denying divine agency, the philosophers cannot logically maintain the Islamic concept of intercession as a willed act of God. Instead, it becomes a deterministic emanation.
Ibn Taymiyya's method is to show that the philosophical system leads to conclusions incompatible with revealed religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقولون ما ذكره ابن سينا وأتباعه كصاحب (۱) «الكتب المضنون بها على غير أهلها، ومن وافقهم من القرامطة والباطنية من الملاحدة والجُهَّال الذين دخلوا عند الفلاسفة في الصوفية وأهل الكلام، كأهل وحدة الوجود وغيرهم، يجعلون «الشفاعة» مبنية على ما يعتقدونه من أن الرب لا يفعل بمشيئته وقدرته، وليس عالماً بـ «الجزئيات»، ولا يقدر أن يغير العالم، بل العالم فيض فاض عنه بغير مشيئته وقدرته وعلمه .
And they say what Ibn Sina and his followers mentioned, like the author of 'The books kept from those not fit for them', and those who agreed with them from the Qarmatians, Batiniyya, heretics, and ignorant ones who entered among the philosophers into Sufism and kalam, like the people of the unity of existence and others, they make 'intercession' based on what they believe: that the Lord does not act by His will and power, and He is not knowledgeable of particulars, and He cannot change the world. Rather, the world is an emanation that emanated from Him without His will, power, and knowledge.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophical basis for intercession (shafa'a). He attributes this view to Ibn Sina and his followers, specifically mentioning the author of 'al-Madnun bihi' (which is a reference to al-Ghazali's controversial book). He then includes various heretical groups—Qarmatians, Batiniyya, and those who mix philosophical ideas into Sufism and kalam, such as the proponents of the unity of existence (wahdat al-wujud). He states that these groups base their concept of intercession on a theological framework that denies God's will, power, and knowledge of particulars. They believe that God does not act with will and power, and that He does not know particular events. Consequently, He cannot change the world. Instead, they claim the world is a necessary emanation from God, not a creation by His will. This emanationist view implies that intercession is not a petitionary prayer answered by God's free choice, but rather a kind of spiritual influence or emanation that occurs naturally when a person achieves closeness to the divine. Ibn Taymiyya's critique is that this framework removes divine agency and replaces it with a deterministic emanation, thus distorting the Islamic understanding of intercession as a gift from God granted by His permission to whom He wills.

Key Terms
الشفاعة
فيض
الجزئيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Intercession is granted by God to the prophets as a reward.
D) Angels intercede on behalf of humans by God's command.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique reveals that the philosophers' concept of intercession is logically dependent on a theology that denies God's will, power, and knowledge of particulars, thus undermining divine agency.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Philosophical Intercession
Critique of philosophers' emanation-based intercession and comparison with Arab pagans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key elements of the philosophers' intercession doctrine as critiqued by Ibn Taymiyya and explain why he considers it worse than Arab paganism.

Core Idea
The philosophers' intercession denies God's will and knowledge, making their shirk worse than that of Arab pagans.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets the philosophical concept of intercession based on emanation (fayd). He contrasts it with the shirk of pre-Islamic Arabs, arguing that the philosophers' version is more severe because it negates God's will, power, and knowledge. To follow his argument, you must understand both the philosophical doctrine and the theological principles he invokes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophical intercession model
Ibn Taymiyya describes how philosophers like Ibn Sina conceive of intercession: the seeker connects to a higher substance (intellect, soul, planet), which then receives divine emanation and passes it on. Note the example of sunlight reflecting off a mirror.
The Active Intellect is the originator of the sublunar world, a key Neoplatonic concept.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's theological objections
He objects that this model implies God does not act by His will and power, does not know particulars, and cannot change the world. Instead, the world is a necessary emanation.
These are fundamental Islamic attributes of God.
3
Compare with Arab polytheists
Ibn Taymiyya notes that the pagan Arabs, while worshipping intercessors, affirmed that God knows, wills, and creates. The philosophers deny these very attributes.
This makes the philosophers' disbelief 'greater' in his assessment.
4
Evaluate the conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the philosophers' shirk is worse because it denies core divine attributes. Consider whether his comparison is logically sound and what implications it has for evaluating other forms of intercession.
The critique is based on degrees of deviation from tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقولون : إن العقل الفعّال مُبدع لما تحت فلك القمر. وهذا أيضاً كفر لم يصل إليه أحد من كفّار أهل الكتاب ومشركي العرب . وهؤلاء يقولون ما ذكره ابن سينا وأتباعه كصاحب «الكتب المضنون بها على غير أهلها، ومن وافقهم من القرامطة والباطنية من الملاحدة والجُهَّال الذين دخلوا عند الفلاسفة في الصوفية وأهل الكلام، كأهل وحدة الوجود وغيرهم، يجعلون «الشفاعة» مبنية على ما يعتقدونه من أن الرب لا يفعل بمشيئته وقدرته، وليس عالماً بـ «الجزئيات»، ولا يقدر أن يغير العالم، بل العالم فيض فاض عنه بغير مشيئته وقدرته وعلمه .

المقام الثالث - الشفاعة عند الفلاسفة

فيقولون : إذا توجه المستشفع إلى من يعظمه من الجواهر العالية كـ «العقول» و «النفوس والكواكب والشمس والقمر، أو إلى النفوس المُفَارِقة مثل بعض الصالحين، فإنه يتصل بذلك المعظم المستشفَع به. فإذا فاض على ذلك ما يفيض من جهة الربّ فاض على هذا المستشفع من جهة شفيعه عندهم ويمثلونه بالشمس إذا طلعت على مرآة، فانعكس الشُّعاعُ الذي على المرآة على تمثيل الشفاعة موضع آخر فأشرق بذلك الشعاع. فذلك الشعاع حَصَل له بمقابلة المرآة، وحصل للمرآة بمقابلة الشمس فهذا الداعي المستشفع إذا توجه إلى شفيعه أشرق عليه من جهته مقصود الشفاعة، وذلك الشفيع يشرق عليه من جهة الحق . ولهذا يرى هؤلاء دعاء الموتى عند القبور وغير القبور، ويتوجهون إليهم، ويستعينون بهم، ويقولون إن أرواحنا إذا توجهت إلى روح المقبور في القبور اتصلت به ففاضت عليها المقاصد من جهته . وكثير منهم ومن غيرهم من الجهال يرون أن الصلوة والدعاء عند قبور الأنبياء والصالحين من أهل البيت وغيرهم أفضلُ من الصلوات الخمس والدعاء في المساجد، وأفضلُ من حج البيت العتيق . ومعلوم أن كفر هؤلاء بما يقولونه في الشفعاء أعظمُ من كفر مشركي العرب بما قالوه فيهم . لأن كلا الطائفتين عَبدوا من دون الله ما لا يضرهم ولا ينفعهم، وقالوا : هؤلاء شُفَعاؤنا عند الله . لكن العرب أقروا بأن الله عالم بهم قادر عليهم، يخلق بمشيئته و قدرته وقالوا : إن هؤلاء ينفعوننا بدعائهم لنا . وأما مشركوا الفلاسفة كما ذكره ابن سينا ومن اتبعه فيقولون : إن من يُسْتَشْفَعُ به في الشفعاء
And they say: The Active Intellect is the originator of what is under the sphere of the moon. This also is disbelief that none of the disbelievers of the People of the Book and the Arab polytheists have reached. And these people say what Ibn Sina and his followers mentioned, like the author of 'the books withheld from those not worthy of them,' and those who agreed with them from the Qarmatians and Batiniyyah of the heretics and the ignorant who entered among the philosophers in Sufism and the theologians, like the people of the unity of existence and others, they make 'intercession' based on what they believe: that the Lord does not act by His will and power, and He is not knowledgeable of 'particulars,' and He cannot change the world, rather the world is an emanation flowing from Him without His will, power, and knowledge.

Third Station - Intercession according to the philosophers

They say: When the seeker of intercession turns to what he magnifies from the high substances, like the 'intellects' and 'souls' and planets and sun and moon, or to the separate souls like some righteous people, then he connects with that magnified intercessor. So when what flows from the Lord flows upon that [magnified being], it flows upon this seeker of intercession from his intercessor according to them. And they give the example of the sun when it rises on a mirror, and the ray that is on the mirror reflects upon another place of the likeness of intercession and shines with that ray. So that ray occurred to it by facing the mirror, and occurred to the mirror by facing the sun. So this supplicant seeker of intercession, when he turns to his intercessor, the purpose of intercession shines upon him from his direction, and that intercessor shines upon him from the direction of the Truth. And therefore these people see the supplication of the dead at graves and elsewhere, and they turn to them, and seek help from them, and they say that our souls, when they turn to the soul of the buried one in the graves, connect with him, and then the purposes flow upon them from his direction. And many of them and others from the ignorant consider that prayer and supplication at the graves of the prophets and righteous people from the Ahl al-Bayt and others are better than the five daily prayers and supplication in the mosques, and better than the pilgrimage to the Ancient House. And it is known that the disbelief of these people in what they say about intercessors is greater than the disbelief of the Arab polytheists in what they said about them. Because both groups worshiped besides God what harms them not nor benefits them, and said: 'These are our intercessors with God.' But the Arabs acknowledged that God is knowledgeable of them, powerful over them, He creates by His will and power, and they said: 'These benefit us by their supplication for us.' And as for the polytheist philosophers, as Ibn Sina and his followers mentioned, they say: 'The one through whom intercession is sought is among the intercessors...'
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophers' concept of intercession (shafa'a), particularly as espoused by Ibn Sina and his followers. He begins by asserting that the philosophers' claim that the Active Intellect creates the sublunar world is an unprecedented disbelief, exceeding that of the People of the Book or Arab pagans. He then explains that these philosophers base intercession on a defective theological framework: they deny that God acts by His will and power, that He knows particulars, and that He can change the world. Instead, they hold that the world is a necessary emanation from God.

Ibn Taymiyya then describes the philosophical model of intercession (al-maqam al-thalith). According to them, a person seeking intercession (mustashfi') directs his attention to a higher substance, such as an intellect, soul, planet, or a separate soul like a saint. This connection causes the flow (fayd) from God to pass through the intercessor to the seeker. They illustrate this with the analogy of sunlight reflecting off a mirror onto another surface. The seeker is like the second surface, the intercessor is like the mirror, and God is like the sun. The intercession is thus a mediated emanation.

Ibn Taymiyya then notes that this view leads to practices such as praying to the dead at graves, seeking their help, and even considering grave visitation superior to obligatory prayers or Hajj. He condemns this as worse than the shirk of the pre-Islamic Arabs. While both groups take intercessors besides God, the Arabs at least affirmed God's knowledge, power, and will. The philosophers, by denying these attributes, commit a greater disbelief. The passage ends with a reference to Ibn Sina's statement about the intercessor, though the text is cut off.

Key points: Ibn Taymiyya refutes the emanationist-metaphysical basis of intercession, distinguishes between acceptable and unacceptable forms of intercession, and argues that the philosophers' shirk is more severe than that of the Arab polytheists because it denies God's attributes.

Key Terms
الشفاعة
العقل الفعّال
الفيض
المستشفع
الجواهر العالية

C) Because the philosophers reject the Qur'an.
