Philosophers' Mirror Analogy for Intercession
Ibn Taymiyya describes philosophers' emanative theory of intercession with mirror analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the philosophers' mirror analogy for intercession and evaluate its validity as an analogical argument.

Core Idea
The mirror analogy reduces intercession to a natural emanation, stripping it of supplication and divine will.

Ibn Taymiyya here reports the philosophers' theory of intercession, which they illustrate with a mirror analogy. This analogy is not just a poetic image; it functions as a logical model that shapes their entire understanding of how intercession works. By dissecting this analogy, you will learn to identify the logical structure of analogical arguments (tamthil) and see how a flawed analogy can lead to theological error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy's components
The analogy has three elements: the sun (source of light), the mirror (reflector), and the illuminated place (recipient). In the passage, they say: 'when the sun shines on a mirror, the ray reflects to another place and illuminates.' Identify these three elements in the Arabic.
2
Map the analogy to the theological claim
The philosophers map: God (the sun) -> intercessor (mirror) -> seeker (illuminated place). The 'flow' from God to intercessor and then to seeker is like light reflecting. Find in the passage where they say 'when what flows from the Lord flows upon that [intercessor], it flows upon this seeker.'
This mapping is essential to see how the analogy drives their theory.
3
Determine the type of analogical reasoning
This is an illustrative analogy (tamthil), not a strict legal qiyas. The 'illa (effective cause) is the unidirectional, natural transmission of light. The philosophers assume that spiritual emanation works the same way: automatically, without will or supplication.
Analogical arguments require that the 'illa is shared. Here, they assume it without proof.
4
Evaluate the analogy's validity
Is the analogy sound? In Islamic theology, intercession (shafa'a) is not a natural emanation but a supplication granted by God's permission. The analogy fails because it ignores the personal, volitional aspect of God's response. The passage hints at this by noting 'they consider supplicating the dead' as a consequence.
A valid analogy must preserve the relevant properties; here the crucial property of 'supplication by permission' is lost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الشفاعة عند الفلاسفة. فيقولون : إذا توجه المستشفع إلى من يعظمه من الجواهر العالية كالعقول والنفوس والكواكب والشمس والقمر، أو إلى النفوس المفارقة مثل بعض الصالحين، فإنه يتصل بذلك المعظم المستشفع به. فإذا فاض على ذلك ما يفيض من جهة الرب فاض على هذا المستشفع من جهة شفيعه. ويمثلونه بالشمس إذا طلعت على مرآة، فانعكس الشعاع الذي على المرآة على موضع آخر فأشرق بذلك الشعاع. فذلك الشعاع حصل له بمقابلة المرآة، وحصل للمرآة بمقابلة الشمس. فهذا الداعي المستشفع إذا توجه إلى شفيعه أشرق عليه من جهته مقصود الشفاعة، وذلك الشفيع يشرق عليه من جهة الحق. ولهذا يرى هؤلاء دعاء الموتى عند القبور وغير القبور، ويتوجهون إليهم، ويستعينون بهم، ويقولون إن أرواحنا إذا توجهت إلى روح المقبور في القبور اتصلت به ففاضت عليها المقاصد من جهته.
The third station - Intercession according to the philosophers. They say: when the seeker of intercession turns to one of the high jewels he magnifies, such as the intellects, souls, planets, sun, and moon, or to the separate souls like some of the righteous, then he connects to that magnified intercessor. So when what flows from the Lord flows upon that [intercessor], it flows upon this seeker from the direction of his intercessor. And they represent it by the sun when it shines on a mirror, so the ray that is on the mirror reflects to another place and illuminates with that ray. So that ray came to it by facing the mirror, and the mirror got it by facing the sun. So this suppliant seeker of intercession, when he turns to his intercessor, the purpose of intercession shines upon him from his direction, and that intercessor shines upon him from the direction of the Truth. For this, they consider supplicating the dead at graves and elsewhere, and turning to them, and seeking their help, and they say: when our souls turn to the soul of the buried one in the graves, they connect to it and the purposes flow upon them from his direction.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage exposes the philosophical theory of intercession. Ibn Taymiyya presents their view: the seeker connects to a high celestial being (intellects, souls, planets) or righteous souls. The intercessor receives emanation from God and passes it on. They use the mirror analogy: as sunlight reflects from a mirror to another place, so the spiritual light reflects from God to intercessor to seeker. This leads them to practices like praying to the dead at graves. The passage is critical because it sets up their false doctrine for refutation.

Key Terms
الشفاعة
المستشفع
الجواهر العالية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Sunlight reflecting from a mirror to another place
C) A king granting a request through a minister
D) A teacher instructing a student

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' mirror analogy reduces intercession to a natural emanation, which is the foundational error that Ibn Taymiyya later refutes.

Degrees of Kufr: Philosophers vs Arab Polytheists
Ibn Taymiyya argues that philosophers' disbelief is greater than Arab polytheists because they deny God's knowledge of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the degrees of disbelief between Arab polytheists and philosophers, identifying the theological errors that increase kufr according to Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
Denying God’s attributes makes disbelief worse than associating partners while affirming them.

Ibn Taymiyya often compares forms of disbelief to highlight the severity of certain theological errors. In this passage, he pits the Arab polytheists, who worshipped idols but affirmed God's knowledge and power, against the philosophers, who deny God's knowledge of particulars. Your task is to dissect this comparison and see why the philosophers' position is deemed worse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Locate the two groups Ibn Taymiyya compares: Arab polytheists (مشركو العرب) and philosopher polytheists (مشركو الفلاسفة). Note the common element: both worship intermediaries (شفعاء).
2
Extract the shared doctrine
Both groups say: 'These are our intercessors with Allah.' They worship beings that neither harm nor benefit. Identify this shared error.
3
Contrast the Arab affirmation
Find the attributes the Arabs affirmed: Allah knows them, is powerful over them, creates by His will. Also they believed intercessors supplicate to Allah for them.
4
Contrast the philosophical denial
Find what philosophers denied: Allah does not know particulars, cannot change anything, does not act by will. They also claim intercessors do not supplicate; rather, emanation flows impersonally.
5
Conclude the degree of kufr
Ibn Taymiyya concludes philosophers' kufr is greater because they strip Allah of essential attributes (knowledge, power, will), while Arabs affirmed them. Explain why this matters in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعلوم أن كفر هؤلاء بما يقولونه في الشفعاء أعظم من كفر مشركي العرب بما قالوه فيهم. لأن كلا الطائفتين عبدوا من دون الله ما لا يضرهم ولا ينفعهم، وقالوا: هؤلاء شفعاؤنا عند الله. لكن العرب أقروا بأن الله عالم بهم قادر عليهم، يخلق بمشيئته وقدرته، وقالوا: إن هؤلاء ينفعوننا بدعائهم لنا. وأما مشركوا الفلاسفة كما ذكره ابن سينا ومن اتبعه فيقولون: إن من يستشفع به لا يدعو الله لنا بشيء، والله لا يعلم دعاءنا ولا دعاءه، ولا يسمع نداءنا ولا نداءه، بل ولا يعرف بنا ولا نراه ولا يعرف به فإنا نحن من الجزئيات والله لا يعلم الجزئيات عندهم، ولا يقدر على تغيير شيء من العالم ولا يفعل بمشيئته. لكن قالوا: لكن نحن إذا توجهنا إلى هؤلاء بالدعاء لهم والسؤال منهم - بل وبالعبادة لهم - فاض علينا ما يفيض منهم، وفاض عليهم ما يفيض من جهة الله.
And it is known that the disbelief of these people in what they say about intercessors is greater than the disbelief of the Arab polytheists in what they said about them. Because both groups worshipped besides Allah what harms them not nor benefits them, and said: 'These are our intercessors with Allah.' But the Arabs acknowledged that Allah knows them, is powerful over them, creates by His will and power, and said: 'These benefit us by their supplication for us.' As for the polytheist philosophers, as mentioned by Ibn Sina and his followers, they say: the one sought for intercession does not supplicate Allah for us at all, and Allah does not know our supplication nor his supplication, nor does He hear our call nor his call; rather He does not know us and we do not see Him nor does He know through him, for we are among the particulars, and Allah does not know particulars according to them, nor is He able to change anything in the world, nor does He act by His will. But they said: when we turn to these by supplication to them and asking from them - rather by worshipping them - then what flows from them flows upon us, and what flows from Allah flows upon them.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage compares the disbelief (kufr) of the philosophers with that of the pre-Islamic Arab polytheists. Both groups worship intermediaries. However, the Arabs acknowledged Allah's attributes of knowledge, power, and will, while the philosophers (following Ibn Sina) deny that Allah knows particulars, can change the world, or acts by will. This makes the philosophers' kufr worse because they strip Allah of His essential attributes. The passage is critical: Ibn Taymiyya uses this comparison to highlight the severity of the philosophical position.

Key Terms
الجزئيات
مشركو العرب

Denying God's essential attributes makes disbelief more severe than associating partners while affirming those attributes.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Shahrastani and True Tawhid on Intermediaries
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes Shahrastani's false balancing and asserts the true Islamic position: no intermediaries in worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of al-Shahrastani's comparison of intermediaries and articulate the correct Islamic position on intermediaries in worship and revelation.

Core Idea
There are no intermediaries in worship; prophets only convey revelation—rejecting both the polytheist and the erroneous theologian.

Ibn Taymiyya here diagnoses a serious error: some theologians, in trying to refute the philosophers, end up denying scientific truths; others, like al-Shahrastani, mistakenly rank celestial spirits above prophets. This lesson unpacks his critique and his clarification of the Hanif position, which is essential for sound tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two errors
List the two mistaken approaches Ibn Taymiyya criticizes: (1) theologians denying scientific facts like the sphericity of the heaven, and (2) al-Shahrastani comparing celestial spirits to prophets and preferring the former.
2
Extract the heart of the critique
Why does Ibn Taymiyya call al-Shahrastani's comparison a 'grave error'? He sees it as a false balancing that elevates spirits above prophets, undermining the unique status of prophethood and tawhid.
3
Distinguish two types of mediation
Ibn Taymiyya clarifies: there are intermediaries in conveying revelation (angels and prophets) but no intermediaries in worship or direct supplication. Mixing these two is the root of shirk.
4
Recognize the Hanif foundation
The foundation is 'la ilaha illa Allah' and 'Muhammadun Rasul Allah'—pure monotheism without intermediaries in worship. Angels are messengers, not objects of worship.
5
Connect to consequences
Polytheists were misled by devils who mixed truth with falsehood. This shows why any mediation in worship leads to misguidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن طائفة من أهل الكلام يردون عليهم باطلهم بقول باطل، فيردون الباطل بفاسد، وإن كان أحدهما أكثر فسادا مثل إنكار كثير منهم لكثير من الأمور الرياضية، كاستدارة الفلك، وغير ذلك مما دل عليه الكتاب والسنة وآثار السلف مع دلالة العقل. أو يفعلون كما فعله الشهرستاني في الملل والنحل، حيث أخذ يذكر المفاضلة بين الأرواح العلوية وبين الأنبياء، ويجعل إثبات هذه وسائط أولى من تلك تفضيلا لأقوال الحنفاء على أقوال الصابئة. وهذا غلط عظيم. فإن الحنفاء لا يثبتون بين الله وبين مخلوقاته واسطة في عبادته وسؤاله، وإنما يثبتون الملائكة في الوسائط في تبليغ رسالاته. فأصل الحنفاء شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله وشهادة أن محمدا وغيره من الرسل رسل الله. وأما الوسائط التي يثبتها المشركون، فيجعلون الملائكة معبودين، وهذا كفر وضلال. وتوسط الملائكة بمعنى تبليغ رسالات الله، أو بمعنى أنهم يفعلون ما يفعلونه بإذن الله، مما اتفق عليه الحنفاء. ومعلوم أن المشركين من عبّاد الأصنام وغيرهم كانت الشياطين تضلهم، وتكلمهم، وتقضي لهم بعض حوائجهم، وتخبرهم بأمور غائبة عنهم. وكان للكهان شياطين تخبرهم وتأمرهم، وإن كان الكذب فيما يقولونه أكثر من الصدق.
Then, a group of theologians refute their falsehood with a false statement, so they refute falsehood with corrupt speech, even if one of them is more corrupt—like the denial by many of them of many mathematical matters, such as the sphericity of the heaven, and other things indicated by the Book, the Sunnah, and the effects of the Salaf, along with rational evidence. Or they do as al-Shahrastani did in Al-Milal wa al-Nihal, where he began to compare the higher spirits and the prophets, making the affirmation of these [spirits] as intermediaries preferable to those [prophets], preferring the statements of the Hanifs over the statements of the Sabians. This is a grave error. For the Hanifs do not affirm any intermediary between Allah and His creatures in worshipping Him and asking from Him; they only affirm angels as intermediaries in conveying His messages. So the foundation of the Hanifs is the testimony that there is no god but Allah and that Muhammad and other messengers are the messengers of Allah. As for the intermediaries that the polytheists affirm, they make angels objects of worship, and this is disbelief and misguidance. And the mediation of angels in the sense of conveying Allah's messages, or in the sense that they do what they do by Allah's permission, is agreed upon by the Hanifs. And it is known that the polytheists, idol-worshippers and others, were misled by devils, who spoke to them, fulfilled some of their needs, and informed them of hidden matters. And the soothsayers had devils that informed them and commanded them, even though falsehood in what they say is more than truth.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage criticizes two errors: one by theologians who deny sound scientific facts (like sphericity of heaven) in response to philosophers, and another by al-Shahrastani who ranked celestial spirits as preferable intermediaries to prophets. Ibn Taymiyya calls this a grave error. He then states the correct Islamic position: there is no intermediary in worship or supplication; only in conveying revelation. Angels are messengers, not objects of worship. He also notes that polytheists were often misled by devils who gave them some true information mixed with much falsehood. The passage is a critical refinement of both the philosophers' view and the incorrect response by some theologians.

Key Terms
الحنفاء
الوسائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
There is no intermediary in worship—only in revelation; this is the pure tawhid of the Hanifs.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Philosophical Reduction of Angels and Devils
Ibn Taymiyya refutes philosophers' reduction of angels and devils.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that angels and devils are real personal beings, not abstract forces, and evaluate its force against the philosophers' claims.

Core Idea
Angels and devils are real personal beings, not mere intellects or psychic forces.

Ibn Taymiyya here attacks a core philosophical doctrine: that angels are the ten intellects of Neoplatonic cosmology and devils are evil psychic forces. Using historical and observed phenomena (soothsayers, sorcery, grave-worshippers), he insists on the objective, personal reality of both angels and devils. This lesson shows how he combines empirical reports and religious necessity to refute a metaphysical reduction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophical doctrine under attack
The philosophers (Ibn Sina) claim angels are ten intellects and devils are evil psychic forces. What exactly is being reduced? Note the specific terms 'al-ʿuqūl al-ʿashara' and 'al-quwā al-khabītha'.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's empirical counter-evidence
He lists examples: devils speaking to polytheists, soothsayers, inciting sorcery, appearing as shaykhs. What pattern do these share? They involve personal interaction and agency, not impersonal forces.
3
Analyze his critique of the philosophers' ignorance
Ibn Sina's three causes (psychic, natural, celestial) ignore the greater agency of devils. Why does Ibn Taymiyya call this ignorance? Because they deny observable phenomena that do not fit their framework.
4
Assess the two grounds of refutation
He claims rational proofs and necessity of religion (darurat al-din) show the corruption. How do these two grounds work together? Rational proofs include empirical observation; religious necessity includes clear Quranic and prophetic teachings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعلوم أن المشركين من عُبّاد الأصنام وغيرهم كانت الشياطين تُضِلُّهم، فتكلّمهم، وتقضي لهم بعض حوائجهم، وتخبرهم بأمور غائبة عنهم. وكان للكهان شياطين تخبرهم وتأمرهم، وإن كان الكذب فيما يقولونه أكثر من الصدق. وهكذا المشركون في زماننا الذين يدعون غير الله كالشيوخ الغائبين والموتى، تتصور لهم الشياطين في صُوَر الشيوخ، حتى يظنوا أن الشيخ حَضَر، أو أن الله صور على صورته ملكاً، وأن ذلك من بركة دعائه. وإنما يكون الذي تصور لهم شيطان من الشياطين. وهذا مما نعرف أنه ابتلى به في زماننا وغير زماننا خلق كثير، أعرف منهم عدداً، وأعرف من ذلك وقائع متعددة. والشياطين أيضاً تُضِلَّ عُباد القبور كما كانت تضل المشركين من العرب وللمقابرية وغيرهم. وكانت اليونان من المشركين يعبدون الأوثان، ويُعانون السحر، كما ذكروا ذلك عن أرسطو وغيره. وكانت الشياطين تضلهم، وبهم يتم سحرُهم. وقد لا يعرفون هم أن ذلك من الشياطين، بل قد لا يُقرون بالشياطين. بل يظنون ذلك كله من «قوة أسباب النفس» أو من أمور طبيعيّة أو من قوى فلكية». فإن هذه الثلاثة أسباب عجائب العالم عند ابن سينا وموافقيه. وهم جاهلون بما سوى ذلك من أفعال الشياطين الذين هم أعظم تأثيراً في العالم في الشر من هذا كله وجاهلون بملائكة الله، الذين يجري بسببهم كلُّ خير في السماء والأرض. فساد دعواهم في الملائكة وما يدعونه من جعل الملائكة هي العقول العشرة أو هي القوى الصالحة في النفس»، وأن الشياطين هي القوى الخبيثة، مما قد عرف فساده بالدلائل العقلية، بل والضرورة من دين الرسول.
It is known that the polytheists, idol-worshippers and others, were misled by the devils, who spoke to them, fulfilled some of their needs, and informed them of unseen matters. The soothsayers had devils that informed them and commanded them, though the falsehood in what they said was more than the truth. Likewise, the polytheists of our time who call upon other than Allah, such as absent shaykhs and the dead, are shown by the devils in the forms of shaykhs, so that they imagine the shaykh has come, or that Allah has formed an angel in his image, and that this is a blessing of his prayer. In reality, what appeared to them is but a devil among devils. This is something we know has afflicted many people in our time and before, and I know many such cases and multiple incidents. The devils also mislead the worshippers of graves as they misled the Arab polytheists and the grave-worshippers and others. The Greek polytheists worshipped idols, practiced sorcery, as mentioned about Aristotle and others. The devils misled them, and through them their sorcery was accomplished. They themselves might not know that this comes from devils; indeed, they might not even acknowledge the existence of devils. Rather, they imagine all that comes from 'the power of psychic causes' or from natural matters or from celestial forces. For these three are, according to Ibn Sina and his followers, the causes of the wonders of the world. They are ignorant of other than that—the actions of devils, who have a greater effect upon the world in evil than all of this—and ignorant of Allah's angels, through whom all good in the heavens and earth occurs. The corruption of their claim about the angels—their assertion that the angels are the ten intellects or the good forces in the soul, and that the devils are the evil forces—is known by rational proofs, indeed by the necessity of the religion of the Messenger.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophers' (specifically Ibn Sina's) conception of angels and devils. He begins by describing the actual historical and observable phenomenon of devils misleading polytheists, soothsayers, and grave-worshippers, to establish the reality of devils as independent entities that interact with humans, not mere psychic forces. He then turns to the Greek polytheists and their sorcery, which the devils facilitated. Ibn Taymiyya notes that the philosophers, like Ibn Sina, attribute the wonders of the world to three causes: psychic powers, natural properties, and celestial forces, thereby denying the agency of devils. He accuses them of ignorance regarding the greater evil influence of devils and the good influence of angels. Finally, he directly refutes the philosophical identification of angels with the ten intellects (a common Neoplatonic idea) and of devils with evil psychic forces, declaring this to be known as corrupt by rational proofs and by the necessary teachings of Islam.

Key Terms
الشياطين (al-shayāṭīn): Real, independent evil entities that mislead humans, speak to them, and cause sorcery; not mere psychic forces. [Note: In Ibn Taymiyya's critique, he insists on their objective reality against the philosophers' psychological reduction.]
العقول العشرة (al-ʿuqūl al-ʿashara): The ten intellects of Neoplatonic cosmology, which the philosophers (e.g., Ibn Sina) identify with angels. [Note: Standard in falsafa; Ibn Taymiyya rejects this equation as un-Islamic.]
القوى الخبيثة (al-quwā al-khabītha): The philosophers' term for evil psychic forces, which they equate with devils; Ibn Taymiyya refutes this reduction. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya criticizes this as a misrepresentation of the reality of devils.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, what do Ibn Sina and his followers wrongly attribute the wonders of the world to?
A) The power of psychic causes, natural matters, and celestial forces
B) The actions of angels and devils directly
C) The pure will of God without intermediate causes
D) The accidental motion of atoms

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the philosophers' identification of angels and devils with abstract forces by appealing to empirical examples of their personal interactions and the necessity of Islamic teachings.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Allegorizing Angels and Devils
Ibn Taymiyya refutes philosophers' allegorization of angels and devils.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the philosophical allegorization of angels and devils that Ibn Taymiyya refutes, and reconstruct his rational and revelation-based arguments against it.

Core Idea
Philosophers reduce angels and devils to abstractions; Ibn Taymiyya restores their real agency by revelation and reason.

Ibn Taymiyya here targets a cornerstone of philosophical theology: the allegorization of angels as abstract intellects and devils as evil psychic forces. He argues that this not only contradicts revelation but also blinds one to the real causes of good and evil in the world. This lesson dissects his critique and its epistemological foundations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' position
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what do philosophers like Ibn Sina attribute the wonders of the world to? They claim these come from 'the power of the causes of the soul, natural matters, or celestial forces.'
Note that they deny the role of angels and devils.
2
Find what the philosophers deny
What do they ignore or deny? They ignore the actions of devils, who have a greater effect in evil, and they are ignorant of the angels through whom every good occurs.
3
Extract the rational proof
Ibn Taymiyya states that the corruption of their claim has been known by rational proofs and necessarily from the religion of the Messenger. This implies both rational and revelatory evidence.
Rational proofs may include the insufficiency of natural causes to explain all phenomena.
4
Assess the critique's force
Why does this matter for Islamic theology? It protects the reality of the unseen from philosophical reductionism, preserving the distinct agency of angels and devils as taught in revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكانت اليونان من المشركين يعبدون الأوثان، ويُعانون السحر، كما ذكروا ذلك عن أرسطو وغيره. وكانت الشياطين تضلهم، وبهم يتم سحرُهم. وقد لا يعرفون هم أن ذلك من الشياطين، بل قد لا يُقرون بالشياطين . بل يظنون ذلك كله من «قوة أسباب النفس أو من أمور طبيعيّة أو من قوى فلكية». فإن هذه الثلاثة أسباب عجائب العالم عند ابن سينا وموافقيه . و هم جاهلون بما سوى ذلك من أفعال الشياطين الذين هم أعظم تأثيراً في العالم في الشر من هذا كله وجاهلون بملائكة الله، الذين يجري بسببهم . كلُّ خير في السماء والأرض. وفساد دعواهم في الملائكة وما يدعونه من جعل الملائكة هي العقول العشرة أو هي القوى الصالحة في النفس»، وأن الشياطين هي القوى الخبيثة، مما قد عُرف فساده بالدلائل العقلية، بل والشياطين بالضرورة من دين الرسول .
The Greeks were among the polytheists who worshipped idols and practiced magic, as mentioned about Aristotle and others. The devils misled them, and through them their magic was accomplished. They themselves did not know that this was from the devils; indeed they did not affirm the existence of devils. Rather they thought all that was from 'the power of the causes of the soul' or from natural matters or from celestial powers. For these three are the causes of the wonders of the world according to Ibn Sina and his followers. And they are ignorant of what is besides that of the actions of the devils, who have a greater effect in the world in evil than all of this, and they are ignorant of the angels of God, through whom every good in the heavens and the earth occurs. And the corruption of their claim regarding the angels—and what they claim of making the angels the ten intellects or the good powers in the soul, and that the devils are the evil powers—has been known by rational proofs, and indeed the devils are necessarily known from the religion of the Messenger.

Commentary
This passage critiques the philosophers' (especially Ibn Sina) explanation of the wonders of the world through natural causes, psychic powers, or celestial forces, ignoring the real influence of devils and angels. Ibn Taymiyya points out that the Greeks, like Aristotle, were polytheists and magicians, and devils were involved in their magic. The philosophers are ignorant of the true nature of devils and angels. Their claim that angels are the 'ten intellects' or 'good powers' and devils are 'evil powers' is false, as known by rational proofs and necessarily from the religion of the Prophet.

Ibn Taymiyya's Epistemological Challenge to Emanation Theory
Author rejects the claim that universal knowledge requires cosmic intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemic criterion Ibn Taymiyya demands and evaluate why the emanation model fails to provide it.

Core Idea
Knowledge requires a criterion; emanation offers no way to distinguish it from conjecture.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya confronts the Illuminationist claim that universal knowledge emanates from a cosmic intellect. Instead of debating metaphysics, he asks a sharp epistemological question: how do we know that what we receive is actually knowledge and not just opinion or ignorance? This shifts the burden of proof back to the logicians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponents' claim
Find what the logicians say happens when we sense particulars. They say universal knowledge comes from 'Wahib al-'Aql' or 'al-'Aql al-Fa''al'.
2
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's counter-question
He asks: how is it known that the universal judgment in the soul is 'knowledge' and not 'zann' (conjecture) or 'jahl' (ignorance)?
This is the crux of his critique.
3
Analyze the epistemic gap
The opponents provide a metaphysical mechanism (emanation) but no epistemological criterion. Ibn Taymiyya exposes that the status of 'knowledge' is assumed, not justified.
4
Summarize the logical move
Ibn Taymiyya does not deny that universals arise; he challenges how we certify them as certain knowledge. This is a classic critical refinement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا: بل عند الإحساس بالجزئيات يحصل في النفس علم كلي من واهب العقل؛ أو: تستعد النفس عند الإحساس بالجزئيات لأن يفيض عليها الكلي من واهب العقل - أو قالوا من العقل الفعّال عندهم أو نحو ذلك، قيل لهم: الكلام في ما به يُعلم أن ذلك الحكم الكلي الذي في النفس «علم» - لا ظن ولا جهل.
And if they say: Rather, upon sensory perception of particulars, a universal knowledge occurs in the soul from the Giver of Intellect; or: the soul becomes disposed upon sensory perception of particulars for the universal to emanate upon it from the Giver of Intellect—or they say from the Active Intellect according to them or similar—it is said to them: The discussion is about how it is known that that universal judgment in the soul is 'knowledge'—not conjecture or ignorance.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents a counter-question from Ibn Taymiyya to the alternative epistemology of the logicians who claim that universal knowledge arises from an external source like the Giver of Intellect (wahib al-'aql) or the Active Intellect (al-'aql al-fa''al). They propose that when the mind senses particulars, it triggers an emanation of universal knowledge from a cosmic intellect. Ibn Taymiyya refutes this by asking a fundamental epistemological question: How do you know that the universal judgment present in the soul is genuine knowledge and not mere conjecture or ignorance? The logicians offer no criterion to distinguish genuine knowledge from false belief in this model.

Key Terms
واهب العقل
العقل الفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) How does the soul connect to the intellect?
B) How do we know that universal judgment is knowledge and not conjecture?
C) How does the Active Intellect exist?
D) How can particulars lead to universals?

Key Takeaway
Epistemological justification cannot be replaced by metaphysical storytelling; the status of 'knowledge' must always be defended with criteria.

Ibn Taymiyya's Linguistic Critique: The True Meaning of 'Kalima'
Ibn Taymiyya argues for the original meaning of 'kalima' as a complete sentence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's argument that 'kalima' in classical Arabic means a complete sentence, and distinguish his evidence from the later grammarians' redefinition.

Core Idea
Kalima in Arabic meant a complete sentence; later grammarians changed it to 'word'.

You may have learned that 'kalima' means 'word' in Arabic grammar. But Ibn Taymiyya challenges this as a later innovation. In this passage, he argues from classical usage—Quran, Hadith, and the authority of Sibawayh—that 'kalima' originally denoted a complete sentence, not a word. Understanding this distinction is crucial for interpreting Islamic texts correctly. Let's analyze his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's claim about 'kalima'
He asserts that in classical Arabic, kalima always means a complete sentence (jumla tamma), not a part of speech like a noun, verb, or particle.
The later grammarians redefined kalima as a generic term for these types.
2
Examine his evidence from Quran and Hadith
Locate the prophetic hadiths and Quranic verses he cites. For each example, check whether 'kalima' refers to a full sentence or a single word. Examples: 'klimatān khafīfatān' refers to two phrases, and 'kalimat Labīd' is a line of poetry (a complete sentence).
3
Understand the critique of later grammarians
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the later grammarians who claimed that 'kalam' sometimes means 'kalima'. He insists both terms refer only to complete sentences, citing Sibawayh's rule that 'al-qawl' is used only for 'kalam' (complete sentences), not isolated words.
This shows the linguistic basis for his critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استطراد آخر: «الكلمة» و«الكلام» في كلام العرب. ثم إن الآخرين جعلوا أن «الكلمة» اسم جنس لهذه الأنواع، ولفظ «الكلمة» لا يوجد في لغة العرب إلا اسماً لجملة تامة - اسمية أو فعلية - كقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «كلمتان خفيفتان على اللسان حبيبتان إلى الرحمن، ثقيلتان في الميزان: سبحان الله وبحمده سبحان الله العظيم»، وقوله: «أصدق كلمة قالها شاعر كلمة لبيد: ألا كل شيء ما خلا الله باطل»، وقوله في النساء: «أخذتموهن بأمانة الله، واستحللتم فروجهن بكلمة الله»، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿وَجَعَل كَلِمَةَ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا السُّفْلَى وَكَلِمَةُ اللهِ هِيَ الْعُلْيَا﴾ [سورة التوبة: 40]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَيُنذِرَ الَّذِينَ قَالُوا اتَّخَذَ اللَّهُ وَلَدًا مَّا لَهُم بِهِ مِنْ عِلْمٍ وَلَا لِآبَائِهِمْ كَبُرَتْ كَلِمَةً تَخْرُجُ مِنْ أَفْوَاهِهِمْ إِن يَقُولُونَ إِلَّا كَذِبًا﴾ [سورة الكهف: 4-5]. ومثل هذا كثير في كلام العرب. و بعض متأخري النحاة لما سمع بعض هذا قال: وقد يراد بـ «الكلام» «الكلمة». وليس الأمر كما زعمه بل لا يوجد في كلام العرب لفظ «الكلمة» إلا للجملة التامة التي هي «كلام». ولا تطلق العرب لفظ «كلمة» ولا «كلام» إلا على جملة تامة. ولهذا ذكر سيبويه أنهم يحكون بـ «القول ما كان كلاماً»، ولا يحكون به ما ليس بكلام.
Another digression: 'al-Kalima' and 'al-Kalam' in the speech of the Arabs. Then the later [grammarians] made 'kalima' a generic name for these types [noun, verb, particle], but the word 'kalima' does not occur in the language of the Arabs except as a name for a complete sentence - nominal or verbal - like the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Two words (kalimatān) light on the tongue, beloved to the Most Merciful, heavy in the balance: Subhan Allah wa bihamdihi, Subhan Allah al-'Azim', and his saying: 'The most truthful word (kalima) spoken by a poet is the word of Labid: "Indeed, everything besides Allah is false"', and his saying concerning women: 'You took them with a trust from Allah, and you made their private parts lawful with the word (kalima) of Allah', and from this is the saying of Allah: 'And Allah made the word (kalima) of those who disbelieve the lowest, and the word (kalima) of Allah is the highest' (Quran 9:40), and His saying: 'And to warn those who say, "Allah has taken a son." They have no knowledge of it, nor had their fathers. Grave is the word (kalima) that comes out of their mouths; they speak not except a lie' (Quran 18:4-5). And similar to this is abundant in the speech of the Arabs. And some of the later grammarians, when they heard some of this, said: 'Sometimes "kalam" is used to mean "kalima".' But the matter is not as he claimed; rather, the word 'kalima' does not occur in the speech of the Arabs except for a complete sentence that is 'kalam'. And the Arabs do not use the word 'kalima' nor 'kalam' except for a complete sentence. For this reason, Sibawayh mentioned that they narrate with 'al-qawl' what is 'kalam', and they do not narrate with it what is not 'kalam'.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic critique by Ibn Taymiyya against later grammarians who redefined 'kalima' as a generic term for noun, verb, and particle. He argues that in classical Arabic usage, 'kalima' always means a complete sentence (like a phrase or utterance), not a single word. He supports this with prophetic hadiths and Quranic verses where 'kalima' refers to full expressions: the Prophet mentions 'two words' which are actually the phrases 'Subhan Allah wa bihamdihi...', and 'the most truthful word' is a line of poetry (a complete sentence). The Quranic verses also use 'kalima' to mean sayings or statements. He then criticizes the later grammarians for claiming that 'kalam' can mean 'kalima', insisting that both terms refer only to complete sentences. He cites Sibawayh, the foundational grammarian, to support his view: they only narrate with 'al-qawl' (saying) what is a complete sentence ('kalam'), not isolated words. This passage guards against the anachronistic imposition of logical terminology onto the Arabic language.

Key Terms
الكلمة (al-kalima): In classical Arabic, it means a complete sentence (juma tamma), not a single word as it later came to be used in logic-influenced grammar. [Note: In later grammatical terminology, kalima was redefined to mean 'word' (a part of speech). Ibn Taymiyya rejects this shift, insisting on the original usage.]
الكلام (al-kalam): A complete sentence that conveys a meaning; in this passage, it is synonymous with 'kalima' in the sense of a full utterance. [Note: In later logic and grammar, 'kalam' sometimes means 'speech' in general, but here Ibn Taymiyya restricts it to a complete sentence.]
القول (al-qawl): Saying; used to report only complete sentences (kalam) according to Sibawayh. [Note: No significant shift; it is used in its standard sense.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what evidence does he use to support his claim that 'kalima' means a complete sentence?
A) The Quran and Hadith show 'kalima' referring to complete sentences.
B) Later grammarians unanimously agree.
C) The word 'kalima' has no usage in Arabic.
D) Sibawayh said 'kalima' means a single word.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's linguistic critique guards against anachronistic interpretations by insisting that 'kalima' in classical Arabic means a complete sentence, not a word.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Examine the Quranic verse: 'وَجَعَلَ كَلِمَةَ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا السُّفْلَى وَكَلِمَةُ اللهِ هِيَ الْعُلْيَا' (Quran 9:40). Explain why Ibn Taymiyya would argue that 'kalima' here means a complete sentence (the statement of disbelief vs. the statement of faith), not a single word.
?

The True Supreme Science: Logicians vs. Muslims
Author contrasts the logicians' supreme science (existence) with the Muslims' (knowledge of Allah).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' supreme science and explain why knowledge of Allah is superior.

Core Idea
Abstract universal concepts cannot perfect the soul; only knowledge of Allah does.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the very foundation of Greek-inspired logic: the claim that the science of existence is the supreme science. He argues that this science deals only with mental constructs, not external reality, and cannot lead to the perfection of the soul. Instead, he asserts that the true supreme science for Muslims is knowledge of Allah, which is the basis of all knowledge and the means to true perfection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing claims
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states the logicians' claim about the supreme science (knowledge of existence) and the Muslims' claim (knowledge of Allah). Note how he sets up the contrast.
The logicians' supreme science is 'al-ilm al-a'la' focused on existence; the Muslims' is knowledge of Allah.
2
Analyze the critique of the logicians' science
Ibn Taymiyya argues that knowledge of existence as a commonality has no external reality. He says it is only knowledge of abstract commonalities, not of actual things. Identify his reasons: existence is a mental construct, it does not entail knowledge of particulars, and its divisions (substance, accident) are not proven.
He specifically criticizes the five substances and nine accidents, showing only 'body' is externally real.
3
Understand the positive alternative
Ibn Taymiyya presents knowledge of Allah as the true supreme science. He says it is the foundation of all sciences, entails knowledge of every existent when complete, and is what perfects the soul.
He connects soul perfection to knowledge and worship of Allah, not abstract concepts.
4
Evaluate the epistemological comparison
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that even the corrupted knowledge of Jews and Christians is superior to the philosophers' science. This shows the low value he places on the logicians' supreme science.
This is a strong rhetorical move, placing Islamic knowledge above all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الوجه الثالث : العلم الأعلى عند المنطقيين وعند المسلمين
وهذا بخلاف العلم الأعلى عند المسلمين فإنه العلم بالله الذي هو في نفسه العلم الأعلى عند المسلمين أعلى من غيره من كل وجه والعلم به أعلى العلوم من كل وجه، والعلم به [۱۱۸] أصل لكل علم وهم يسلّمون أن العلم به إذا حَصَل على الوجه التام يستلزم العلم بكل موجود.
وهذا بخلاف العلم بمسمى الوجود». فإن هذا لا حقيقة له في الخارج ؛ ولا العلم بالقدر المشترك يستلزم العلم بأجناسه وأنواعه وما يتميز به كلُّ شيء؛ بل ليس فيه إلا علم بقدر مشترك لا تصور له في الخارج ؛ وإنما هو علم بهذه المشتركات .
وليس في مجرد العلم بذلك ما يوجب كمال النفس، بل ولا في العلم بأقسامه العامة. فإنا إذا علمنا أن «الوجود» ينقسم إلى «جوهر» و «عرض»، وأن أقسام «الجوهر» خمسة كما زعموه (۱) . مع أن ذلك ليس بصحيح، ولا يثبت مما ذكروه إلا الجسم». وأما «المادة»، و «الصورة»، و «النفس»، و «العقل»، فلا يثبت لها حقيقة في الخارج، إلا أن يكون جسماً) أو عرضاً». ولكن ما يثبتونه يعود إلى أمر مقدر في النفس لا في الخارج، كما قد بسط في موضعه .
وقد اعترف بذلك من ينصرهم ويعظمهم كأبي محمد بن حزم (۲) وغيره . تعظيم ابن ولتعظيمه / المنطق رواه بإسناده إلى مَتَى التَّرْجُمان الذي ترجمه إلى العربية. ومع هذا فاعترف بما ذكرناه وقد بسط ذلك في موضعه .
ونحن نفرض هنا وجود ذلك في الخارج. فالعلم بانقسام ذلك إلى «جواهر خمسة»، وانقسام «العرض) إلى الأنواع التسعة (٤) . مع أنه لم يقم دليل على انقسامه إلى تسعة عند بعضهم . وقد أنشدوا فيها :
زيد الطويل الأسود ابن مالكِ في داره بالأمس كان يتكي
في يده سيف نضاه فانتضى فهذه عشر مقولات سوى
فذكر في هذين البيتين الجوهر والكم والكيف والإضافة [١١٩] والأين ومتى
والوضع والمِلْك وأن يفعل وأن ينفعل .
ولما لم يقم دليل على حصر أجناسها العالية في تسعة جعلها بعضهم خمسة،
وعندهم كلما كان أعم كان أقرب إلى
وبعضهم ثلاثة : الكم والكيف، والإضافة. والمقصود هنا أنه إذا علم هذا التقسيم (۱) ؛ المعقول وكان البرهان عليه أقوم، فإنه لا يقوم برهان واجب القبول دائماً إلا على ما لا يتغير . وهذه الأعراض عندهم لا تقوم بـ واجب الوجود»، بل ولا بـ «العقول» إلا بعضها على نزاع بينهم فيعود الكمال إلى تصور وجود «مطلق لا حقيقة له في الخارج، كتصور ذات مطلقة»، و «شيء مطلق»، و «حقيقة مطلقة».
وأي كمال للنفس في مجرد تصور هذه الأمور العامة الكلية إذا لم تتصور أعيانَ الموجودات المعينة الجزئية؟ وأي علم في هذا برب العالمين الذي لا تكمل النفوس إلا / بمعرفته وعبادته محبة وذلاً ، كما قد بسط في موضعه (۲)
ولهذا كانت نهاية الفلاسفة - إذا هداهم الله بعض الهداية - بداية اليهود والنصارى الكفار، فضلاً عن المسلمين، أمة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم. فإن ما عند اليهود بداية اليهود والنصارى والنصارى الكفار بعد النسخ والتبديل مما هو من نوع كمال النفس أفضلُ في الجنس ـ الكم والكيف - مما عند الفلاسفة .
The Third Station - the Third Aspect: The supreme science according to the logicians and according to the Muslims.
And this is contrary to the supreme science according to the Muslims, for it is knowledge of Allah, which is in itself the supreme science according to the Muslims, higher than everything else in every respect, and knowledge of it is the highest of sciences in every respect, and knowledge of it [118] is the foundation of every science, and they acknowledge that knowledge of it, if obtained in a complete manner, entails knowledge of every existent.
And this is contrary to knowledge of what is called 'existence'. For this has no reality in the external world; nor does knowledge of the commonality entail knowledge of its genera and species and what distinguishes each thing; rather, there is in it only knowledge of a commonality that has no conception in the external world; rather, it is knowledge of these commonalities.
And there is not in mere knowledge of that anything that necessitates perfection of the soul, nor even in knowledge of its general divisions. For if we know that 'existence' divides into 'substance' and 'accident', and that the divisions of 'substance' are five as they claim (1), while that is not correct, and from what they have mentioned nothing is established except 'body'. As for 'matter', 'form', 'soul', and 'intellect', no reality for them is established in the external world, unless it be a body or an accident. But what they establish goes back to something estimated in the mind, not in the external world, as has been expounded in its place.
And this has been acknowledged by those who support and venerate them, like Abu Muhammad ibn Hazm (2) and others. The veneration of Ibn Hazm the Andalusian for logic: he narrated by his chain to Matta al-Tarjuman who translated it into Arabic. Yet with this he acknowledged what we have mentioned, and that has been expounded in its place.
And we here assume the existence of that in the external world. So knowledge of its division into 'five substances' and the division of 'accident' into the nine types (4), while no proof has been established for its division into nine according to some of them. And they have recited concerning it:
Zayd al-Tawil al-Aswad ibn Malik was in his house yesterday reclining
In his hand was a sword, he drew it, so these are ten categories besides
Thus he mentioned in these two verses: substance, quantity, quality, relation [119], position, when, posture, possession, and to act and to be acted upon.
And since no proof was established for limiting their highest genera to nine, some of them made them five, and according to them the more general the closer to, and some made them three: quantity, quality, and relation. And the point here is that if one knows this division (1) and the demonstration upon it is more established, yet a demonstration that is necessarily acceptable is only established upon what does not change. And these accidents according to them do not subsist by the 'Necessary Existent', nor even by 'intellects' except some of them, with disagreement among them, so the perfection returns to the conception of an 'absolute existence' that has no reality in the external world, like the conception of an 'absolute essence', an 'absolute thing', and an 'absolute reality'.
And what perfection is there for the soul in the mere conception of these universal general matters if it does not conceive the individual particular entities of existents? And what knowledge is there in this of the Lord of the worlds, whose knowledge and worship in love and humility perfect the souls, as has been expounded in its place (2)?
And therefore, the end of the philosophers - if Allah graces them with some guidance - is the beginning of the Jews and Christians, the disbelievers, let alone the Muslims, the nation of Muhammad, peace be upon him. For what the Jews have, the beginning of the Jews and Christians, the disbelievers after abrogation and alteration, is better in kind - quantity and quality - than what the philosophers have.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a critical comparison between the logicians' concept of the supreme science (al-ilm al-a'la) and that of the Muslims. The author, Ibn Taymiyya, argues that the logicians' supreme science is knowledge of existence as a commonality, which has no external reality and thus cannot perfect the soul. In contrast, the Muslims' supreme science is knowledge of Allah, which is the foundation of all sciences and entails knowledge of all existents. He then critiques the logicians' division of substance into five and accident into nine categories, pointing out that only 'body' is proven, while 'matter', 'form', 'soul', and 'intellect' are not established externally. He mentions that even supporters like Ibn Hazm acknowledged this. The categories, such as those recited in the verses (substance, quantity, quality, relation, etc.), are arbitrary; some philosophers reduced them to five or three. The key point is that such knowledge of universal concepts does not lead to soul perfection; only knowledge of Allah, the specific Lord, does. The passage ends with a strong critique that the philosophers' end is inferior to even the corrupted knowledge of Jews and Christians.

Key Terms
العلم الأعلى
الوجود
الجوهر
العرض
كمال النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge of existence as a common concept
B) Knowledge of the five substances and nine accidents
C) Knowledge of Allah, the Lord of the worlds
D) Knowledge of the categories as enumerated by logicians

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' supreme science of existence is merely abstract and cannot perfect the soul; only knowledge of Allah, the true supreme science, can.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Realist Universals
Ibn Taymiyya argues that universals exist only in the mind, not externally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that universals exist only in the mind, and distinguish his position from the realist view.

Core Idea
The universal exists only in the mind; outside it is always particular.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the prevalent view among logicians that universals have a real existence in the external world. In this passage, he uses both linguistic evidence from the Quran and the sayings of the Companions, as well as logical analysis, to argue that universality is a mental construct. Understanding this critique is essential for grasping the broader debate about the ontological status of concepts in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target position
What claim about universals is Ibn Taymiyya refuting? He references Pythagoras and 'abstract numbers' as the beginning of the philosophy that posits universals externally.
The realist position holds that universals have extra-mental reality.
2
Examine the linguistic evidence
Ibn Taymiyya cites common expressions like 'in myself' and the Quranic verse about Jacob's need to show that mental existence is spoken of as if it were external.
This establishes that mental existence is real in a sense, but does not imply external universals.
3
Grasp the logical core
Why can't a universal exist externally? Because anything external is particular (mu'ayyan), not universal. Universality requires applicability to multiple instances, which only the mind can supply.
The key phrase: 'wa al-kulli idha wajada fi al-kharij la yakun illa mu'ayyanan, la yakun kulliyyan.'
4
Assess the refutation of a third realm
Ibn Taymiyya dismisses the notion of an essence neither in mind nor external as incoherent: a complete conception of the statement suffices to know its corruption.
This is a swift dismissal based on the impossibility of conceiving such an essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الوجه الرابع : مبتدأ فلسفة فيثاغورس هو «الأعداد المجردة» وما في الذهن يطلق عليه أنه قد يوجد في الخارج، كما يقال: «فعلتُ ما في ما في الذهن قد يوجد في نفسي»، و «في نفسي أمور أريد فعلها، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿ إِلَّا حَاجَةً فِي نَفْسِ يَعْقُوبَ في الخارج قضَهَا ﴾ [سورة يوسف : ٦٨] ، وقول عمر : كنت زَوَّرْتُ في نفسي مقالة أحببتُ أن أقولها». ونظائره كثيرة . والكلي إذا وجد في الخارج لا يكون إلا معيناً، لا يكون كلياً. فكونه كلياً مشروط بكونه في الذهن . ومن أثبت ماهية لا في الذهن ولا في الخارج، فتصور قوله تصوراً تاماً يكفي في العلم بفساد قوله. وهذه الأمور مبسوطة في غير هذا الموضع .
The third station - the fourth aspect: The beginning of the philosophy of Pythagoras is 'abstract numbers'. And what is in the mind is said to possibly exist externally, as it is said: 'I did what is in what is in the mind may exist in myself', and 'In myself are matters I wish to do', and from it is the saying of the Most High: 'except a need in the soul of Jacob which he fulfilled' [Surah Yusuf: 68], and the saying of Umar: 'I had prepared in myself a speech that I wished to deliver.' And similar instances are many. And the universal, if it exists externally, is only particular, not universal. So its being universal is conditioned by its being in the mind. And whoever posits an essence neither in the mind nor externally, a complete conception of his statement suffices for knowing the corruption of his statement. These matters are expounded elsewhere.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the realist position on universals. Ibn Taymiyya begins by noting that people sometimes speak of 'what is in the mind' as if it could exist externally, giving examples from the Quran and the saying of Umar. He then makes the central point: the universal (al-kulli), if it were to exist in the external world, would be a particular (mu'ayyan), not a universal. Therefore, universality is conditional upon being in the mind. Anyone who asserts an essence that is neither in the mind nor externally is refuted simply by understanding the statement. The argument follows the order of: observation of common usage, then logical analysis, then dismissal of incoherent claims.

Key Terms
الكلي (al-kulli): The universal: a concept that applies to many individuals; Ibn Taymiyya argues it cannot exist in the external world as a universal, only as a particular. [Note: In standard Islamic logic, al-kulli is often considered to have some extra-mental reality as a nature; Ibn Taymiyya here rejects that.]
الذهن (al-dhihn): The mind: the locus where universals exist as concepts, according to Ibn Taymiyya. [Note: No significant shift; stable across texts.]
الخارج (al-kharij): The external world: where only particulars exist, not universals. [Note: Standard term for extra-mental reality; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for something being a universal (kulli)?
A) It must exist in the external world as a universal.
B) It must be in the mind.
C) It must be a particular.
D) It must be praised by the scholars.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that universals are mental constructs; external existence is always particular, undermining the realist position.

True Proofs Without Ultimate Benefit
Ibn Taymiyya argues that logic and mathematics are true but useless for salvation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the spiritual uselessness of logical and mathematical sciences and distinguish it from a rejection of their truth.

Core Idea
True logical proofs do not guarantee spiritual success or salvation.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya returns to his central critique: even though logical and mathematical sciences produce true proofs, they do not perfect the soul, save from punishment, or bring happiness. He invokes al-Ghazali's famous classification to drive the point home. In this lesson, we will unpack this critique and understand its implications for the value of logic in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the opening sentence: Ibn Taymiyya states that the particular science (logic) produces true proofs, yet it does not perfect the soul, nor save from punishment, nor bring happiness. This is the core critique.
2
Analyze al-Ghazali's classification
Find the quotation from Abu Hamid al-Ghazali. He divides the sciences of the logicians into two categories: true but useless, and false opinions that are sinful. Note that mathematical sciences are in the first category, while theology and astrology are in the second.
The phrase 'and we seek refuge in Allah from knowledge that does not benefit' is a hadith that underscores the danger of useless knowledge.
3
Understand the implication
Ibn Taymiyya is not denying the truth of logical and mathematical sciences. Rather, he denies their ultimate worth. Ask yourself: what is the criterion for benefit? For Ibn Taymiyya, it is spiritual salvation. By this measure, logic fails.
4
Contrast with alternative views
Consider how this critique challenges the claim that logic is a necessary tool for theology. Ibn Taymiyya's position is that even if it were useful, it is not necessary for salvation. This is a radical refinement of the value of logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عود إلى أصل الموضوع : والمقصود هنا أن هذا العلم الخاص هو الذي تقوم عليه براهين صادقة، لكن لا تكمل بذلك نفس، ولا تنجو به من عذاب ولا يحصل لها به سعادة. ولهذا قال أبو حامد الغزالي وغيره في علوم هؤلاء : هي بين علوم صادقة لا منفعة فيها - ونعوذُ بالله من علم لا ينفع؛ وبين ظنون كاذبة لا ثقة بها - وإِن بَعْضَ الظَّنِ إثْم ﴾ [الحجرات: ١٢]». يشيرون بالأول إلى العلوم الرياضية، وبالثاني إلى ما يقولونه في الإلهيات، وفي أحكام النجوم، ونحو ذلك . الأسباب المُغْرِية بالاشتغال بالعلم الرياضي وما أشبهه التذاذ النفس : لكن قد تلتذ النفس كما تلتذ بغير ذلك. فإن الإنسان يلتذ بعلم ما لم يكن علمه، وسماع ما لم يكن سمعه ، إذا لم يكن مشغولاً عن ذلك بما هو أهم عنده منه، كما قد يلتذ بأنواع من الأفعال التي هي من جنس اللهو واللعب. وأيضاً ففي الإدمان على معرفة ذلك تعتاد النفس العلم الصحيح، والقضايا الصادقة، والقياس المستقيم، فيكون في ذلك تصحيح الذهن والإدراك، وتعويد النفس أنها تعلم الحق وتقوله لتستعين بذلك على المعرفة التي هي فوق ذلك . ولهذا يقال : إنه كان أوائل الفلاسفة أول ما يعلمون أولادهم العلم الرياضي. وكثير من شيوخهم في آخر أمره إنما يشتغل بذلك، لأنه لما نظر في طرقهم وطرق من عارضهم من أهل الكلام الباطل لم يجد في ذلك ما هو حق، أخذ يشغل نفسه بالعلم الرياضي، كما كان يجري مثل ذلك لمن هو من أئمة الفلاسفة كابن واصل (۱) ، وغيره . وكذلك كثير من متأخري أصحابنا يشتغلون وقت بطالتهم بعلم الفرائض، والحساب والجبر والمقابلة والهندسة ونحو ذلك، لأن فيه تفريحاً للنفس، وهو علم صحيح لا يدخل فيه غلط . وقد جاء عن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه أنه قال : «إذا لهَوْتم فالهوا بالرمي، وإذا تحدثتم فتحدثوا بالفرائض ) . فإن حساب الفرائض علم معقول مبني على (۲))
Return to the original subject: And the intended meaning here is that this particular knowledge is that upon which true proofs are based, but the soul is not perfected thereby, nor does it escape punishment, nor does it attain happiness through it. That is why Abu Hamid al-Ghazali and others said regarding the sciences of these people: 'They are between true sciences with no benefit in them — and we seek refuge in Allah from knowledge that does not benefit; and false opinions with no confidence — and indeed some opinion is sin.' They refer by the first to the mathematical sciences, and by the second to what they say in theology, judgments of the stars, and the like. The enticing reasons for occupying oneself with mathematical sciences and the like: Pleasure of the soul: But the soul may take pleasure as it takes pleasure in other things. For a person takes pleasure in knowing what he did not know, and hearing what he did not hear, if he is not preoccupied with what is more important to him, just as he may take pleasure in types of actions that are of the kind of amusement and play. Also, through addiction to knowing this, the soul becomes accustomed to correct knowledge, true propositions, and sound syllogism, so in that there is correction of the mind and perception, and training the soul that it learns and speaks the truth to use that for knowledge that is above that. For this reason, it is said that the early philosophers used to first teach their children mathematical science. And many of their elders at the end of their life occupy themselves with that, because when they looked at their methods and the methods of those who opposed them from the people of false scholastic theology, and did not find any truth therein, they began to occupy themselves with mathematical science, as happened to those who were among the leading philosophers like Ibn Wasil and others. Likewise, many of our later companions occupy themselves in their free time with the science of inheritance shares (fara'id), calculation, algebra, geometry, and the like, because in it there is relaxation for the soul, and it is a correct science into which no error enters. And it has come from Umar ibn al-Khattab that he said: 'If you amuse yourselves, amuse yourselves with archery; if you speak, speak of inheritance shares.' For the calculation of inheritance shares is an intelligible science based on...
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the value of logic and mathematical sciences. Ibn Taymiyya returns to the main subject: the logical science produces true proofs, yet it does not perfect the soul, save from punishment, or bring happiness. He quotes al-Ghazali's classification: these sciences are either true but useless, or false opinions that are sinful. The mathematical sciences are the former, and speculative theology and astrology are the latter. Then, Ibn Taymiyya discusses why people still engage in mathematics: (1) the soul takes pleasure in learning, (2) it trains the mind in correct reasoning, and (3) it provides relaxation. He gives historical examples: early philosophers taught their children mathematics, and later scholars turned to it out of frustration with theological disputes. He notes that many of his contemporaries occupy themselves with fara'id and mathematics in their free time, citing a hadith of Umar encouraging speaking about inheritance shares. The overall point is that while these sciences may be true and beneficial for the mind, they are not the ultimate knowledge that saves or perfects the soul.

Key Terms
البراهين الصادقة
العلوم الرياضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because they are false.
B) Because they are true but the soul is not perfected by them.
C) Because they are forbidden by the Qur'an.
D) Because they are too difficult to learn.

Key Takeaway
Logical and mathematical sciences may produce true proofs, but they do not perfect the soul or lead to salvation; only knowledge that guides to action and piety has ultimate benefit.

The Enticements of Mathematics: Pleasure, Training, and Relaxation
Ibn Taymiyya lists reasons why people engage in mathematics: pleasure, mental training, and relaxation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and evaluate the secondary benefits of mathematics that Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges, and explain why they do not constitute ultimate benefit.

Core Idea
Mathematics is pursued for mental pleasure and training, but remains a pastime, not a path to truth.

After establishing that logical and mathematical sciences lack spiritual benefit, Ibn Taymiyya turns to explain why people nonetheless engage in them. He identifies several enticements: intellectual pleasure, mental training, and relaxation. In this lesson, we will examine these reasons and see how they fit into his broader critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first enticement: intellectual pleasure
Ibn Taymiyya says the soul takes pleasure in learning new things, just as it enjoys play. This pleasure is similar to that of amusement (lahw). Recognize that he is not condemning this pleasure, but he is relativizing it.
2
Identify the second enticement: mental training (tas-hih al-dhihn)
He argues that through addiction to mathematics, the soul becomes accustomed to correct knowledge, true propositions, and sound syllogism. This 'correction of the mind' enables one to learn and speak truth, and can be used for higher knowledge. But note: he says 'to use that for knowledge that is above that'—meaning it is a means, not an end.
The phrase 'knowledge that is above that' likely refers to revealed knowledge or spiritual truths.
3
Identify the third enticement: relaxation and diversion
Ibn Taymiyya gives examples: early philosophers taught children mathematics; later philosophers turned to it when frustrated with theological disputes. He also notes that his own contemporaries (later Hanbalis) occupied free time with fara'id, algebra, geometry, etc., because it is a correct science with no errors, and it relaxes the soul.
He quotes Umar ibn al-Khattab recommending archery for amusement and speaking about inheritance shares for conversation, showing that such activities are permissible but not spiritually essential.
4
Assess the cumulative argument
Consider: these benefits—pleasure, mental training, relaxation—are real. Yet they do not contradict Ibn Taymiyya's main claim that these sciences do not lead to salvation. They are merely worldly benefits. This step forces you to distinguish between intrinsic value and ultimate value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عود إلى أصل الموضوع : والمقصود هنا أن هذا العلم الخاص هو الذي تقوم عليه براهين صادقة، لكن لا تكمل بذلك نفس، ولا تنجو به من عذاب ولا يحصل لها به سعادة. ولهذا قال أبو حامد الغزالي وغيره في علوم هؤلاء : هي بين علوم صادقة لا منفعة فيها - ونعوذُ بالله من علم لا ينفع؛ وبين ظنون كاذبة لا ثقة بها - وإِن بَعْضَ الظَّنِ إثْم ﴾ [الحجرات: ١٢]». يشيرون بالأول إلى العلوم الرياضية، وبالثاني إلى ما يقولونه في الإلهيات، وفي أحكام النجوم، ونحو ذلك . الأسباب المُغْرِية بالاشتغال بالعلم الرياضي وما أشبهه التذاذ النفس : لكن قد تلتذ النفس كما تلتذ بغير ذلك. فإن الإنسان يلتذ بعلم ما لم يكن علمه، وسماع ما لم يكن سمعه ، إذا لم يكن مشغولاً عن ذلك بما هو أهم عنده منه، كما قد يلتذ بأنواع من الأفعال التي هي من جنس اللهو واللعب. وأيضاً ففي الإدمان على معرفة ذلك تعتاد النفس العلم الصحيح، والقضايا الصادقة، والقياس المستقيم، فيكون في ذلك تصحيح الذهن والإدراك، وتعويد النفس أنها تعلم الحق وتقوله لتستعين بذلك على المعرفة التي هي فوق ذلك . ولهذا يقال : إنه كان أوائل الفلاسفة أول ما يعلمون أولادهم العلم الرياضي. وكثير من شيوخهم في آخر أمره إنما يشتغل بذلك، لأنه لما نظر في طرقهم وطرق من عارضهم من أهل الكلام الباطل لم يجد في ذلك ما هو حق، أخذ يشغل نفسه بالعلم الرياضي، كما كان يجري مثل ذلك لمن هو من أئمة الفلاسفة كابن واصل (۱) ، وغيره . وكذلك كثير من متأخري أصحابنا يشتغلون وقت بطالتهم بعلم الفرائض، والحساب والجبر والمقابلة والهندسة ونحو ذلك، لأن فيه تفريحاً للنفس، وهو علم صحيح لا يدخل فيه غلط . وقد جاء عن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه أنه قال : «إذا لهَوْتم فالهوا بالرمي، وإذا تحدثتم فتحدثوا بالفرائض ) . فإن حساب الفرائض علم معقول مبني على (۲))
Return to the original subject: And the intended meaning here is that this particular knowledge is that upon which true proofs are based, but the soul is not perfected thereby, nor does it escape punishment, nor does it attain happiness through it. That is why Abu Hamid al-Ghazali and others said regarding the sciences of these people: 'They are between true sciences with no benefit in them — and we seek refuge in Allah from knowledge that does not benefit; and false opinions with no confidence — and indeed some opinion is sin.' They refer by the first to the mathematical sciences, and by the second to what they say in theology, judgments of the stars, and the like. The enticing reasons for occupying oneself with mathematical sciences and the like: Pleasure of the soul: But the soul may take pleasure as it takes pleasure in other things. For a person takes pleasure in knowing what he did not know, and hearing what he did not hear, if he is not preoccupied with what is more important to him, just as he may take pleasure in types of actions that are of the kind of amusement and play. Also, through addiction to knowing this, the soul becomes accustomed to correct knowledge, true propositions, and sound syllogism, so in that there is correction of the mind and perception, and training the soul that it learns and speaks the truth to use that for knowledge that is above that. For this reason, it is said that the early philosophers used to first teach their children mathematical science. And many of their elders at the end of their life occupy themselves with that, because when they looked at their methods and the methods of those who opposed them from the people of false scholastic theology, and did not find any truth therein, they began to occupy themselves with mathematical science, as happened to those who were among the leading philosophers like Ibn Wasil and others. Likewise, many of our later companions occupy themselves in their free time with the science of inheritance shares (fara'id), calculation, algebra, geometry, and the like, because in it there is relaxation for the soul, and it is a correct science into which no error enters. And it has come from Umar ibn al-Khattab that he said: 'If you amuse yourselves, amuse yourselves with archery; if you speak, speak of inheritance shares.' For the calculation of inheritance shares is an intelligible science based on...
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the value of logic and mathematical sciences. Ibn Taymiyya returns to the main subject: the logical science produces true proofs, yet it does not perfect the soul, save from punishment, or bring happiness. He quotes al-Ghazali's classification: these sciences are either true but useless, or false opinions that are sinful. The mathematical sciences are the former, and speculative theology and astrology are the latter. Then, Ibn Taymiyya discusses why people still engage in mathematics: (1) the soul takes pleasure in learning, (2) it trains the mind in correct reasoning, and (3) it provides relaxation. He gives historical examples: early philosophers taught their children mathematics, and later scholars turned to it out of frustration with theological disputes. He notes that many of his contemporaries occupy themselves with fara'id and mathematics in their free time, citing a hadith of Umar encouraging speaking about inheritance shares. The overall point is that while these sciences may be true and beneficial for the mind, they are not the ultimate knowledge that saves or perfects the soul.

Key Terms
تصحيح الذهن
العلوم الرياضية
علم الفرائض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Taymiyya mean by 'correction of the mind' (tas-hih al-dhihn) in connection with mathematics?
A) Mathematics corrects errors in theology.
B) Mathematics trains the soul in correct knowledge and sound reasoning.
C) Mathematics replaces the need for revelation.
D) Mathematics is the only way to attain truth.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges that mathematics offers real benefits—pleasure, mental training, and relaxation—but insists these do not constitute spiritual salvation.

Benefits of Exact Sciences: Pleasure and Mental Training
Ibn Taymiyya on benefits of mathematical sciences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's view on the psychological and cognitive benefits of mathematical sciences and identify the condition he attaches to their pursuit.

Core Idea
Exact sciences train the mind but must not distract from higher knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges that the soul delights in learning new things, much like in play. He further notes that persistent engagement with mathematical sciences accustoms the mind to correct knowledge and straight reasoning. But he warns that this benefit is conditional: it should not preoccupy one from what is more important. This lesson unpacks these benefits and their limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the benefits listed
Read the passage and list the three main benefits Ibn Taymiyya attributes to mathematical sciences: (1) psychological pleasure in learning, (2) habit of correct knowledge and truthful propositions, (3) correction of the mind and perception.
The pleasure is compared to amusement, implying it is not the highest form.
2
Analyze the condition attached
Observe that the pleasure is enjoyed 'if not occupied by what is more important.' So the benefit is contingent on proper prioritization. What does 'more important' refer to? It suggests religious sciences or foundational knowledge.
This condition is key to Ibn Taymiyya's overall critique.
3
Examine the training effect
Consider how 'persisting' in mathematical sciences trains the soul in correct knowledge and straight syllogism. This is a cognitive benefit that can be transferred to other sciences.
Ibn Taymiyya views this as preparation for higher knowledge, not an end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأسباب المغربة بالاشتغال بالعلم الرياضي وما أشبهه التذاذ النفس : لكن قد تلتذ النفس كما تلتذ بغير ذلك. فإن الإنسان يلتذ بعلم ما لم يكن علمه، وسماع ما لم يكن سمعه ، إذا لم يكن مشغولاً عن ذلك بما هو أهم عنده منه، كما قد يلتذ بأنواع من الأفعال التي هي من جنس اللهو واللعب.
وأيضاً ففي الإدمان على معرفة ذلك تعتاد النفس العلم الصحيح، والقضايا الصادقة، والقياس المستقيم، فيكون في ذلك تصحيح الذهن والإدراك، وتعويد النفس أنها تعلم الحق وتقوله لتستعين بذلك على المعرفة التي هي فوق ذلك .
ولهذا يقال : إنه كان أوائل الفلاسفة أول ما يعلمون أولادهم العلم الرياضي. وكثير من شيوخهم في آخر أمره إنما يشتغل بذلك، لأنه لما نظر في طرقهم وطرق من عارضهم من أهل الكلام الباطل لم يجد في ذلك ما هو حق، أخذ يشغل نفسه بالعلم الرياضي، كما كان يجري مثل ذلك لمن هو من أئمة الفلاسفة كابن واصل ، وغيره .
وكذلك كثير من متأخري أصحابنا يشتغلون وقت بطالتهم بعلم الفرائض، والحساب والجبر والمقابلة والهندسة ونحو ذلك، لأن فيه تفريحاً للنفس، وهو علم صحيح لا يدخل فيه غلط .
وقد جاء عن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه أنه قال : «إذا لهوتم فالهوا بالرمي، وإذا تحدثتم فتحدثوا بالفرائض ) . فإن حساب الفرائض علم معقول مبني على أصل مشروع ، فتبقى فيه رياضة العقل وحفظ الشرع . لكن ليس هو علماً يطلب لذاته، ولا تكمل به النفس .
The reasons that motivate engagement with mathematical science and the like: psychological pleasure: but the soul may take pleasure just as it takes pleasure in other things. For a person takes pleasure in knowing what he did not know, and hearing what he did not hear, if he is not occupied by what is more important to him than that, just as he may take pleasure in types of actions that are of the kind of amusement and play.
Also, by persisting in knowing that, the soul becomes accustomed to correct knowledge, truthful propositions, and straight syllogism, so in that there is correction of the mind and perception, and training the soul to know and speak the truth in order to use that for knowledge that is above that.
Hence it is said: The early philosophers used to first teach their children mathematical science. And many of their later elders occupy themselves with it at the end of their lives, because when they looked at their methods and the methods of those who opposed them from the people of false theology, they did not find any truth in that, so they began to occupy themselves with mathematical science, as happened to some of the leading philosophers like Ibn Wasil and others.
Similarly, many of our later scholars occupy themselves in their spare time with the science of inheritance, arithmetic, algebra, geometry, and the like, because in it there is entertainment for the soul, and it is a correct science in which no error enters.
And it has been reported from Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'When you amuse yourselves, amuse yourselves with archery; and when you converse, converse about inheritance shares.' For the calculation of inheritance shares is a rational science built on a legitimate foundation, so there remains in it the exercise of the mind and the preservation of the Sharia. But it is not a science sought for its own sake, nor does the soul attain perfection through it.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a critical reflection on the value of mathematical sciences. Ibn Taymiyya begins by noting that the soul can take pleasure in learning, just as in amusement, but he implies that this pleasure is not the highest purpose. Then he points out that persistent engagement with exact sciences trains the mind in correct knowledge and sound reasoning, which can be a preparation for higher knowledge. He cites the example of early philosophers teaching mathematics to their children and later occupying themselves with it when disillusioned with false theology. Then he shifts to the practice of later Muslim scholars who engage in inheritance calculation (fara'id) and mathematics in their spare time, noting that this provides mental exercise and preserves the Sharia, but it is not an end in itself. He quotes Umar ibn al-Khattab to support the value of fara'id as a lawful intellectual exercise. The overall tone is that such sciences are useful tools but not ultimate knowledge.

Key Terms
القياس المستقيم
التذاذ النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) One must not be occupied by what is more important.
C) One must study them for the sake of worldly gain.
D) One must first master Greek logic.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya affirms that exact sciences offer real mental benefits, but only as a means; the ultimate goal is higher revealed knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning, construct a brief argument explaining why a Muslim student should not spend all his time on advanced mathematics at the expense of learning the obligatory sciences of Sharia.
?
Identify a modern equivalent of 'knowledge that is above that'—what higher knowledge does Ibn Taymiyya refer to? Write one sentence explaining how mathematical sciences can serve that higher knowledge.
