The Proper Place: Sciences as Means, Not Ends
Ibn Taymiyya on the limited value of fara'id as a science

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate Ibn Taymiyya's critical distinction between sciences that are means and sciences that are ends, using his example of the science of inheritance (fara'id) and the saying of Umar ibn al-Khattab.

Core Idea
Even Sharia-based rational sciences are not ends; they serve higher purposes.

In the second half of the passage, Ibn Taymiyya applies his critique to a specific Islamic science: al-fara'id (inheritance shares). He notes that later Muslim scholars engage in it for its entertainment value and because it is free of error, but it is not sought for itself. He quotes Umar ibn al-Khattab to support the idea that such sciences are a lawful amusement and mental exercise, but not the perfection of the soul. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the science and its description
Find where Ibn Taymiyya mentions 'fara'id' and list the adjectives he uses for it: 'rational science' (ma'qul), 'built on a legitimate foundation' (mabni 'ala asl mashru'). Note that he says it contains 'exercise of the mind and preservation of the Sharia'.
Despite these positive qualities, he denies it is sought for itself or perfects the soul.
2
Extract the saying of Umar
Read Umar's saying: 'When you amuse yourselves, amuse yourselves with archery; when you converse, converse about inheritance shares.' How does this support Ibn Taymiyya's point? It places fara'id in the category of permissible conversation and mental engagement, not ultimate pursuit.
Archery is a physical amusement; fara'id is a mental amusement with Sharia benefit.
3
Derive the principle
From the contrast with 'knowledge that is above that' in the first part, and the statement that fara'id 'is not a science sought for its own sake, nor does the soul attain perfection through it,' formulate the principle: there are instrumental sciences and ultimate sciences.
This principle is a critical refinement of overvaluing rational disciplines.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Logic's Greek Origins
Author traces the origin of logic to geometry and criticizes Greek pagan foundations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Greek origins of logic and identify his alternative Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
Logic's Greek origin in geometry reveals its pagan and indirect nature, contrasting with direct Islamic knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya traces the historical roots of logic to ancient Greek polytheism and geometry. By linking logic to planet worship and geometrical shapes, he argues that Greek logic is an inferior, indirect path to knowledge. This lesson unpacks his historical critique and the epistemological alternative he presents for Muslims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical context
Note that Ibn Taymiyya describes the Greeks as polytheists who worshipped planets, used sorcery, and built temples. They observed planetary magnitudes and conjunctions for their invocations.
This sets the stage: logic emerges from a pagan, astrological milieu.
2
Trace the link from planets to geometry to logic
See how spherical orbits required geometry, and from geometry they derived logical 'shapes' and 'terms' (hudud) to transfer from sensible to intelligible forms.
The progression: planets → geometry → logic, all under a pagan worldview.
3
Understand the criticism: weak intellects and indirect path
Ibn Taymiyya claims the Greeks created this system because of weak intellects and difficulty in knowing truth directly, so they needed sensible aids.
He contrasts this with the Islamic method of direct knowledge through faith and righteous action.
4
Contrast with the Islamic alternative
Identify how Ibn Taymiyya asserts that Allah has given Muslims knowledge and clear exposition, surpassing all human kinds through righteous action and faith.
The critique is not just historical but epistemological: Muslims have a superior, direct path.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأولئك المشركون كانوا يعبدون الكواكب، ويبنون لها الهياكل، ويدعونها بأنواع الاستعانة على معرفة الدعوات، كما هو معروف من أخبارهم وما صنف على طريقهم من الكتب الموضوعة في الشرك، والسحر، ودعوة الكواكب، والعزائم والأقسام التي بها يعظم إبليس وجنوده. وكان الشيطان بسبب السحر والشرك يغويهم بأشياء هي التي دعتهم إلى ذلك الشرك والسحر. فكانوا يرصدون الكواكب ليتعلموا مقاديرها ومقادير حركاتها، وما بين بعضها وبعض من الاتصالات، ليستعينوا بذلك على ما يرونه مناسباً لها .
ولما كانت الأفلاك مستديرة، ولم يكن معرفة حسابها إلا بمعرفة الهندسة معرفة الخطوط المنحنية والمستقيمة، تكلموا في الهندسة لذلك، ولعمارة الدنيا . وعمارة الدنيا فلهذا صاروا يتوسعون في ذلك . وإلا فلو لم يتعلق بذلك غرض إلا مجرد تصور الأعداد والمقادير : لم تكن هذه الغاية مما يوجب طلبها بالسعي المذكور.
وربما كانت هذه غاية لبعض الناس الذين يتلذذون بذلك؛ فإن لذات النفوس أنواع. ومنهم من يلتذ بالشطرنج، والنرد، والقمار، حتى يشغله ذلك عما هو أنفع له منه من الهندسة .
فكان مبدأ وضع المنطق من الهندسة. فجعلوه أشكالاً كالأشكال الهندسية، وسموه حدوداً كحدود تلك الأشكال، لينتقلوا من الشكل المحسوس إلى الشكل المعقول . وهذا لضعف عقولهم وتعذر المعرفة عليهم إلا بالطريق البعيدة .
والله تعالى قد يسر للمسلمين من العلم والبيان مع العمل الصالح والإيمان، ما برزوا به على كل نوع من أنواع جنس الإنسان، والحمد لله رب العالمين.
And those polytheists used to worship the planets, build temples for them, and call upon them with various means of seeking help in knowing invocations, as is known from their reports and the books composed according to their method, placed in polytheism, sorcery, planetary invocation, spells and oaths by which Iblis and his hosts are magnified. And Satan, through sorcery and polytheism, used to seduce them with things that called them to that polytheism and sorcery. So they used to observe the planets to learn their magnitudes, the magnitudes of their movements, and the conjunctions between them, in order to seek help by that in what they deemed appropriate for them.
And since the spheres were circular, and knowledge of their calculation could only be through knowledge of geometry—knowledge of curved and straight lines—they spoke about geometry for that reason, and for the building of the world. And the building of the world, so they expanded in that. Otherwise, if no purpose were attached to that except mere conceptualization of numbers and quantities, then this end would not necessitate seeking it with the aforementioned effort.
And perhaps this is an end for some people who take pleasure in that; for the pleasures of souls are of various kinds. Among them there are those who take pleasure in chess, backgammon, and gambling, until that distracts them from what is more beneficial for them than geometry.
Thus the origin of the establishment of logic was from geometry. They made it into shapes like geometrical shapes, and they called them terms like the terms of those shapes, so that they might transfer from the sensible shape to the intelligible shape. And this is due to the weakness of their intellects and the difficulty of knowledge for them except by a remote path.
And Allah the Exalted has made easy for the Muslims knowledge and clear exposition, with righteous action and faith, with which they have excelled every type of the human species. And praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a critical historical exposition of the origins of logic, linking it to pagan star worship. Ibn Taymiyya describes how the polytheists worshipped planets, built temples, and practiced sorcery. To aid their invocations, they observed planetary magnitudes, movements, and conjunctions. Since the celestial spheres are circular, calculation required geometry, so they studied geometry for astrological purposes and for worldly construction. He notes that if the only aim were conceptualizing numbers, such effort would be unjustified; in fact, it often becomes mere pleasure like gambling. The key claim is that logic originated from geometry: logicians adopted geometrical shapes and terms (hudud) to formulate logical forms, moving from sensible to intelligible shapes. Ibn Taymiyya attributes this to the weakness of Greek intellects, which required a long, indirect path. He contrasts this with Allah's gift to Muslims of direct knowledge and clear exposition through faith and righteous action, surpassing all humanity.

Key Terms
المنطق
الهندسة
الحدود

A) Because geometry is the most certain science.

C) To improve their architectural skills.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that logic's origin in geometry, tied to pagan star worship, renders it an inferior, indirect path to knowledge compared to the direct knowledge granted by Allah to Muslims.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of Philosophers on the Unseen
Ibn Taymiyya attacks philosophers' inability to affirm the unseen and their reduction of prophetic teachings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's epistemic objection to the philosophers' concept of the unseen and distinguish his critique from outright rejection of reason.

Core Idea
Philosophers' 'unseen' is mental; prophets' unseen is real.

Ibn Taymiyya here tackles a fundamental epistemological divide between the philosophers and the prophets. He argues that the philosophers' claim to knowledge of the unseen is hollow, based on mental constructs rather than external reality. This lesson walks through his critique, focusing on his distinction between the intellectual world and the genuine unseen affirmed by revelation.

Guided Path
1
Locate the epistemic claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that the philosophers have no proof that the world is eternal. He says their arguments only indicate perpetuity of types, not specific things. This reveals the weak foundation of their universal knowledge.
Look at the sentence: 'ليس معهم دليل أصلاً يدلهم على أن العالم لم يزل ولا يزال هكذا'.
2
Understand the mental vs. real distinction
Ibn Taymiyya dismisses the philosophers' 'intellectual world' as mere mental posits, not external existents. Contrast this with the prophets' information about real entities like Allah, angels, and the resurrection, which are more perfect than sensed things.
Focus on the statement: 'إن ما يثبتونه من المعقولات إنما يعود عند التحقيق إلى أمور مقدرة في الأذهان، لا موجودة في الأعيان'.
3
Analyze the reconciliation argument
Ibn Taymiyya exposes how philosophers, overwhelmed by the truth of prophethood, claimed that prophets addressed the masses using imaginative representations. He shows this demeans prophetic knowledge.
Identify the phrase: 'قالوا : إن الرسل قصدوا إخبار الجمهور بما يتخيَّل إليهم'.
4
Evaluate the conclusion on universal judgments
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that the philosophers' universal judgments (e.g., types being eternal) are ignorance, not knowledge, because they are based on a false assumption of the world's eternity. He ties this to their rejection of prophetic reports.
See the final part: 'عُلم أن غاية ما عندهم من الأحكام الكلية ليست مطابقة، بل هي جهل، لا علم'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الوجه الخامس : أقوال الفلاسفة في الغيب وفي الرسل

فإذا عرفت الأقسام فليس فيها ما هو علم بمعلوم لا يقبل التغيّر والاستحالة. فإن «الممكن» ليس بواجب هذه الأقسام عامتها إنما هو في هذا العالم وكل ذلك يقبل التغير والاستحالة. وليس معهم دليل أصلاً يدلهم على أن العالم لم يزل ولا يزال هكذا. وجميع ما يحتجون به على دوام الفاعل والفاعلية والزمان، والحركة، وتوابع ذلك، فإنما يدل على قِدَم نوع ذلك ودوامه، لا على قدم شيء معين ولا دوام شيء معين. فالجزم بأن مدلول تلك الأدلة هو هذا العالم أو شيء منه جهل محض لا مستند له إلا عدم العلم بموجود غير هذا العالم. وعدم العلم ليس علماً بالعدم.

ولهذا لم يكن عند القوم إيمان بالغيب الذي أخبرت به الأنبياء. فهم لا يؤمنون، لا بالله، ولا ملائكته ولا كُتُبه، ولا رسله، ولا البعث بعد الموت.

وإذا قالوا: نحن نثبت العالم العقلي» أو «المعقول الخارج عن العالم المحسوس»، وذلك هو «الغيب»، فإن هذا - وإن كان قد ذكره طائفة من المتكلمة والمتفلسفة - خطأ وضلال. فإن ما يثبتونه من المعقولات إنما يعود عند التحقيق إلى أمور مقدرة في الأذهان، لا موجودة في الأعيان والرسل أخبرت عما هو موجود في الخارج، وهو أكمل وأعظم وجوداً مما نَشْهده في الدنيا. فأين هذا من هذا ؟

وهم لما كانوا مكذبين بما أخبرت به الرسل في نفس الأمر، واحتاجوا إلى الجمع بين قولهم وبين تصديق الرسل لِمَا بَهَرَهم من أمر الرسل، قالوا : إن الرسل قصدوا إخبار الجمهور بما يتخيَّل إليهم لينتفعوا بذلك في العدل الذي أقاموه لهم.

ثم منهم من يقول : إن الرسل عرفت ما عرفناه من نفى هذه الأمور»؛ ومنهم من يقول : بل لم يكونوا يعرفون هذا، وإنما كان كمالهم في القوة العملية، لا النظرية.

وأقلُّ أتباع الرسل إذا تصوَّر حقيقة ما عندهم وجده مما لا يرضى به أقل أتباع الرسل. وإذا عُلم بالأدلة العقلية أن هذا العالم يمتنع أن يكون شيء منه قديماً أزلياً، وعلم بأخبار الأنبياء المؤيدة بالعقل أنه كان قبله عالم آخر منه خُلق، وأنه سوف يستحيل، وتقوم القيامة، ونحو ذلك، عُلم أن غاية ما عندهم من الأحكام الكلية ليست مطابقة، بل هي جهل، لا علم . وهب أنهم لم يعلموا ما أخبرت به الرسل، فليس في العقل ما يوجب ما ادعوه من كون هذه الأنواع الكلية التي في هذا العالم أزلية أبدية لم تزل ولا تزال. فلا يكون العلم بذلك علماً بكليات ثابتة . وعامة فلسفتهم الأولى وحكمتهم العليا من هذا النمط.
Third Station - Fifth Aspect: Statements of the Philosophers Concerning the Unseen and the Messengers

So if you know the divisions, there is nothing in them which is knowledge of an object that does not accept change and transformation. For 'the possible' is not necessary; the majority of these divisions are only in this world, and all of that accepts change and transformation. And there is no proof at all with them that indicates that the world has always been and will always be like this. All that they argue with for the perpetuity of the agent and effectivity and time and motion and their corollaries only indicates the eternity of the type of that and its perpetuity, not the eternity of any specific thing nor the perpetuity of any specific thing. So the assertion that the implication of those proofs is this world or something from it is pure ignorance, with no basis except lack of knowledge of an existent other than this world. And lack of knowledge is not knowledge of non-existence.

For this reason, these people had no faith in the unseen which the prophets informed about. They do not believe in Allah, nor His angels, nor His books, nor His messengers, nor the resurrection after death.

And when they say: 'We affirm the intellectual world' or 'the intelligible external to the sensible world', and that is 'the unseen' — this, although a group of theologians and philosopher-theologians have mentioned it — is an error and misguidance. For what they affirm of intelligibles, upon investigation, only returns to matters posited in minds, not existing in external entities, whereas the messengers informed about what exists externally, and it is more perfect and greater in existence than what we witness in this world. So where is this compared to that?

And because they were deniers of what the messengers informed about in actual fact, and they needed to reconcile between their own statement and believing the messengers due to being overwhelmed by the matter of the messengers, they said: The messengers intended to inform the masses with what they imagined, so that they might benefit by that in the justice they established for them.

Then among them are those who say: The messengers knew what we knew of denying these matters; and among them are those who say: Rather they did not know this, and their perfection was only in the practical power, not the theoretical.

And the least of the followers of the messengers, if he imagines the reality of what they (the philosophers) have, finds it something that the least of the followers of the messengers would not be pleased with. And when it is known by rational proofs that it is impossible for anything of this world to be eternal and pre-eternal, and it is known by the reports of the prophets supported by reason that before it there was another world from which it was created, and that it will undergo transformation, and the resurrection will occur, and similar things, then it is known that the utmost of what they have of universal judgments is not correct, but rather is ignorance, not knowledge. And even if they did not know what the prophets informed about, there is nothing in reason that necessitates what they claim of these universal types in this world being eternal and everlasting, never ceasing and never ending. So knowledge of that is not knowledge of fixed universals. And most of their first philosophy and their highest wisdom is of this pattern.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation from Ibn Taymiyya directed against the philosophers (falasifa) regarding their understanding of the unseen (ghayb) and their attitude towards the prophets. He argues that their alleged rational knowledge is actually ignorance because it is based on a limited view confined to this world, which is subject to change. Their arguments for eternity only prove the eternity of types, not specific individuals, and they wrongly conclude that this world is eternal. Consequently, they lack faith in the unseen that the prophets conveyed. Ibn Taymiyya then addresses the philosophers' claim that the 'intellectual world' or 'intelligibles' constitute the unseen; he dismisses this as a mistake, because these are mere mental constructs, not external realities. In contrast, the prophets spoke of real external entities that are more perfect than what we perceive. Ibn Taymiyya further critiques the philosophers' attempts to reconcile their denials with the apparent truth of prophethood by claiming that the prophets addressed the masses with imaginative representations. He rebuts this by stating that even the least follower of the prophets would reject this view. Finally, he asserts that rational proofs and prophetic reports together demonstrate that this world is not eternal, and that the philosophers' universal judgments are false—they are ignorance, not knowledge. The passage concludes by noting that the entire enterprise of first philosophy (metaphysics) is flawed in this regard.

Key Terms
الغيب
العالم العقلي
الأحكام الكلية

B) Because they claim that the universal types in this world are eternal, which is not supported by reason or revelation.

D) Because they deny the existence of the world altogether.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique exposes that the philosophers' claim to knowledge of the unseen is based on mental constructs, not external reality, and that true knowledge of the unseen comes from prophetic revelation.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Ibn Sina's Sources
Ibn Taymiyya discusses Ibn Sina's unoriginal ideas and his borrowing from Muslims and Ismaili heretics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's strategy of discrediting Ibn Sina by tracing his philosophical ideas to heretical Ismaili sources rather than engaging in logical refutation.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya attacks Ibn Sina's originality via source criticism, not logic.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya moves from logical critique to historical polemic. He argues that Ibn Sina's ideas on prophethood, resurrection, and divine matters were not original but derived from Muslims and, crucially, from Ismaili heretics. By linking Ibn Sina personally to the Batiniyya, Ibn Taymiyya aims to undermine the authority of his philosophical system. This lesson unpacks that strategy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target
Find the subject of Ibn Taymiyya's attack. He begins with a general category of thinkers who partially follow the philosophers, then focuses specifically on Ibn Sina.
The target is not just Ibn Sina but his followers who repeat his words.
2
Extract the claim about Ibn Sina's originality
Ibn Taymiyya states that Ibn Sina spoke about things his philosophical predecessors did not. This sets up the question: where did these new ideas come from?
3
Note the dual attribution
Ibn Taymiyya says Ibn Sina 'derived them from the Muslims' but also 'took from the heretics who trace themselves to the Muslims, like the Ismailis.' This dual attribution is key: it both gives credit to Islam and taints the source.
The phrase 'إن كان إنما أخذ' indicates a concessive clause: 'although he took from heretics.'
4
Identify the personal connection
Ibn Taymiyya adds that Ibn Sina's family were callers of the Ismaili da'wa and followers of the heretical Fatimid caliph al-Hakim. This personal link strengthens the accusation of heretical influence.
This is an ad hominem argument in the historical context.
5
Assess the polemical purpose
Recognize that Ibn Taymiyya is not just stating facts; he is using source criticism to discredit Ibn Sina's philosophical authority, implying his ideas are contaminated by heresy.
This is a common tactic in Islamic polemics (radd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الوجه الخامس : بعض أحوال الرئيس ابن سينا والفرقة الباطنية وأمثال هؤلاء ممن لم يجرّد القول لنصر مذهبهم مطلقاً، ولا تخلّص من أشراك ضلالهم مطلقاً، بل شاركهم في كثير من ضلالهم، وشككهم في كثير من محالهم، وتخلص من بعض وبالهم، وإن كان أيضاً لم يُنصفهم في بعض ما أصابوا فيه، وأخطأ لعدم علمه بمرادهم، أو لعدم معرفته أن ما قالوه صواب. مآخِذُ علوم أبى علي ابن سينا، وشيء من أحواله ثم إن هؤلاء إنما يتبعون كلام ابن سينا. وابن سينا تكلم في أشياء من الإلهيات والنبويات والمعاد، والشرائع، لم يتكلم فيها سلفه، ولا وصلت إليها عقولهم، ولا بلغتها علومهم . فإنه استفادها من المسلمين، وإن كان إنما أخذ عن الملاحدة المنتسبين إلى المسلمين كالإسماعيلية وكان أهل بيته من أهل دعوتهم من أتباع الحاكم العُبَيْدِي الذي كان هو وأهل بيته وأتباعه معروفين عند المسلمين بالإلحاد.
The third station - the fifth aspect: Some conditions of the chief Ibn Sina and the Batiniyya sect. And the likes of those who did not completely devote their speech to supporting their doctrine, nor did they entirely extricate themselves from the snares of their misguidance, but rather shared in much of their misguidance, and doubted much of their absurdities, and extricated themselves from some of their burden, although they also did not treat them fairly in some matters in which they were correct, and erred due to their lack of knowledge of their intention, or due to their ignorance that what they said was correct. [Heading] The sources of the sciences of Abu Ali Ibn Sina, and something of his conditions. Then these people only follow the speech of Ibn Sina. And Ibn Sina spoke about things in theology, prophethood, resurrection, and religious laws, which his predecessors did not speak about, nor did their intellects reach them, nor did their sciences attain them. For he derived them from the Muslims, although he took from the heretics who trace themselves to the Muslims, like the Ismailis, and his family were among their callers, followers of al-Hakim al-'Ubaydi, who was, along with his family and followers, known among the Muslims for heresy.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition of Ibn Sina's intellectual origins and the nature of his followers. It begins with a heading indicating it is the third station, fifth aspect, concerning Ibn Sina and the Batiniyya (esoteric) sect. Ibn Taymiyya describes a category of thinkers who neither fully endorse the philosophers' doctrines nor completely extricate themselves from their errors—they share in misguidance, doubt absurdities, but free themselves from some burdens. Yet they also fail to acknowledge the philosophers' correct points and make errors due to misunderstanding or ignorance. Then he turns specifically to Ibn Sina, stating that his followers merely repeat his words. Ibn Sina spoke about divine matters, prophethood, resurrection, and religious laws in ways that his predecessors among the philosophers did not. Ibn Taymiyya claims that Ibn Sina derived these from Muslims but also took from heretics who affiliate with Muslims, namely the Ismailis. He notes that Ibn Sina's family were themselves callers of the Ismaili da'wa, followers of al-Hakim bi-Amr Allah, a Fatimid caliph known for heresy. This argument is meant to discredit Ibn Sina's originality and orthodoxy, suggesting his ideas are not purely philosophical but borrowed from sources Muslims consider deviant.

Key Terms
الفرقة الباطنية
الملاحدة
الحاكم العبيدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) From the Muslims and also from Ismaili heretics.
C) From his own purely rational investigations.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya discredits Ibn Sina not by logical argument but by tracing his ideas to heretical sources, a powerful polemical move in Islamic intellectual debates.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Philosophers' Ignorance of the Divine
Author argues philosophers are more ignorant of God than Jews, Christians, and Muslim heretics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the philosophers' ignorance of divine science and explain his concession regarding their natural sciences.

Core Idea
Philosophers are masters of physics but fools of metaphysics.

Ibn Taymiyya delivers a devastating verdict on the philosophers, yet he does not dismiss their entire enterprise. He acknowledges their achievements in natural sciences while reserving his sharpest criticism for their divine science. This lesson dissects his strategy: how he defends religious knowledge of God against philosophical pretensions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target group
Find the specific philosophers being criticized. Ibn Taymiyya names Aristotle and his followers as the primary targets.
He also implicitly includes Ibn Sina by referring to 'these Muslims to whom he (Ibn Sina) used to attribute himself'.
2
Extract the comparative claim
Identify the groups Ibn Taymiyya compares the philosophers to: Jews, Christians, and Muslim innovators (ahl al-bid'a). He claims the philosophers are more ignorant than all of them.
This is a strong rhetorical move: even heretical Muslims know God better than the philosophers.
3
Locate the specific evidence
Find the reference to Aristotle's Metaphysics (Book Lambda) and note that Ibn Taymiyya claims to have demonstrated its ignorance.
This is not just assertion; he claims to have provided a detailed critique elsewhere.
4
Identify the concession
Find where Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges the philosophers' competence in natural sciences (al-ṭabīʿiyyāt). He says their speech there is 'mostly good' and abundant.
This concession makes his critique more targeted and credible.
5
Summarize the overall verdict
State the final judgment: the philosophers are ignorant of divine science to the utmost, possessing only a little with much error.
This establishes why Islamic theology (kalam) is superior to falsafah in the highest matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهؤلاء المسلمون الذين كان ينتسب إليهم، وهم مع الإلحاد الظاهر والكفر الباطن أعلم بالله المبتدعون أعلم بالله من سلفه الفلاسفة، كأرسطو وأتباعه. فإن أولئك ليس عندهم من العلم بالله إلا ما من الفلاسفة عند عُبَّاد مشركي العرب ما هو خير منه. وقد ذكرت كلامَ أرسطو نفسه الذي ذكره في علم ما بعد الطبيعة في «مقالة اللام»، وغيرها، وهو آخر منتهى فلسفته، وبينت بعض ما فيه من الجهل. فإنه ليس في الطوائف المعروفين الذين يتكلمون في العلم الإلهي مع الخطأ والضلال مثل علماء اليهود والنصارى وأهل البدع من المسلمين وغيرهم أجهلُ من هؤلاء، ولا أبعد عن العلم بالله تعالى منهم. نعم لهم في «الطبيعيات» كلام غالبه جيد، وهو كلام كثير واسع. ولهم عقول عرفوا بها ذلك، وهم قد يقصدون الحق، لا يظهر عليهم العناد. لكنهم جهال بـ «العلم الإلهي» إلى الغاية، ليس عندهم منه إلا قليل كثير الخطأ.
And these Muslims to whom he [Ibn Sina] used to attribute himself, while they have apparent heresy and hidden disbelief, are more knowledgeable of God. The innovators are more knowledgeable of God than his predecessors the philosophers, like Aristotle and his followers. For those have no knowledge of God except something less than what the philosophers have among the idol-worshipping Arabs. And I have mentioned the very words of Aristotle which he mentioned in 'Metaphysics' in the 'Article Lam', and elsewhere, and it is the furthest end of his philosophy, and I have shown some of the ignorance in it. For there is not among the known sects who speak about divine science with error and misguidance, like the scholars of the Jews and Christians and the people of innovation among the Muslims and others, anyone more ignorant than these, nor further from knowledge of God Almighty than them. Yes, they have in 'natural sciences' speech most of which is good, and it is abundant and extensive speech. And they have intellects by which they recognized that, and they often intend the truth, stubbornness does not appear from them. But they are ignorant of 'divine science' to the utmost, having only a little with much error.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a critical attack on the philosophers, particularly Aristotle and his followers, regarding their lack of true knowledge of God. Ibn Taymiyya begins by comparing them unfavorably to the Muslims (Ibn Sina's claimed affiliation) and even to innovators (ahl al-bid'a), asserting that these heretical groups have more knowledge of God than the philosophers. He then narrows in on Aristotle, referencing his Metaphysics (article Lam) as the pinnacle of his philosophy, and claims to have demonstrated its ignorance. Importantly, Ibn Taymiyya makes a concession: the philosophers excel in natural sciences (al-tabi'iyyat), their work there is largely good and based on sound intellect without evident stubbornness. However, in divine science (al-'ilm al-ilahi), they are utterly ignorant, possessing only a little that is riddled with error. The structure is a classic polemical move: acknowledge partial competence in one domain while delivering a devastating verdict in the domain that matters most—knowledge of God. This establishes the epistemological basis for Ibn Taymiyya's broader critique: Greek philosophy fails precisely where it claims authority, i.e., metaphysics.

Key Terms
العلم الإلهي
الطبيعيات
مقالة اللام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, how do the philosophers compare to other groups in knowledge of God?
A) Philosophers are the most knowledgeable about God.
B) Philosophers are more ignorant than Jews, Christians, and Muslim innovators.
C) Philosophers and Christians are equally ignorant.
D) Philosophers are superior to Muslim innovators.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique is precise: he does not reject all philosophy but identifies the philosophers' failure precisely where they claim authority—divine science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Identify a modern claim about 'science vs. religion' and apply Ibn Taymiyya's method: concede the empirical domain while critiquing metaphysical overreach.

Philosophers' Ignorance of Divine Science
Critique of philosophers' ignorance of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of philosophers' ignorance of God and distinguish it from their competence in natural sciences.

Core Idea
Philosophers are masters of nature but ignorant of God.

Ibn Taymiyya launches a sharp critique against the philosophers, especially Aristotle and his followers, claiming they have no true knowledge of God. He compares them unfavorably to other groups that speak on divine science, such as Jews, Christians, and Muslim innovators. This lesson unpacks Ibn Taymiyya's argument and its implications for evaluating philosophical claims about God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
Find the specific group Ibn Taymiyya is criticizing. Look for the names Aristotle and his followers, and the term 'philosophers' (الفلاسفة).
The critique is directed at the Hellenistic philosophical tradition, not all philosophy.
2
Extract the comparative claim
Ibn Taymiyya compares the philosophers to other groups (Jews, Christians, Muslim innovators) regarding knowledge of God. What does he say about their relative ignorance?
He says they are more ignorant than all these groups.
3
Analyze the distinction between natural and divine sciences
Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges the philosophers have good work in natural sciences, but claims they are extremely ignorant in divine science. Why does he make this distinction?
This shows the critique is not total rejection; it focuses on their competence in metaphysics.
4
Assess the epistemological basis
What does Ibn Taymiyya say about the philosophers' intentions and methods? He notes they sometimes intend truth and are not stubborn. What does this imply about their epistemological status?
Their error is not due to malice but to reliance on faulty principles in divine matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهؤلاء المسلمون الذين كان ينتسب إليهم، وهم مع الإلحاد الظاهر والكفر الباطن أعلم بالله المبتدعون أعلم بالله من سلفه الفلاسفة، كأرسطو وأتباعه. فإن أولئك ليس عندهم من العلم بالله إلا ما من الفلاسفة عند عُبَّاد مشركي العرب ما هو خير منه. وقد ذكرت كلامَ أرسطو نفسه الذي ذكره في علم ما بعد الطبيعة في مقالة اللام، وغيرها، وهو آخر منتهى فلسفته، وبينت بعض ما فيه من الجهل. فإنه ليس في الطوائف المعروفين الذين يتكلمون في العلم الإلهي مع الخطأ والضلال مثل علماء اليهود والنصارى وأهل البدع من المسلمين وغيرهم أجهلُ من هؤلاء، ولا أبعد عن العلم بالله تعالى منهم. نعم لهم في الطبيعيات كلام غالبه جيد، وهو كلام كثير واسع. ولهم عقول عرفوا وهم قد يقصدون الحقِّ، لا يظهر عليهم العناد. لكنهم جهال بالعلم الإلهي إلى الغاية، ليس عندهم منه إلا قليل كثير الخطأ. وابن سينا لَمَّا عَرَف شيئاً من دين المسلمين ـ وكان قد تلقى ما تلقاه عن وعمن هو خير منهم من المعتزلة والرافضة - أراد أن يجمع بين ما عرفه بعقله من هؤلاء وبين ما أخذه من سلفه. فتكلم في الفلسفة بكلام مركَّب من كلام سلفه ومما أحدثه، مثل كلامه في النبوات وأسرار الآيات والمنامات بل وكلامه في بعض الطبيعيات والمنطقيات، وكلامه في واجب الوجود، ونحو ذلك. وإلا، فأرسطو وأتباعه ليس في كلامهم ذكر واجب الوجود، ولا شيء من الأحكام التي لـ واجب الوجود. وإنما يذكرون العلة الأولى، ويُثبتونه من حيث هو للحركة الفلكية بتحرك الفلك للتشبه به. فابن سينا أصلَحَ تلك الفلسفة الفاسدة بعض إصلاح، حتى راجَتْ على من لم يعرف دين الإسلام من الطلبة النظّار. وصاروا يظهرُ لهم بعضُ ما فيها من التناقض. فيتكلم كل منهم بحسب ما عنده ولكن سَلَّموا لهم أصولاً فاسدة في المنطق والطبيعيات والإلهيات، ولم يعرفوا ما دخل فيها من الباطل. فصار ذلك سبباً إلى ضلالهم في مطالب عالية إيمانية ومقاصد سامية قرآنية، خرجوا بها عن حقيقة العلم والإيمان، وصاروا بها في كثير من ذلك لا يسمعون ولا يعقلون، بل يتسَفْسَطون في العقليات، ويقر مطون في السمعيات .
These Muslims who claim affiliation to them, while they are with apparent atheism and hidden disbelief, are more knowledgeable of God than the innovators are more knowledgeable of God than their predecessors the philosophers, like Aristotle and his followers. For those [philosophers] have no knowledge of God except what the philosophers have from the idol-worshippers of the Arabs that is better than it. And I have mentioned the statement of Aristotle himself that he mentioned in the Metaphysics in the 'Lambda' article, and others, which is the ultimate end of his philosophy, and I have shown some of the ignorance in it. For among the known groups who speak on divine science with error and misguidance, like the scholars of Jews and Christians and the innovators among Muslims and others, there is none more ignorant than these [philosophers], nor farther from knowledge of God Almighty. Yes, they have in the natural sciences speech that is mostly good, abundant and extensive. They have intellects by which they know, and they sometimes intend the truth; stubbornness does not appear from them. But they are extremely ignorant of divine science; they have nothing from it except a little with much error. And Ibn Sina, when he came to know something of the religion of the Muslims – and he had received from those better than him from the Mu'tazila and the Rawafid – he wanted to combine what he knew by his intellect from these with what he took from his predecessors. So he spoke in philosophy with a speech composed from the speech of his predecessors and from what he innovated, like his speech on prophecies, secrets of verses and dreams, and even his speech in some natural sciences and logic, and his speech on the Necessary Existent, and the like. Otherwise, Aristotle and his followers have no mention of the Necessary Existent in their speech, nor any of the judgments belonging to the Necessary Existent. They only mention the First Cause, and they affirm it from the aspect that it is the final cause for the motion of the sphere, as the sphere moves to imitate it. So Ibn Sina reformed that corrupt philosophy to some extent, until it became popular among those students and theorists who did not know the religion of Islam. And they started to see some of the contradictions in it. So each of them speaks according to what he has, but they accepted corrupt principles in logic, natural sciences, and divine sciences, and did not know the falsehood that entered into them. That became a cause for their misguidance in lofty matters of faith and sublime Qur'anic objectives, by which they departed from the reality of knowledge and faith, and became in many of that neither hearing nor understanding, but rather they engage in sophistry in intellectual matters and become confined in transmitted matters.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a critique by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophers' claims to knowledge of God, and of Avicenna's attempt to synthesize philosophy with Islam. He begins by stating that those Muslims who affiliate with the philosophers are actually more knowledgeable of God than the philosophers themselves, because the philosophers (like Aristotle) have no true knowledge of God – what they have is even less than what Arab idol-worshippers possess from philosophers. He then emphasizes that among all groups speaking on divine science (including Jews, Christians, and Muslim innovators), none are more ignorant or farther from God than these philosophers. However, he acknowledges that they have good work in natural sciences, and they sometimes seek truth, but they are extremely ignorant of divine science, possessing only a little with much error. He then turns to Ibn Sina, who learned something of Islam from Mu'tazila and Rawafid, and attempted to combine his intellectual knowledge with the philosophy of his predecessors. He produced a hybrid philosophy, speaking on prophecies, secrets of verses, dreams, natural sciences, logic, and the Necessary Existent. Ibn Taymiyya points out that Aristotle and his followers never mentioned the Necessary Existent; they only spoke of a First Cause as the final cause of celestial motion. Avicenna partially reformed this corrupt philosophy, making it popular among students unfamiliar with Islam. These students accepted corrupt principles in logic, natural sciences, and divine sciences, leading them astray from faith and true knowledge, engaging in sophistry in intellectual matters and becoming stuck in transmitted matters.

Key Terms
العلم الإلهي
الفلاسفة
الطبيعيات
أرسطو

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The philosophers like Aristotle and his followers.
B) The Jews and Christians.
C) The Muslim innovators.
D) The Arab idol-worshippers.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique distinguishes between the philosophers' competence in natural sciences and their profound ignorance in divine science, a theme central to his broader attack on Hellenistic philosophy.

Avicenna's Synthetic Philosophy and the Necessary Existent
Critique of Avicenna's synthesis and the concept of Necessary Existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's analysis of Avicenna's synthesis of philosophy and Islam, and contrast Aristotle's First Cause with Avicenna's concept of the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
Avicenna's Necessary Existent was an innovation Aristotle never knew.

Ibn Taymiyya turns his attention to Avicenna, who attempted to merge the philosophical tradition of Aristotle with Islamic teachings he learned from Mu'tazila and Rawafid. This lesson examines Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Avicenna's hybrid philosophy, focusing on the concept of the Necessary Existent (واجب الوجود) and how it differs from Aristotle's First Cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify Avicenna's sources
According to Ibn Taymiyya, from whom did Avicenna learn Islam? Find the groups mentioned: Mu'tazila and Rawafid (a pejorative term for Shia). Also note he took from his philosophical predecessors.
This shows Avicenna's synthesis draws from multiple, sometimes conflicting, sources.
2
Extract Avicenna's innovations
What new topics did Avicenna introduce into philosophy? Ibn Taymiyya lists: prophecies, secrets of verses, dreams, and especially the Necessary Existent. Why are these considered innovations?
These are topics that Aristotle did not discuss in his metaphysical works.
3
Compare Aristotle's First Cause with Avicenna's Necessary Existent
According to the passage, how does Aristotle's First Cause differ from Avicenna's Necessary Existent? Aristotle's cause is tied to celestial motion, while Avicenna's is a necessary being in itself.
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that Aristotle never used the term 'Necessary Existent'.
4
Evaluate the effect of Avicenna's reform
What was the result of Avicenna's partial reform? The philosophy became popular among students ignorant of Islam, leading them to accept corrupt principles in logic, natural sciences, and divine sciences.
Ibn Taymiyya sees Avicenna's work as making philosophical errors more attractive and thus more dangerous.

The Soul's Two Powers: Why Perfection Requires Both Knowledge and Worship
A critical argument refuting intellectualist perfection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's two-power argument against the philosophers' intellectualism and explain why he insists on the necessity of worship for the soul's perfection.

Core Idea
The soul's perfection demands not just knowledge but also worship, because it has both theoretical and practical powers.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya critiques the philosophers' claim that the soul can be perfected through mere theoretical knowledge. He argues that the soul has two distinct powers—theoretical and practical—and therefore requires both knowledge of God and worship of Him. This reframes human perfection as an integrated act of knowing, loving, and serving God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim under attack
Locate where Ibn Taymiyya states what the philosophers assert: that the soul is perfected by mere knowledge (al-‘ilm). This is the target of his critique.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counter-argument: the two powers
He introduces the soul's two powers: a theoretical/scientific power (quwwa ‘ilmiyya naẓariyya) and a volitional/practical power (quwwa irādiyya ‘amaliyya). Note that he does not deny the theoretical power but adds the practical.
This is a key move: he affirms the philosophers' focus on the theoretical but argues it's incomplete.
3
Trace the necessary conclusion for perfection
Because the soul has two powers, perfection (kamāl) must involve both. Ibn Taymiyya states that the soul 'inevitably requires perfection of both powers' through knowledge of God and worship.
The word 'la budda' (inevitably) indicates logical necessity.
4
Analyze his definition of worship
Examine how he defines worship: it comprises knowledge (ma‘rifa), love (maḥabba), and servitude/humility (‘ubūdiyya/dhull). This shows that worship is not mere ritual but includes inner states.
This definition integrates both theoretical and practical aspects.
5
Connect the argument to the purpose of revelation
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that this is the message of all prophets and scriptures: calling to worship God alone. This universalizes his argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود هنا التنبيه على أنه لو قدّر أن النفس تكمل بمجرد العلم كما زعموه؛ فإن النفس لها قوتان قوة علمية نظرية وقوة إرادية عملية. فلا بد لها من كمال القوتين بمعرفة الله، وعبادته. وعبادته تجمع محبته والذل له. فلا تكمل نفس قط إلا بعبادة الله وحده لا شريك له. والعبادة تجمع معرفته ومحبته والعبودية له. وبهذا بعث الله الرسل، وأنزل الكتب الإلهية؛ كلها تدعو إلى عبادة الله وحده لا شريك له.
And the purpose here is to draw attention to the fact that even if it were assumed that the soul is perfected by mere knowledge as they claim; for the soul has two powers: a theoretical, scientific power and a volitional, practical power. So it inevitably requires perfection of both powers by knowledge of God and worship of Him. And worship of Him comprises love for Him and humility before Him. So no soul is ever perfected except by worshipping God alone, without any partner. And worship comprises knowledge of Him, love for Him, and servitude to Him. And with this God sent the messengers and revealed the divine books; all of them call to worshipping God alone, without any partner.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the philosophers' claim that the soul can be perfected by mere theoretical knowledge. He begins by stating that even if that claim were assumed, it is false because the soul has two distinct powers: a theoretical/scientific power (quwwa ‘ilmiyya naẓariyya) and a volitional/practical power (quwwa irādiyya ‘amaliyya). Therefore, perfection (kamāl) of the soul requires both powers to be perfected, which is achieved through knowledge of God (ma‘rifa) and worship of Him (‘ibāda). Worship incorporates both love (maḥabba) and humility (dhull) before God. Hence, the soul is never perfected except by worshipping God alone, without any partner. Worship encompasses knowledge, love, and servitude. This is the purpose of all divine messages and scriptures: to call people to worship God alone. The argument is a direct refutation of the philosophers' intellectualism, insisting that practical devotion is integral to human perfection.

Key Terms
النفس (al-nafs): The soul, which has two powers (theoretical and practical) and requires both for perfection. [Note: Standard in Islamic philosophy; Ibn Taymiyya uses it to emphasize the practical aspect.]
القوة العلمية النظرية (al-quwwa al-‘ilmiyya al-naẓariyya): The theoretical/intellectual power of the soul, which seeks knowledge. [Note: Common in falsafa; Ibn Taymiyya accepts this but adds the practical power.]
القوة الإرادية العملية (al-quwwa al-irādiyya al-‘amaliyya): The volitional/practical power of the soul, which inclines to action and worship. [Note: Less emphasized in pure Aristotelian psychology; Ibn Taymiyya highlights it.]
العبادة (al-‘ibāda): Worship, which comprises knowledge, love, and servitude to God, and is essential for soul's perfection. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya defines it broadly to include both inner and outer acts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what are the two powers of the soul?
A) Irrational and rational
B) Theoretical and practical
C) Sensual and intellective
D) Active and passive

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that the soul's perfection requires both theoretical knowledge and practical worship, integrated in the act of worshipping God alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Ibn Sina's Harmonization
Author introduces the context: Ibn Sina's attempt to reconcile philosophy and Islam leading to corruption from Greek logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three corrupt Greek principles accepted by Muslim philosophers and explain their consequences for Islamic faith and methodology.

Core Idea
Accepting Greek logical principles corrupts Islamic epistemology.

This lesson examines Ibn Taymiyya's condemnation of Muslim philosophers who adopted Greek logic uncritically. He argues that even those who noticed contradictions still accepted corrupt foundations, leading to sophistry in rational matters and blind acceptance in scriptural matters, ultimately deviating from true faith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Who is Ibn Taymiyya criticizing? Ibn Sina and his followers who attempted to harmonize philosophy with Islam.
2
Extract the three corrupted sciences
According to the passage, which three fields had corrupt principles accepted from the Greeks? Logic (mantiq), physics (tabi'iyyat), and theology (ilahiyyat).
3
Trace the consequences
What resulted from accepting these corrupt principles? Deviation from the reality of knowledge and faith, sophistry in rational matters, and blind acceptance in scriptural matters.
4
Contrast with true Islamic methodology
Ibn Taymiyya implies that true Islamic knowledge is not obtained through Greek logic but through genuine science and faith grounded in revelation.
5
Evaluate the critique
Consider the internal consistency: Does Ibn Taymiyya's argument logically follow that acceptance of foreign principles leads to doctrinal corruption?
This step is analytical, not evaluative of the truth of the claim.

The third station - the fifth aspect: Ibn Sina's attempt to combine the speech of the philosophers and the speech of the Muslims. He knows the religion of Islam from the student observers. And they began to show some of the contradiction in it, so each spoke according to what he had, but they accepted corrupt principles from them in logic, physics, and theology, and they did not know what falsehood entered into them. That became a cause for their deviation in high matters of faith and lofty Quranic objectives, by which they departed from the reality of knowledge and faith, and they became in much of that not hearing nor understanding, rather they behave sophistically in rational matters and accept [blindly] in scriptural matters.

Commentary
This passage is a critical introduction to the problem caused by Muslim philosophers who followed Greek logic and metaphysics. Ibn Taymiyya identifies that despite noticing contradictions in Greek philosophy, these scholars accepted corrupt foundational principles in logic (mantiq), physics (tabi'iyyat), and metaphysics (ilahiyyat). This led them to deviate from true Islamic beliefs and methodology. They became sophistical in rational discourse while blindly following scriptural matters, losing both genuine science and faith.

Worship as an End, Not a Means
Author attacks instrumental view of worship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the instrumental view of worship and reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique against it.

Core Idea
Reducing worship to a means destroys its intrinsic obligation for all, including the elite.

Ibn Taymiyya attacks a philosophical view that turns acts of worship into preparatory tools for knowledge. This view claims that once knowledge is attained, worship is no longer required. In this passage, he exposes this as a dangerous antinomian position rooted in Isma'ili and other esoteric traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the instrumental claim
Locate where the passage states that worship is made a 'pure means' (وسائل محضة) to knowledge. Note that the purpose of worship is reduced to reforming the soul for knowledge or practical wisdom.
2
Trace the consequence
If worship is only a means, then when the end (knowledge) is attained, the means is no longer needed. Find the phrase 'dropped from whoever has attained the goal' (ساقطاً عمن حَصَّل المقصود).
3
Locate the accused groups
Ibn Taymiyya names the Isma'ili atheists (الملاحدة الإسماعيلية) and others among Sufis, theologians, and Shi'a who adopted this view.
4
Assess the refutation
Ibn Taymiyya's critique is implicit: by showing that this view is held by sects known for antinomianism, he undermines its legitimacy. The refutation is also theological: worship is commanded for its own sake, not as a dispensable tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهؤلاء يجعلون العبادات التي أُمرت بها الرسل مقصودها إصلاح أخلاق النفس لتستعد للعلم الذي زعموه أنه كمال النفس. أو مقصودها إصلاح المنزل والمدينة، وهو الحكمة العملية. فيجعلون العبادات وسائل محضةً إلى ما يدّعونه من العلم. ولهذا يرون ذلك ساقطاً عمن حَصَّل المقصود، كما تفعل الملاحدة الإسماعيلية، ومن دخل في الإلحاد أو بعضه، وانتسب إلى الصوفية، أو المتكلمين، أو الشيعة، أو غيرهم.
And these people make the acts of worship that the messengers were commanded: their purpose is to reform the soul's character to prepare for the knowledge which they claim is the soul's perfection; or their purpose is the reform of the household and the city, which is practical wisdom. So they make acts of worship pure means to what they claim of knowledge. And therefore they consider it dropped from whoever has attained the goal, just as the Isma'ili atheists do, and those who entered into atheism or part of it, and associated themselves with the Sufis, or the theologians, or the Shi'a, or others.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage criticizes philosophers and those influenced by them who reinterpret worship ('ibadat) as merely preparatory for theoretical knowledge or as social reform. By reducing worship to a means, they claim that those who have already attained the ultimate knowledge ('ilm) are no longer obligated to perform worship. Ibn Taymiyya traces this view to the Isma'ili sect and other groups that lean toward antinomianism, whether among Sufis, mutakallimun, or Shi'a.

Key Terms

ملاحدة إسماعيلية

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of the Jahmiyya Definition of Faith
Author critiques the Jahmiyya view that faith is merely knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in the Jahmiyya definition of faith and explain why Ibn Taymiyya considers it incomplete.

Core Idea
Faith is not mere knowledge; it demands love and action.

This passage shows how Ibn Taymiyya critiques a rival theological definition. He contrasts the Jahmiyya view with that of the philosophers, exposing a reductionist error. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to evaluate theological claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Jahmiyya claim
Pinpoint their assertion: 'faith is mere knowledge of God.' This is their core definition.
2
Note Ibn Taymiyya's concession
He acknowledges this view is 'better' than the philosophers' but still flawed. This sets up his critique.
3
Extract the implied criticism
The definition omits necessary correlates: knowledge of angels, books, messengers, and by extension love and action. Faith becomes purely cognitive.
4
Understand the severity of his judgment
He declares the Jahmiyya among the greatest innovators, some deeming them outside the 72 sects. This shows the gravity of a defective definition.
This is a critique of a definition by its consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Falsehood of the statement that faith is mere knowledge of God. And the Jahmiyya said: faith is mere knowledge of God. And this statement, although better than the statement of those [philosophers], is that it made knowledge of God with what necessarily follows from that of knowledge of His angels, His books, and His messengers. And these [philosophers] made perfection the knowledge of absolute existence and its concomitants. And this matter, even if it had reality externally, would not be perfection for the soul, except knowledge of its Creator, exalted is He. So these Jahmiyya are among the greatest innovators of the Muslims, rather more than one made them outside the seventy-two sects, as is narrated from Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak and Yusuf ibn ...
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage refutes the Jahmiyya sect who defined faith (iman) as mere knowledge (ma'rifa) of God. Ibn Taymiyya concedes that this view is better than the philosophers' who equate perfection with knowledge of 'absolute existence,' but still flawed. The Jahmiyya omission of love, humility, and action reduces faith to mere cognition. He further asserts that the Jahmiyya are extreme innovators, with some scholars deeming them outside the 72 sects of deviation.

Refuting the Jahmiyya: Faith Is Not Mere Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the Jahmiyya equation of faith with mere knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Jahmiyya definition of faith and reconstruct his reductio ad absurdum argument.

Core Idea
Faith requires more than knowledge; even the devils know God.

Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at the Jahmiyya who define faith (iman) as mere knowledge (ma'rifa) of God. While their view is superior to the falasifa's notion of absolute being, it still falls short. In this passage, he shows the absurd consequences of such a definition and reports the stern reaction of early imams. You will learn to identify the logical flaw in equating faith with knowledge alone and to apply this critique to similar errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Find the sentence in the Arabic where the Jahmiyya define faith as mere knowledge of God. Note the term 'ma'rifa'.
The Jahmiyya say: 'الإيمان مجرد معرفة الله'
2
Locate the comparative contrast
Ibn Taymiyya first compares the Jahmiyya view favorably to the falasifa. Why? Because it at least involves knowledge of God and His attributes, unlike the logicians' focus on absolute existence.
The phrase 'وهذا القول، وإن كان خيراً من قول هؤلاء' shows the comparison.
3
Find the criticism and the reductio
He attacks the Jahmiyya view as innovation and reports takfir. The key logical move: if faith is mere knowledge, then Iblis, Pharaoh, and the Jews who knew God would be believers. This is absurd, so the definition is false.
Look for 'هذا يلزم منه أن يكون إبليس، وفرعون، واليهود... مؤمنين'
4
Understand the takfir reports
He cites early authorities (e.g., Waki' ibn al-Jarrah, Ahmad ibn Hanbal) who declared takfir on those holding this view. This shows the severity of the error and the consensus of early imams.
The authority of these imams strengthens the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث - الوجه الخامس : تزييف القول بأن الإيمان مجرد معرفة الله قول الجهمية والجهمية قالوا: الإيمان مجرد معرفة الله وهذا القول، وإن كان خيراً من قول هؤلاء قولهم؛ فإنه جعل معرفة الله بما يلزم ذلك من معرفة ملائكته، وكتبه، ورسله. وهؤلاء جعلوا الكمال معرفة الوجود المطلق ولواحقه. وهذا أمر لو كان له حقيقة في الخارج لم يكن كمالاً للنفس، إلا معرفة خالقها سبحانه وتعالى. فهؤلاء الجهمية من أعظم مبتدعة المسلمين، بل جعلهم غير واحدٍ خارجين عن الثنتين وسبعين فرقة، كما يُروى ذلك عن عبد الله بن المبارك، ويوسف بن أسباط، وهو قول طائفة من المتأخرين من أصحاب أحمد، وغيرهم. وقد كفر غير واحد من الأئمة كوكيع بن الجراح، وأحمد بن حنبل، وغيرهما، لمن يقول هذا القول. وقالوا هذا يلزم منه أن يكون إبليس، وفرعون، واليهود الذين يعرفونه كما يعرفون أبناءهم، مؤمنين. فقول الجهمية خير من قول هؤلاء.
The third station - the fifth aspect: Falsifying the claim that faith is mere knowledge of God. The Jahmiyya said: Faith is mere knowledge of God. This statement, although it is better than the statement of these [logicians] - for it makes knowledge of God necessary, along with knowledge of His angels, His books, and His messengers - whereas these [logicians] made perfection the knowledge of absolute existence and its concomitants. And this is something that, even if it had a reality in the external world, would not be a perfection for the soul except knowledge of its Creator, may He be glorified and exalted. So these Jahmiyya are among the greatest innovators of the Muslims; indeed, more than one made them outside the seventy-two sects, as is reported from 'Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak and Yusuf ibn Asbat. And it is the saying of a group of later Hanbalis and others. Indeed, more than one of the imams, such as Waki' ibn al-Jarrah and Ahmad ibn Hanbal and others, declared takfir upon whoever says this saying. And they said: This entails that Iblis, Pharaoh, and the Jews who know Him as they know their own sons would be believers. So the statement of the Jahmiyya is better than the statement of these [logicians].
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the Jahmiyya's definition of faith (iman) as mere knowledge (ma'rifa) of God. Ibn Taymiyya begins by acknowledging that the Jahmiyya's view is better than that of the logicians (falasifa), because it at least includes knowledge of God and His attributes, whereas the logicians reduce perfection to knowledge of 'absolute existence' (al-wujud al-mutlaq) and its accidents. However, he then strongly criticizes the Jahmiyya. He describes them as among the greatest innovators (mubtadi'a) in Islam, cited by earlier authorities as outside the seventy-two sects. He reports that leading imams like Waki' ibn al-Jarrah and Ahmad ibn Hanbal declared takfir (excommunication) against those who hold this view. The reason is that this definition leads to an absurd consequence: it would make Iblis, Pharaoh, and the Jews (who knew God but rejected Him) believers, since they possessed knowledge. Thus, faith cannot be merely intellectual knowledge; it must include tasdiq (assent of the heart), love, fear, and action. Ibn Taymiyya uses this reductio ad absurdum to demolish the Jahmiyya position.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الجهمية
تكفير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does Ibn Taymiyya claim follows from the Jahmiyya definition of faith as mere knowledge?
A) People would not be accountable for their actions.
B) Iblis, Pharaoh, and the Jews would be considered believers.
C) The prophets would be unnecessary.
D) All humans would be equally faithful.

Key Takeaway
Faith is not mere knowledge; it requires heartfelt assent and action, as shown by the absurd consequence of the Jahmiyya definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Reduction of Iman to Mere Knowledge: Ibn Taymiyya's Reductio ad Absurdum
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that iman is mere knowledge by showing its absurd consequences and citing early imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the reductio ad absurdum argument Ibn Taymiyya uses to refute the claim that faith is mere knowledge and explain its implications for defining Islamic concepts.

Core Idea
If faith were mere knowledge, Iblis would be a believer—an absurdity that refutes the definition.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians attacks the claim that iman (faith) is simply ma'rifa (knowledge or cognition) of Allah. He argues that this definition collapses, because it would include beings like Iblis and Pharaoh who certainly had knowledge of God but were not believers. By drawing this absurd consequence, he defends the classical understanding that faith includes inner states like fear, love, and reverence, as well as outward actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
Find the precise statement that iman is merely ma'rifa of Allah. The author attributes this to a late group among the followers of Ahmad ibn Hanbal.
2
Extract the reductio argument
Ibn Taymiyya says: if faith is mere knowledge, then Iblis, Pharaoh, and the Jews who recognized the Prophet would be believers. Since they are clearly not believers, the definition is false.
This is a classical reductio ad absurdum.
3
Analyze the comparison with the Jahmiyya
The author says the Jahmiyya's view is better because they at least focus on knowledge as a core, while the subject group strips faith of its essential accompaniments like fear, love, and reverence.
4
Understand the author's caveat
Ibn Taymiyya clarifies that exposing falsehood does not automatically condemn the person; damnation depends on rejecting prophets, not merely erroneous reasoning.
This refines the critique to focus on the argument, not ad hominem.

المقام الثالث - الوجه الخامس : تزييف القول بأن الإيمان مجرد معرفة الله. وهو قول طائفة من المتأخرين من أصحاب أحمد، وغيرهم. وقد كفر غير واحد من الأئمة كـوكيع بن الجراح، وأحمد بن حنبل، وغيرهما، لمن يقول هذا القول. وقالوا هذا يلزم منه أن يكون إبليس، وفرعون، واليهود الذين يعرفونه كما يعرفون أبناءهم، مؤمنين. فقول الجهمية خير من قول هؤلاء. فإن ما ذكروه هو أصلُ ما تكمل به النفوس. قولهم أفسد من قول الجهمية في نفس العلم، وإن لم يصدقه قول ولا عمل ولا اقترن به من الخشية، والمحبة، والتعظيم، وغير ذلك مما هو من أصول الإيمان ولوازمه. وأما هؤلاء فبعدوا عن الكمال غاية البعد. والمقصود هنا الكلام على برهانهم فقط، وإنما ذكرنا بعض ما لزمهم بسبب أصولهم الفاسدة. واعلم أن بيان ما في كلامهم من الباطل والنقض لا يستلزم كونهم أشقياء في الآخرة، إلا إذا بعث الله إليهم رسولاً فلم يتبعوه. بل يُعرف به أن مَنْ جاءته الرسل بالحق، فعدل عن طريقهم إلى طريق هؤلاء، كان من الأشقياء في الآخرة. والقوم - لولا الأنبياء - لكانوا أعقل من غيرهم. لكن الأنبياء جاؤا بالحق، وبقاياه في الأمم وإن كفروا ببعضه حتى مشركي العرب كان عندهم بقايا من دين إبراهيم فكانوا بها خيراً من الفلاسفة المشركين الذين يوافقون أرسطو وأمثاله على أصولهم.
The third station - the fifth aspect: Refuting the claim that faith is merely knowledge of Allah. It is the statement of a group of late followers of Ahmad and others. More than one of the imams, such as Waki' ibn al-Jarrah, Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and others, declared the one who says this statement to be a disbeliever. They said: This necessarily implies that Iblis, Pharaoh, and the Jews who know Him as they know their own sons would be believers. So the statement of the Jahmiyya is better than the statement of these people. For what they mentioned is the origin of what souls are perfected by. Their statement is more corrupt than the statement of the Jahmiyya regarding knowledge itself, even if no statement or action confirms it and it is not accompanied by fear, love, reverence, and other things that are from the foundations of faith and its necessities. As for these people, they are far from perfection, extremely far. The purpose here is to speak about their demonstration only; we only mentioned some of what befalls them due to their corrupt principles. Know that exposing the falsehood and contradiction in their speech does not necessitate that they are wretched in the hereafter, unless Allah sends a messenger to them and they do not follow him. Rather, by it, one knows that whoever the messengers come to with truth, then he deviates from their path to the path of these people, he will be among the wretched in the hereafter. The people—were it not for the prophets—would have been more intelligent than others. But the prophets came with truth, and its remains in the nations, even if they disbelieved in some of it, until the polytheists of the Arabs had remains of the religion of Abraham, and by that they were better than the polytheist philosophers who agree with Aristotle and his likes on their principles.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This critical passage refutes the theological claim that faith (iman) is merely knowledge (ma'rifa) of Allah. Ibn Taymiyya begins by identifying this as the view of a late group among the followers of Ahmad ibn Hanbal and others, then reports the severe judgment of early imams like Waki' and Ahmad himself, who declared such claimants disbelievers. The core argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if faith were only intellectual knowledge, then Iblis, Pharaoh, and the Jews who recognized the Prophet would be believers. This is clearly false and against Islamic consensus. The author then compares this view unfavorably to the Jahmiyya's position, which at least emphasized knowledge as a core component, whereas the subject group strips faith of all essential actions and states (fear, love, reverence). The passage then explains the real intent: to critique their demonstration (burhan), not necessarily to condemn their persons. It concludes with a warning about deviating from the prophetic path toward the philosophers, noting that even the pagan Arabs had remnants of Abrahamic religion that made them better than Aristotle's followers.

Key Terms
الإيمان (al-iman): In this passage, the opponents define it as mere knowledge (ma'rifa), but the author defines it inclusively with actions and states (fear, love, reverence). [Note: Ibn Taymiyya's definition is broader than the logicians' epistemic focus; he refutes the reduction to mere cognition.]
المعرفة (al-ma'rifa): Here it means intellectual cognition or awareness, as opposed to the comprehensive faith that includes volitional and practical aspects. [Note: In earlier falsafa contexts, ma'rifa could mean gnosis; here it is critiqued as an insufficient basis for iman.]
الجهمية (al-jahmiyya): A theological sect that emphasized knowledge; Ibn Taymiyya considers their view better than the subject group's view. [Note: No significant shift in this passage; it is a standard reference.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is an absurd consequence of the claim that faith is mere knowledge?
A) That prophets would be disbelievers.
B) That Iblis, Pharaoh, and Jews would be believers.
C) That the Jahmiyya would be correct.
D) That the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
A definition that fails to exclude clear counterexamples (like Iblis) is invalid; faith must include inner states and actions beyond mere cognition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar reductio against the claim that 'prayer (salat) is merely physical movements (arkaan)'. Show an absurd consequence that follows from that definition, using an Islamic example.
?
Identify the key difference between Ibn Taymiyya's definition of iman and the logicians' definition in this passage. How does his definition avoid the absurd consequence?

The Agent's Effect Cannot Be Eternal: Critique of Ibn Sina
Ibn Taymiyya refutes Ibn Sina on the eternity of the effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Ibn Taymiyya argues that an agent's specific effect cannot be eternal, and identify his critique of Ibn Sina's view that a possible effect can be eternal with its necessary cause.

Core Idea
An agent's specific act always occurs in time; only the type can be eternal.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique of causality by clarifying the nature of the agent (al-fa'il). He argues that a specific effect cannot coexist eternally with its agent, even if the agent is capable of producing effects successively. He then targets Ibn Sina's attempt to reconcile eternal creation with the concept of a necessary cause, accusing him of combining incompatible positions from the theologians and the Aristotelian tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim about the agent
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that 'it is impossible for the agent's specific effect to coexist with it'. This is the foundational claim: the agent's specific effect must be temporally originated, even if the agent acts perpetually.
2
Understand the analogy with movement
The author compares the eternity of the type of act to the eternity of the type of movement. Just as movement as a type can be eternal while each individual motion is temporally originated, so too the agent's act-type can be perpetual while each specific act is new.
This shows that the agent's perpetual action does not require any specific act to be eternal.
3
Compare Aristotle's and Ibn Sina's views on the First Cause
Ibn Taymiyya notes that Aristotle and his followers posited only a final cause (illa gha'iyya) for the world, not an efficient cause (mubdi'). Therefore, they did not face the problem of an eternal effect. Ibn Sina, however, claimed that the First is an efficient cause whose effect is eternal.
Aristotle's First Cause is like a beloved that moves the lover, not a creator.
4
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Ibn Sina's innovation
The author accuses Ibn Sina of combining the theologians' concept of creation (efficient cause) with Aristotle's eternal world, resulting in the claim that a possible effect (al-mumkin al-ma'lul) can be eternal with its cause. This contradicts earlier philosophers and the majority of rational people, as even Ibn Rushd refuted it.
The critique is that Ibn Sina's position is internally inconsistent and unprecedented.
5
Identify the followers of Ibn Sina listed
Ibn Taymiyya names al-Suhrawardi, al-Razi, al-Amidi, and al-Tusi as those who accepted Ibn Sina's view. This shows the influence of the doctrine despite its deviation from the consensus.
Recognizing these figures helps track the transmission of this idea in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما «الفاعل» فيمتنع أن يقارنه مفعوله المعيَّن، وإن لم يمتنع أن يكون فاعلاً لشيء بعد شيء. فِقدَم نوع الفعل كقدم نوع الحركة وذلك لا ينافي حدوث كل جزء من أجزائها، بل يستلزمه لامتناع قدم شيء منها بعينه . وهذا مما عليه جماهير العقلاء من جميع الأمم ، حتى أرسطو وأتباعه. فإنهم، الأول ليس مبدعاً للعالم وإن قالوا بقدم العالَم، فهم لم يثبتوا له «مُبدِعاً» ولا «علة فاعلة»، بل «علة غائية يتحرك الفلك للتشبه بها، لأن حركة الفلك إرادية . وهذا القول ـ وهو إن الأوّل ليس مبدعاً للعالم، وإنما هو علة غائية للتشبه به ـ قول ابن سينا وإن كان في غاية الجهل والكفر، فالمقصود أنهم وافقوا سائر العقلاء في أن الممكن الممكن المعلول لا يكون قديماً بقدم علّته، كما يقول ذلك ابن سينا وموافقوه . ولهذا أنكر هذا القولَ ابنُ رُشد وأمثاله من الفلاسفة الذين اتبعوا طريقة أرسطو وسائر العقلاء في ذلك، وبينوا أن ما ذكره ابن سينا مما خالف به سلفه وجماهير العقلاء. وكان قصده أن يركب مذهباً من مذهب المتكلمين ومذهب سلفه، فيجعل الموجود الممكن معلول الواجب) - مفعولاً له ـ مع كونه أزلياً قديماً بقدمه. واتبعه على إمكان ذلك أتباعه في ذلك كالسُّهْرَوَرْدِي الحلبي، والرَّازي، والآمدي، والطُّوسي، وغيرهم.
As for the agent (al-fa'il), it is impossible for its specific effect to coexist with it, even though it is not impossible for it to be an agent for something after something else. So the eternity of the type of act is like the eternity of the type of movement, and that does not conflict with the temporal origination of every individual part of it; rather, it necessitates it because of the impossibility of the eternity of any individual part. This is what the majority of rational people from all nations affirm, even Aristotle and his followers. For they hold that the First is not the creator of the world, even though they affirm the eternity of the world; they did not establish for Him a 'creator' or 'efficient cause,' but rather a 'final cause' that the sphere moves to imitate, because the movement of the sphere is voluntary. And this statement—that the First is not the creator of the world, but only a final cause to be imitated—is the statement of Ibn Sina, even though it is extreme ignorance and unbelief. The point is that they agreed with other rational people that the possible effect cannot be eternal with the eternity of its cause, as Ibn Sina and his followers claim. For this reason, Ibn Rushd and similar philosophers who followed the method of Aristotle and the rest of rational people denied this statement, and they explained that what Ibn Sina mentioned contradicts his predecessors and the majority of rational people. His intention was to combine the method of the theologians and the method of his predecessors, making the possible existent an effect of the Necessary—an effect of Him—while being eternal and ancient with His eternity. He was followed in that possibility by his followers such as al-Suhrawardi al-Halabi, al-Razi, al-Amidi, al-Tusi, and others.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage continues the critique of causality. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that an agent (fa'il) cannot have its specific effect coexistent with it; only the type of act can be perpetual, like the type of movement, but each individual act is temporally originated. He then notes that even Aristotle and his followers, despite affirming the eternity of the world, did not posit an efficient cause (mubdi') but only a final cause (illa gha'iyya). Ibn Sina, however, tried to combine the theological notion of creation with the Aristotelian eternal world, claiming that a possible effect can be eternal with its necessary cause. Ibn Taymiyya refutes this, citing Ibn Rushd's criticism, and lists those who followed Ibn Sina.

Key Terms
الفاعل (al-fa'il)
العلة الغائية (al-illa al-gha'iyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what was Ibn Sina's innovation regarding the possible effect?
A) He denied the existence of possible effects.
B) He claimed that a possible effect can be eternal with its necessary cause.
C) He argued that possible effects are created ex nihilo.
D) He rejected the concept of causality entirely.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that an agent's specific effect is always temporally originated, and Ibn Sina's claim that a possible effect can be eternal with its cause contradicts the consensus of rational people and earlier philosophers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an argument using Ibn Taymiyya's principle: if an agent's specific effect cannot be eternal, then consider the claim that God's creation of the world is an eternal act. Explain why this claim must be rejected on Ibn Taymiyya's grounds.
?
Identify which of the following philosophers would agree with Ibn Taymiyya's critique: Aristotle, Ibn Sina, Ibn Rushd, al-Razi. Justify your answer using the passage.
